Propositions

Definition of Proposition and Its Two Types
Author defines qaḍiyya and divides it into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given utterance is a proposition and classify it as categorical or conditional.

Core Idea
A proposition is defined by its capacity for truth, and all propositions are either categorical or conditional.

We now begin a new chapter on propositions, the building blocks of logical argument. Before studying how propositions combine, we must know what a proposition is and what its basic forms are. The author gives a crisp definition and then a fundamental twofold division.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of qaḍiyya
The author defines a proposition as 'what admits truth by itself'. This means the utterance's truth or falsity does not depend on context; it is inherent. Note that not all sentences are propositions — only those that are declarative and truth-apt.
The term khabar is used synonymously here, though earlier it contrasted with ṭalab (request).
2
Recognize the two types: categorical and conditional
Categorical (ḥamliyya) propositions affirm or deny a predicate of a subject, e.g., 'Zayd is fasting'. Conditional (shartiyya) propositions connect two propositions with a conditional or disjunctive relationship, e.g., 'If the sun rises, then it is day'.
Conditional propositions can be muttasila (connected) or munfaṣila (disjunctive), which will be studied later.
3
Apply the definition to distinguish propositions from non-propositions
Test sentences: imperatives, interrogatives, exclamations are not propositions because they cannot be true or false. Only declarative statements that can be evaluated as true or false are propositions.
Example: 'O Allah, forgive me!' is a supplication, not a proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابٌ فِي القَضَايا (وَأَحْكامِهَا) (٥٠) مَا احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ جَرَى بَيْنَهُمُ: «قَضِيَّةٌ) وَ(خَبَرَا» (٥١) ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ : شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ وَالثَّانِي
A chapter on propositions and their rulings. (50) What admits truth by itself, they call 'proposition' and 'report'. (51) Then propositions according to them are two kinds: conditional and categorical, and the second [is categorical].
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on propositions (qaḍāyā). First, it defines a proposition (qaḍiyya) as an utterance that admits truth or falsehood by itself, i.e., regardless of context. It is also called khabar (report). Then it divides propositions into two main types: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (ḥamliyya). A categorical proposition asserts a predicate of a subject, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. A conditional proposition connects two propositions with a conditional, e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then it is daytime'. This division is fundamental because different logical operations apply to each type.

Key Terms
قضية
خبر
حمليَّة
شرطيَّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a proposition (qaḍiyya)?
A) 'O Allah, forgive me!'
B) 'Zayd is standing'
C) 'Stand up!'
D) 'What is that?'

Key Takeaway
A proposition is an utterance that by itself admits truth or falsehood, and all propositions are either categorical or conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify whether each of the following is a proposition (qaḍiyya): a) 'O Allah, forgive me!' — b) 'Zayd is fasting' — c) 'Do not lie!' — d) 'Every intoxicant is prohibited'.
?
2. Classify the following as categorical or conditional: 'If the sun is eclipsed, then perform the eclipse prayer.'

Subdivisions of Categorical Propositions
Author subdivides categorical propositions by quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify categorical propositions as universal, particular, singular, or indefinite based on their quantifier.

Core Idea
A categorical proposition's quantity depends on its quantifier, or lack thereof.

In this passage, the author continues breaking down propositions. After dividing them into categorical and conditional, he now classifies categorical propositions by quantity. You will learn to recognize whether a proposition is universal, particular, singular, or indefinite by looking for quantifiers like 'every' or 'some'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main classification
Notice the text says propositions are either conditional (shartiyya) or categorical (hamliyya). Focus on the categorical type.
2
Distinguish quantified vs indefinite
The categorical is either musawwar (quantified) or muhmal (indefinite). A quantified proposition has an explicit quantifier (sur); an indefinite one has none.
Example: 'Every human is mortal' (quantified) vs 'Human is mortal' (indefinite).
3
Identify the types of quantifiers
The quantifier can be universal (kullī) like 'every' (kull) or particular (juz'ī) like 'some' (ba'd). The passage also mentions 'nothing' (indefinite) and 'not some' (i.e., 'none').
These map to the four types: A, I, E, O in the square of opposition.
4
Learn the special case: singular propositions
Separately, a singular proposition (shakhsiyya) about an individual, like 'Zayd is standing', is treated as universal in quantity because it covers the entire subject.
This is a nuance: singular propositions are often considered universal in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ : شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ وَالثَّانِي: (٥٢) كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ، وَالأَوَّلُ : إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ، وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ (٥٣) وَالسُّورُ : كُلِّيّا، وَجُزْئِيَّا يُرَى وَأَرْبَعُ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرَى : (٥٤) إِمَّا بِكُلِّ، أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ، أَوْ بِلَا شَيْءٍ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ أَوْ شِبْهِ جَلَا
Then propositions according to them are two kinds: conditional and categorical, and the second: (52) universal, particular, and the first [i.e., categorical] is either quantified or indefinite. (53) And the quantifier is seen as universal or particular, and its four kinds wherever it occurs: (54) either 'every', or 'some', or nothing, and not 'some' or its like, it is clear.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage continues the division of propositions. First, it repeats the division into categorical and conditional. Then it subdivides categorical propositions into three types based on quantity: kulliyya (universal), shakhṣiyya (singular), and the first (which refers to the quantified ones). Actually the text says 'wa al-awwal: immā musawwar wa immā muhmal' — the first (i.e., the categorical proposition) is either quantified (musawwar) or indefinite (muhmal). Quantified propositions have a quantifier (sūr); the quantifier is either universal (kullī) like 'every' or particular (juz’ī) like 'some'. There are four kinds of quantifiers: (1) 'every' (kull), (2) 'some' (ba‘ḍ), (3) nothing (indefinite, e.g., 'human is animal' without quantifier), and (4) 'not some' or its like (i.e., 'none'). This classification is essential for understanding the square of opposition and conversion.

Key Terms

شخصية
مسور
مهمل
سور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'Every human is mortal'
B) 'Some scholars are generous'
C) 'Human is animal'
D) 'Zayd is standing'

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are classified by quantity: universal (kull), particular (ba'd), singular (shakhs), or indefinite (no quantifier).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Take a verse from the Quran like 'Indeed, Allah is over all things competent' (2:106). Is it universal, particular, singular, or indefinite? Explain using the quantifier present.

Eight Types of Categorical Propositions
Author states that combining quantity and quality yields eight types of categorical propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight types of categorical propositions by combining quantity and quality, and identify each type from an example.

Core Idea
Two qualities × four quantities = eight proposition types.

This short line from the matn reminds us that categorical propositions are classified by both quantity (universal, particular, singular, indefinite) and quality (affirmative, negative). The combination yields eight distinct types. This classification is a foundation for the square of opposition and syllogistic reasoning. Let's break down how we arrive at eight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four quantity types
Recall the four quantities: universal (كل/جميع), particular (بعض), singular (زيد), and indefinite (إنسان). Each quantity can be either affirmative or negative.
The indefinite (مهملة) is often treated as equivalent to particular in classical logic.
2
Identify the two quality types
Quality is either affirmative (موجبة) – asserting the predicate of the subject – or negative (سالبة) – denying the predicate.
3
Cross quantity with quality
For each of the four quantities, there are two qualities. So 4 × 2 = 8 combinations. For example, universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O), singular affirmative, singular negative, indefinite affirmative, indefinite negative.
The traditional square of opposition focuses on A, E, I, O, but the eight include singular and indefinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّهَا : مُوجَبَةٌ، وَسَالِبَهُ فَهيَ إِذا إِلَى الثَّمَانِ آيبَهُ
And all of them are positive and negative; so they, then, return to eight [types].
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This very short line indicates that when we take the four quantity types (universal, particular, singular, indefinite) and cross them with the two qualities (positive and negative), we theoretically get eight types. However, in practice, the traditional square of opposition focuses on four: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O). The eight may include singular and indefinite. The author is reminding the reader of the combinatorial result.

Key Terms
موجبة
سالبة
ثمانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many types of categorical proposition result from the combination of quantity and quality?
A) 4
B) 6
C) 8
D) 2

Key Takeaway
There are eight categorical proposition types: four quantities each paired with affirmative and negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a table with rows for the four quantities and columns for affirmative and negative. Fill in all eight types and give an example of each from Islamic theology or jurisprudence.
?
Identify the quantity and quality of the proposition: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Then state which of the eight types it belongs to.

Identifying Parts of Propositions: Subject, Predicate, Antecedent, Consequent
Author identifies subject and predicate in categorical propositions and introduces conditional propositions with their parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject and predicate in any categorical proposition and the antecedent and consequent in any conditional proposition.

Core Idea
Every proposition has two parts: the thing judged and what is judged of it.

In logic, every proposition has a structure. For categorical propositions (ḥamliyya), the two parts are the subject (mawḍū‘) and predicate (maḥmūl). For conditional propositions (shartiyya), the parts are the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). This lesson will teach you to recognise these parts in any Islamic text, from fiqh rulings to kalām arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the statement makes a direct attribution (categorical) or links two statements with a condition (conditional). Categorical: e.g. 'The world is temporally originated.' Conditional: e.g. 'If the sun has set, then the Maghrib prayer is obligatory.'
2
For categorical: locate subject and predicate
The subject is what the statement is about; the predicate is what is attributed to it. In 'The world is temporally originated', subject = 'the world', predicate = 'temporally originated'. Both are essential and equally important.
The predicate is not always preceded by 'is'; in Arabic it may be implied.
3
For conditional: locate antecedent and consequent
The antecedent (muqaddam) is the part after 'if' (or equivalent), and the consequent (tālī) is the result. In 'If the sun has set, then the Maghrib prayer is obligatory', antecedent = 'the sun has set', consequent = 'the Maghrib prayer is obligatory'.
In a disconnected conditional (either-or), both alternatives are antecedents in a sense, but the terms muqaddam and tālī are still used.
4
Distinguish connected from disconnected conditionals
A connected conditional (muttaṣila) expresses implication: if A then B. A disconnected conditional (munfaṣila) expresses a disjunction: either A or B. In both, the parts are called muqaddam and tālī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ : «المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة (٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ (٥٨) أَيْضاً : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَهُ، وَمِثْلِهَا شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَهُ (٥٩) جُزَاهُمَا: مُقَدَّمُ، وَتَالِي
And the first [part of a categorical proposition] is the subject in the categorical, and the last is the predicate equally. (57) And if judgment is made on the basis of a condition in it, then it is conditional, and it also divides (58) into a connected conditional and its like a disconnected conditional. (59) Their parts: antecedent and consequent.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage explains the parts of propositions. In a categorical proposition (ḥamliyya), the first part is the subject (mawḍū‘) and the second part is the predicate (maḥmūl). Both are equally essential. Then it moves to conditional propositions (shartiyya), where the judgment is based on a condition (ta‘līq). A conditional proposition is one where two propositions are connected by a condition. It is divided into two types: muttaṣila (connected) where the condition implies a result, e.g., 'If the sun is up, then it is day'; and munfaṣila (disconnected) where there is an either-or relationship. Both types have two parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tālī). In a connected conditional, the antecedent is the 'if' part, the consequent is the 'then' part. In a disconnected conditional, they are the two alternatives.

Key Terms
موضوع
محمول
شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة
مقدم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) 'light'

Key Takeaway
Every proposition has two parts: the subject and predicate (categorical) or antecedent and consequent (conditional); identifying them is the first step to logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Three Types of Disjunctive Conditional
Author defines three types of disjunctive conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditional (māni‘ al-jam‘, māni‘ al-khuluw, ḥaqīqī akhaṣṣ) and identify which type an example belongs to.

Core Idea
The three types of disjunction are defined by truth possibilities: neither both true nor both false?

In this lesson, we explore the three types of disjunctive conditional (sharṭiyya munfaṣila) as outlined by the author. Understanding these types is crucial for correctly interpreting statements that offer alternatives, especially in Islamic legal reasoning (fiqh) and theology (kalām). You will learn to identify whether a disjunction excludes both being true, both being false, or both.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the disjunction
Look at the two propositions separated by 'or'. For example, 'Either this is a plant or it is an animal'. The two parts are clearly distinct.
In Arabic, they are called juz'an.
2
Check if both parts can be true together
If both cannot be true simultaneously, the disjunction is māni‘ al-jam‘. If both can be true, it is not māni‘ al-jam‘.
Māni‘ al-jam‘ = preventing conjunction.
3
Check if both parts can be false together
If both cannot be false simultaneously (i.e., at least one must be true), the disjunction is māni‘ al-khuluw. If both can be false, it is not māni‘ al-khuluw.
Māni‘ al-khuluw = preventing emptiness.
4
Classify the disjunction
Now combine the results: if it prevents both conjunction and emptiness (only one true), it is ḥaqīqī akhaṣṣ (real exclusive). If only prevents conjunction, it is māni‘ al-jam‘. If only prevents emptiness, it is māni‘ al-khuluw.
The real exclusive is the most specific.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَمَّا بَيَانُ ذَاتِ الأَنْصَالِ : (٦٠) «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَلَازُمَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَذَاتُ الانْفِصَالِ دُونَ مَيْنِ : (٦١) «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا أَقْسَامُهَا ثَلَاثَةٌ فَلْتُعْلَمَا (٦٢) مَانِعُ جَمْعٍ، أَوْ خُلُوِّ ، أَوْ هُمَا وَهْوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ فَاعْلَمَا
As for the explanation of the disjunctive: (60) 'What necessitates the coexistence of the two parts' and the disjunctive without doubt: (61) 'What necessitates incompatibility between them', its divisions are three, so let them be known: (62) preventing conjunction, or preventing emptiness, or both, and it is the real, the most specific, so know.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage clarifies the disjunctive conditional (sharṭiyya munfaṣila). It first contrasts it with the connected conditional: the connected necessitates the coexistence of the two parts (i.e., if antecedent true, consequent true). The disjunctive necessitates incompatibility (tanāfur) between the two parts. The disjunctive has three types: (1) Māni‘ al-jam‘ (preventing conjunction): only one can be true, but both can be false, e.g., 'Either this is a plant or it is an animal'. (2) Māni‘ al-khuluw (preventing emptiness): at least one must be true, but both can be true, e.g., 'Either Zayd is at home or he is at the mosque' (could be both? Actually this is inclusive or). (3) Māni‘ al-jam‘ wa al-khuluw (preventing both conjunction and emptiness): exactly one is true, the real exclusive disjunction, e.g., 'This number is either even or odd'. This is the most specific (akhaṣṣ).

Key Terms
مانع جمع
مانع خلوا
حقيقي أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of disjunction is 'Either this number is even or odd'?
A) Māni‘ al-jam‘
B) Māni‘ al-khuluw
C) Ḥaqīqī akhaṣṣ
D) None

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunction are distinguished by whether they prevent both parts being true, both being false, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following disjunction from kalām: 'The world is either created or eternal.' Determine which type of disjunction it is, and explain why.
?

5 min
Definition of Proposition and Its Main Divisions
Defines proposition and divides it into two types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a logical proposition according to the Islamic tradition and distinguish between categorical and conditional propositions.

Core Idea
A proposition is an utterance that can be true or false by itself.

In Islamic logic, the basic unit of reasoning is the proposition (qadiyya). This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary defines it clearly: it is an utterance that admits truth and falsehood by its own nature. The definition immediately excludes commands, questions, and wishes. After defining, the text divides propositions into two fundamental types: conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). Understanding this taxonomy is essential before studying syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of proposition
In the Arabic text, find the phrase 'ما احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ' (what admits truth by its essence). This is the core definition: an utterance that by itself can be evaluated as true or false.
The definition excludes utterances like commands or questions, which are not truth-apt.
2
Identify the two main divisions
Immediately after the definition, the text says 'ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ: شَرْطِيَّةٌ وَحَمْلِيَّةٌ'. This means propositions are divided into conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya).
Categorical propositions assert a relationship between subject and predicate; conditional ones involve a condition.
3
Understand the sub-divisions of categorical propositions
The text then notes that categorical propositions can be universal (kulliyya) or particular (shakhsiyya), and they can be quantified (musawwar) or indefinite (muhmal). This classification determines how the subject is taken.
Universal applies to all individuals of the subject; particular applies to some or a specific individual.
4
Apply the classification to an example
Take a proposition like 'Every prayer is obligatory'. This is categorical (subject-predicate), universal (kulliyya), and quantified (musawwar with 'kull'). Classify it according to the divisions learned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابٌ) في القَضَايا (وَأَحْكامِهَا)
حاشية الام الناجون على السلم المرونق
(٥٠) مَا احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ جَرَى بَيْنَهُمُ: «قَضِيَّةٌ) وَ(خَبَرَا» (٥١) ثُمَّ القَضَايَا عِنْدَهُمْ قِسْمَانِ : شَرْطِيَّةٌ حَمْلِيَّةٌ وَالثَّانِي: (٥٢) كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ، وَالأَوَّلُ : إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ، وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ (٥٣) وَالسُّورُ : كُلِّيّا، وَجُزْئِيَّا يُرَى وَأَرْبَعُ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرَى : (٥٤) إِمَّا بِكُلِّ، أَوْ بِبَعْضٍ، أَوْ بِلَا شَيْءٍ، وَلَيْسَ بَعْضُ أَوْ شِبْهِ جَلَا (٥٥) وَكُلُّهَا : مُوجَبَةٌ، وَسَالِبَهُ فهيَ إِذا إِلَى الثَّمَانِ آيبَهُ
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. Commentary of the Imam al-Najun? on al-Sullam al-Murawnaq. (50) What admits truth and falsehood by its essence is called among them 'proposition' and 'informative statement'. (51) Then propositions according to them are two types: conditional and categorical. And the second (52) is universal and particular, and the first [of them] is either quantified or indefinite. (53) And the quantifier: universal and particular are seen, and its four parts where it runs: (54) either with 'kull' (all), or with 'ba'd' (some), or with 'la shay'' (nothing), or 'laysa ba'd' (not some) or like that. (55) And all of them are affirmative and negative, so they become eight.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the proposition and divides it into its primary types. A proposition is an utterance that by its nature can be true or false. It is called either qadiyya or khabar. Propositions are first divided into conditional (shartiyya) and categorical (hamliyya). Categorical propositions are then divided into universal (kulliyya) and particular (shakhsiyya) relative to the subject, and they can be quantified (musawwar) or indefinite (muhmal). The quantifier (sur) is either universal or particular. The text then lists four quantifiers: 'kull' (all), 'ba'd' (some), 'la shay'' (no), and 'laysa ba'd' (not some). The fourth is problematic; it likely means 'not some' i.e., the quantifier for particular negative. Then it notes that each of these can be affirmative or negative, giving eight types (four quantifiers × two qualities).

Quantifiers and the Eight Categorical Propositions
Lists quantifiers and explains they combine with quality to produce eight types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four quantifiers and explain how they combine with quality (affirmation/negation) to produce eight categorical proposition types.

Core Idea
Four quantifiers times two qualities equals eight proposition types.

After dividing propositions into categorical and conditional, the text focuses on the quantifiers (sur) that specify the scope of the subject in categorical propositions. It lists four quantifiers: 'kull' (all), 'ba'd' (some), 'la shay'' (none/not one), and 'laysa ba'd' (not some). Each quantifier can be paired with affirmative or negative quality, yielding eight distinct proposition types. This systematic classification is crucial for assessing logical relations and valid inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four quantifiers in the Arabic text
In lines 53-54, the text mentions 'بِكُلِّ' (with all), 'بِبَعْضٍ' (with some), 'بِلَا شَيْءٍ' (with nothing), and 'لَيْسَ بَعْضٌ' (not some). These are the quantifiers for universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative respectively.
'La shay'' (no thing) means 'none' and indicates universal negation. 'Laysa ba'd' (not some) is the quantifier for particular negation, often expressed as 'some not'.
2
Understand the combination of quantifier and quality
The text says 'وَكُلُّهَا: مُوجَبَةٌ، وَسَالِبَةٌ' meaning all of them (the quantifiers) can be affirmative or negative. So for each quantifier, there are two possibilities: e.g., 'Every X is Y' (universal affirmative) and 'No X is Y' (universal negative). This yields 4 × 2 = 8 types.
The fourth quantifier 'laysa ba'd' is the particular negative; it is often confusing because it uses negation in the quantifier itself.
3
Work through an example for each of the eight types
Take the subject 'prayer' and predicate 'obligatory'. Universal affirmative: 'Every prayer is obligatory'. Universal negative: 'No prayer is obligatory'. Particular affirmative: 'Some prayer is obligatory'. Particular negative: 'Some prayer is not obligatory'. Note the quantifier for particular negative is 'not some' (laysa ba'd), but in Arabic logical tradition it is treated as a distinct quantifier.
The eight types are foundational for the Square of Opposition and immediate inferences.
4
Apply the classification to a fiqh ruling
Consider the ruling: 'All intoxicants are forbidden' (universal affirmative). Its negative counterpart: 'No intoxicant is forbidden' (false, but logically valid form). Identify which quantifier and quality each represents.

Categorical and Conditional Propositions
Explains subject/predicate and conditional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between categorical and conditional propositions and identify their parts (subject/predicate, antecedent/consequent).

Core Idea
Every proposition is either categorical (subject-predicate) or conditional (antecedent-consequent).

The passage explains the basic structure of propositions in Islamic logic. Categorical propositions have a subject and a predicate, while conditional propositions are based on a condition and divide into conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or). Understanding this division is essential for analyzing arguments in Fiqh and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish proposition type
Look for a condition word like 'if' or 'either'; if present, the proposition is conditional. Otherwise, it is categorical.
In the Arabic text, the term 'تعليق' indicates conditionality.
2
Identify subject and predicate in categorical
In a categorical proposition, the subject (موضوع) is what is being talked about, and the predicate (محمول) is what is affirmed or denied of it.
For example, in 'Zakat is obligatory', 'Zakat' is the subject and 'obligatory' is the predicate.
3
Recognize antecedent and consequent in conditionals
In a conditional, the antecedent (مقدم) is the condition and the consequent (تالي) is the result.
In 'If you possess nisab, then zakat is due', the antecedent is 'you possess nisab' and the consequent is 'zakat is due'.
4
Classify conditionals as conjunctive or disjunctive
If the conditional indicates that the antecedent necessarily leads to the consequent (correlation), it is conjunctive (متصل). If it indicates opposition (تنافر) between the parts, it is disjunctive (منفصل).
The passage defines conjunctive as necessitating correlation and disjunctive as necessitating opposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ : «المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة (٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ (٥٨) أَيْضاً : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَهُ، وَمِثْلِهَا شَرْطِيَّةٌ مُنْفَصِلَهُ (٥٩) جُزَاهُمَا: مُقَدَّمُ، وَتَالِي أَمَّا بَيَانُ ذَاتِ الأَنْصَالِ : (٦٠) «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَلَازُمَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَذَاتُ الانْفِصَالِ دُونَ مَيْنِ : (٦١) «مَا أَوْجَبَتْ تَنَافُراً بَيْنَهُمَا أَقْسَامُهَا ثَلَاثَةٌ فَلْتُعْلَمَا
(٦٢) مَانِعُ جَمْعٍ، أَوْ خُلُوِّ ، أَوْ هُمَا وَهْوَ الحَقِيقِيُّ الأَخَصُّ فَاعْلَمَا
(56) And the first [of the categorical] is the subject in the categorical, and the other is the predicate equally. (57) And if a judgment is made based on a condition in it, then it is 'conditional', and it divides (58) also into conditional conjunctive and similarly conditional disjunctive. (59) Their two parts: antecedent and consequent. As for the explanation of the essence of the conjunctive: (60) 'That which necessitates correlation of the two parts.' And the essence of the disjunctive: (61) 'That which necessitates opposition between them. Its divisions are three, so let them be known: (62) Preventer of conjunction, or preventer of disjunction, or both, and it is the veritable specific one, so know it.'
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of categorical and conditional propositions. In categorical (hamliyya), the first part is the subject (mawdu') and the second is the predicate (mahmul). When a proposition is based on a condition (ta'liq), it is called conditional (shartiyya), which divides into conjunctive (mutassila) and disjunctive (munfasila). The two parts of a conditional are the antecedent (mukaddam) and consequent (tali). The conjunctive conditional is defined as that which necessitates correlation (talazum) between its two parts, meaning if the antecedent is true, the consequent is true. The disjunctive conditional is defined as that which necessitates opposition (tanafur) between its parts, meaning they cannot both be true or both false in a certain way. The disjunctive has three types: (1) mani' jam' (preventer of conjunction), meaning they cannot both be true; (2) mani' khulu (preventer of disjunction), meaning they cannot both be false; (3) both, which is the true disjunctive (haqiqi), where exactly one is true.

تنافر

Key Takeaway
Propositions are either categorical (subject-predicate) or conditional (antecedent-consequent); conditionals are further divided into conjunctive (correlation) and disjunctive (opposition).

Types of Disjunctive Propositions
Defines disjunctive and lists its three subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of disjunctive propositions: mani' jam', mani' khulu, and haqiqi.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions express opposition; the three types determine whether the options can be both true, both false, or exactly one.

The passage introduces disjunctive conditionals as those that necessitate opposition between their parts, and then briefly mentions three subtypes. In this lesson, we unpack these subtypes and learn how to test which type a given disjunctive belongs to. This skill is crucial for analyzing either-or arguments in Islamic theology and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify a disjunctive proposition
Look for a proposition that presents two alternatives with an 'or' (أو). It indicates opposition (تنافر) between the parts.
For example: 'The prayer is either shortened or complete.'
2
Test if both parts can be true simultaneously
If both parts cannot be true at the same time, the disjunctive is mani' jam' (preventer of conjunction).
Example: 'Either you are in travel or you are resident' – both cannot be true, so it is mani' jam'.
3
Test if both parts can be false simultaneously
If both parts cannot be false at the same time (i.e., at least one must be true), it is mani' khulu (preventer of disjunction).
Example: 'Either the sun has risen or it is night' – both cannot be false, so it is mani' khulu.
4
Identify the haqiqi (true disjunctive)
If both conditions hold (cannot both be true and cannot both be false), it is haqiqi, meaning exactly one part is true.
Example: 'Either the person is alive or dead' – exactly one is true, so it is haqiqi.

The Five Rational Proofs
Author classifies rational proofs into five types and ranks them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five rational proofs and explain why demonstration (burhan) is the highest.

Core Idea
Demonstration alone gives certainty; the other four proofs are weaker.

Proofs are either transmitted (from revelation) or rational (from intellect). The rational proofs come in five types, but they are not equal. The author ranks them, with demonstration at the top because it alone is built from certain premises. Let's learn to distinguish them and understand the hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish transmitted from rational proof
Note the two broad categories: naqliyya (based on revelation or tradition) and aqliyya (based on intellect). All five proofs listed are aqliyya.
Transmitted proofs are not discussed further here.
2
Memorize the five rational proof types
The five are: khataba (rhetoric), shi'r (poetry), burhan (demonstration), jadal (dialectic), safsata (sophistry). Notice the order in the text: this is a standard classification.
Some classifications replace poetry with another type, but this list is canonical.
3
Rank the proofs by certainty
The author says burhan is the 'greatest' (ajalluha). Why? Because its premises are certain (yaqiniyyat). The other proofs use premises that are merely persuasive or commonly accepted, not certain.
This ranking is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences.
4
Apply the classification to an example
Consider a theological argument: 'The world is temporally originated, therefore it has a creator.' If the premise 'everything temporally originated has a creator' is certain, this is burhan. If it's merely commonly accepted, it could be jadal.
Practice: find a real kalam argument and decide which proof it belongs to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(أَقْسَامُ الحُجَّةِ)
وَحُجَّةٌ نَقْلِيَّةٌ عَقْلِيَّة أَقْسَامُ هَذِي خَمْسَةٌ جَلِيَّة: خَطَابَةٌ، شِعْرٌ، وَبُرْهَانٌ، جَدَلْ وَخَامِسٌ: سَفْسَطَةٌ؛ نِلْتَ الأَمَل أَجَلُّهَا «البُرْهَانُ»: مَا أُلْفَ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ بِاليَقِينِ تَفْتَرِنْ مِنْ: أَوَّلِيَّاتٍ، مُشَاهَدَاتِ مُجَرَّبَاتٍ، مُتَوَاتِرَاتِ وَحَدَسِيَّاتٍ، وَمَحْسُوسَاتِ؛ فَتِلْكَ جُمْلَةُ اليَقِينِيَّاتِ وَفِي دَلَالَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ خِلَافٌ آتِ عَقْلِيُّ، أَوْ عَادِيُّ، أَوْ تَوَلُّدُ، أَوْ وَاجِبٌ؛ وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ
Divisions of Proof: And a proof is either transmitted or rational. Its divisions are five clear ones: rhetoric, poetry, demonstration, dialectic, and the fifth: sophistry; you have attained the hope. The greatest of them is 'demonstration': it is composed of premises that are certain: from self-evidents, observations, experiments, mass-transmitted reports, intuitives, and sensibles. Those are the sum of certainties. And concerning the indication of the premises upon the conclusion, there is a coming disagreement: rational, customary, generative, or necessary; and the first is the supported one.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage classifies proofs (hujaj) into two broad categories: transmitted (naqliyya, based on revelation or tradition) and rational (aqliyya, based on intellect). Within rational proofs, there are five specific types: rhetoric (khataba), poetry (shi'r), demonstration (burhan), dialectic (jadal), and sophistry (safsata). The author declares demonstration as the highest because it is composed of certain premises. He then lists six types of certain premises: self-evidents (awwaliyyat), observations (mushahadat), experiments (mujarrabat), mass-transmitted reports (mutawatirat), intuitives (hadasiyyat), and sensibles (mahsusat). These together constitute the certainties (yaqiniyyat). Finally, he notes a future disagreement about how the premises indicate the conclusion: whether the connection is rational (aqli), customary (adi), generative (tawallud), or necessary (wajib), and states that the 'rational' view is the supported one.

Key Takeaway
The five rational proofs are not equal; demonstration (burhan) is the only one that yields certainty because its premises are yaqiniyyat.

The Six Certain Premises
Author lists six types of premises that yield certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and define each of the six types of certain premises (yaqiniyyat) and explain how each type yields certainty.

Core Idea
Certainty comes from six sources: self-evident truths, observation, experiment, mass transmission, intuition, and sensation.

Demonstration (burhan) is built from certain premises. But what makes a premise certain? The author lists six types of premises that are accepted as yielding certain knowledge. Memorizing and understanding these is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the six terms
The six are: awwaliyyat (self-evidents), mushahadat (observations), mujarrabat (experiments), mutawatirat (mass-transmitted reports), hadasiyyat (intuitives), and mahsusat (sensibles). Say them aloud and write them down.
These are the yaqiniyyat, the 'certainties'.
2
Define each type
For each term, write a brief definition: awwaliyyat are self-evident truths like 'the whole is greater than the part'; mushahadat are based on direct observation; mujarrabat are from repeated experiments; mutawatirat are reports with so many narrators that agreement on falsehood is impossible; hadasiyyat are intuitive insights (like knowing fire is hot without seeing it); mahsusat are from the five senses.
Some definitions may overlap; the key is the source of certainty.
3
Associate each with an Islamic science example
For instance, awwaliyyat are used in fiqh usul: 'a ruling cannot be both wajib and haram at the same time' is self-evident. Mutawatirat are the basis of Quranic transmission. Mahsusat are used in witnessing court cases.
Think of examples from your own studies.
4
Explain why they are called 'certain'
Each of these premises carries certainty (yaqin) because they cannot be doubted. The author lists them as the building blocks of burhan. Without them, an argument cannot be demonstrative.
Compare with premises of other proofs: mashhurat (commonly accepted) for jadal, muqni'at (persuasive) for khataba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(أَقْسَامُ الحُجَّةِ)
وَحُجَّةٌ نَقْلِيَّةٌ عَقْلِيَّة أَقْسَامُ هَذِي خَمْسَةٌ جَلِيَّة: خَطَابَةٌ، شِعْرٌ، وَبُرْهَانٌ، جَدَلْ وَخَامِسٌ: سَفْسَطَةٌ؛ نِلْتَ الأَمَل أَجَلُّهَا «البُرْهَانُ»: مَا أُلْفَ مِنْ مُقَدِّمَاتٍ بِاليَقِينِ تَفْتَرِنْ مِنْ: أَوَّلِيَّاتٍ، مُشَاهَدَاتِ مُجَرَّبَاتٍ، مُتَوَاتِرَاتِ وَحَدَسِيَّاتٍ، وَمَحْسُوسَاتِ؛ فَتِلْكَ جُمْلَةُ اليَقِينِيَّاتِ وَفِي دَلَالَةِ المُقَدِّمَاتِ عَلَى النَّتِيجَةِ خِلَافٌ آتِ عَقْلِيُّ، أَوْ عَادِيُّ، أَوْ تَوَلُّدُ، أَوْ وَاجِبٌ؛ وَالأَوَّلُ المُؤَيَّدُ
Divisions of Proof: And a proof is either transmitted or rational. Its divisions are five clear ones: rhetoric, poetry, demonstration, dialectic, and the fifth: sophistry; you have attained the hope. The greatest of them is 'demonstration': it is composed of premises that are certain: from self-evidents, observations, experiments, mass-transmitted reports, intuitives, and sensibles. Those are the sum of certainties. And concerning the indication of the premises upon the conclusion, there is a coming disagreement: rational, customary, generative, or necessary; and the first is the supported one.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage classifies proofs (hujaj) into two broad categories: transmitted (naqliyya, based on revelation or tradition) and rational (aqliyya, based on intellect). Within rational proofs, there are five specific types: rhetoric (khataba), poetry (shi'r), demonstration (burhan), dialectic (jadal), and sophistry (safsata). The author declares demonstration as the highest because it is composed of certain premises. He then lists six types of certain premises: self-evidents (awwaliyyat), observations (mushahadat), experiments (mujarrabat), mass-transmitted reports (mutawatirat), intuitives (hadasiyyat), and sensibles (mahsusat). These together constitute the certainties (yaqiniyyat). Finally, he notes a future disagreement about how the premises indicate the conclusion: whether the connection is rational (aqli), customary (adi), generative (tawallud), or necessary (wajib), and states that the 'rational' view is the supported one.

Carrying Absolute to Restricted: Condition of Single Restrictor
Objection and answer on mutlaq/muqayyad principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition for carrying mutlaq to muqayyad in usul al-fiqh and apply it to examples with multiple restrictions.

Core Idea
Absolute is carried to restricted only if the restrictor is single or multiple restrictions are unified.

In usul al-fiqh, a key principle is that when a command is given in absolute terms and also in restricted terms, the absolute is carried to the restricted. However, as this passage clarifies, this principle only applies when the restriction is a single one. When multiple different restrictions exist, carrying the absolute becomes problematic. Let's examine the objection and its answer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector states that the known principle among usuliyyun is to carry the absolute to the restricted, citing the example of zihar and killing verses where the absolute 'free a slave' was carried to 'free a believing slave'. Note that this contradicts the previous point being made in the original text.
The objection is based on empirical examples from Qur'anic rulings.
2
Extract the answer's condition
The answerer responds that the carrying is conditioned on the restrictor being only one. If there are multiple different restrictors, it is not permissible to carry the absolute to all due to conflict, nor to choose one arbitrarily.
The condition is a logical safeguard against arbitrariness.
3
Understand the exception from al-Shirshimi
Al-Shirshimi clarifies that if multiple restrictions are essentially unified (e.g., all describing the same quality), the absolute is carried to the restricted as if the restrictor were single.
Unified restrictions are treated as one restrictor in effect.
4
Apply the condition to new examples
Given a set of texts with absolute and restricted expressions, determine whether the condition of single/unified restriction is met. If restrictions conflict, the absolute is not carried.
Practice with usul al-fiqh examples to solidify the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ قِيلَ: القَاعِدَةُ عِنْدَ الأُصُولِيِّينَ وَغَيْرِهِمْ عَكْسُ ذَلِكَ - أَعْنِي : حَمْلَ المُطْلَقِ عَلَى المُقَيَّدِ - ؛ كَمَا فِي آيَتَي الظَّهَارِ وَالقَتْلِ، فَإِنَّهُمْ حَمَلُوا المُطْلَقَةَ عَنِ التَّقِيدِ بِـ «المُؤْمِنَةِ» عَلَى الْمُقَيَّدَةِ بِهَا . أَجِيْبَ بِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ مَشْرُوطٌ بِكَوْنِ المُقَيِّدِ وَاحِداً فَقَطْ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا كَانَ مُتَعَدِّداً وَتَغَايَرَتِ القُيُودُ ، إِذْ لَا جَائِزَ أَنْ يُحْمَلَ المُطْلَقُ عَلَى الكُلِّ لِتَنَافِي القُيُودِ، وَلَا أَنْ يُحْمَلَ عَلَى وَاحِدٍ دُونَ آخَرَ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنَ التَّحَكُمِ. وَالشِّرْشِيمِيُّ قَالَ: قَوْلُهُ: (وَتَغَايَرَتِ القُيُودُ) بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا اتَّحَدَتْ، فَإِنَّ المُقَيَّدَ المُتَعَدِّدَ كَالْمُقَيَّدِ الْوَاحِدِ؛ وَذَلِكَ كَمَا إِذَا قِيلَ: فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ، وَقِيلَ أَيْضاً: فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُذْعِنَةٍ وَرَاضِيَةٍ بِمَا جَاءَ بِهِ النَّبِيُّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، وَقِيلَ أَيْضاً: «فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ»؛ فَيُحْمَلُ المُطْلَقُ عَلَى المُقَيَّدِ أَيْضاً.
For it is said: The principle with the usuliyyun and others is the opposite of that - I mean: carrying the absolute to the restricted - as in the two verses of zihar and killing, for they carried the absolute (free from restriction by 'believing') onto the restricted by it. It was answered that that is conditioned upon the restrictor being only one, contrary to when it is multiple and the restrictions differ, for it is not permissible to carry the absolute onto all (restrictions) due to the conflict of restrictions, nor to carry it onto one to the exclusion of another, due to the arbitrariness therein. And al-Shirshimi said: His statement: 'and the restrictions differ' is contrary to when they are unified, for the multiple restricted (expressions) are like a single restricted one; and that is as when it is said: 'freeing a believing slave', and it is also said: 'freeing a slave who is submissive and content with what the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought', and it is also said: 'freeing a slave'; then the absolute is carried onto the restricted as well.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding a principle in usul al-fiqh. The objection states that contrary to the earlier point, the established rule among usuliyyun is to carry the absolute (mutlaq) onto the restricted (muqayyad) - for example, in the verses of zihar (Q. 58:3-4) and killing (Q. 4:92), where the absolute command to free a slave is restricted by 'believing' in one verse, and the absolute in another verse is carried onto that restricted form. The answer clarifies that this carrying is conditional on the restrictor being a single one. If there are multiple different restrictors (e.g., 'believing,' 'submissive,' etc.), it is not permissible to carry the absolute onto all of them because they conflict, nor to choose one arbitrarily. Al-Shirshimi adds an explanatory note: if the multiple restrictors are essentially the same (e.g., all describe the same quality), the absolute is carried onto the restricted as if it were a single restrictor. This is illustrated with examples from fiqh: if one text says 'free a believing slave,' another says 'free a submissive and content slave,' and a third says just 'free a slave,' the absolute is carried onto the restricted because the various descriptions are compatible.

مطلق
مقيد
قاعدة
القُيُود
المُقَيِّد

A) The absolute must be grammatically definite.
B) The restrictors must be only one in number.
C) The restrictors must all be compatible and effectively one.
D) The restricted must be a proper noun.

Key Takeaway
The carrying of absolute to restricted requires the restriction(s) to be effectively single or unified; otherwise arbitrariness results.

Insha'iyya vs Khabariyya: When is the Basmala a Proposition?
Discussion of basmala as performative or declarative and its status as a proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between insha'iyya and khabariyya uses of the basmala and determine whether it qualifies as a logical proposition.

Core Idea
The basmala is a proposition only when intended declaratively, not performatively.

In Islamic logic, every utterance at the start of a book is examined for its propositional status. The basmala can be either a performative act (insha'iyya) or a declarative statement (khabariyya). Only the latter counts as a proposition (qadiyya), which is essential for truth-conditional analysis. This lesson clarifies the distinction using the criteria from the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Note that the basmala can be either insha'iyya (performative) or khabariyya (declarative). Performative utterances effect an action (like seeking help), while declarative statements inform about a state of affairs.
2
Check the condition for being a proposition
Only khabariyya is called a proposition (qadiyya). Insha'iyya is not a proposition because it does not have truth value; it is an act.
3
Examine the implied verb
The passage explains that the type depends on the implied verb (e.g., 'asta'in' for seeking help vs 'u'allif' for composing). The intended meaning determines the category.
Al-Anbabi's commentary elaborates on the estimation of the verb.
4
Apply to real examples
Consider the basmala before a contract: if intended as a performative act of blessing, it is insha'iyya; if intended to inform that one begins with Allah's name, it is khabariyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِكُلِّ شَارِعٍ فِي فَنْ أَنْ يَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَى البَسْمَلَةِ بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُ ذَلِكَ الفَنِّ؛ بِحَقِّ البَسْمَلَةِ، وَهُوَ: أَنْ لَا يَتْرُكَ الكَلَامَ عَلَيْهَا رَأْساً. وَبِحَقِّ الفَنِّ المَشْرُوعِ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ: أَنْ يَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَيْهَا بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُ ذَلِكَ الفَنِّ. وَنَحْنُ الآنَ شَارِعُونَ فِي فَنِّ المَنْطِقِ، فَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ نَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَيْهَا بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُهُ؛ فَنَقُولُ: قَدِ اشْتُهِرَ أَنَّ جُمْلَةَ البَسْمَلَةِ يَصِحُ أَنْ تَكُونَ إِنْشَائِيَّةً، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ خَبَرِيَّةً؛ فَعَلَى الْأَوَّلِ لَا تُسَمَّى تِلْكَ الجُمْلَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى بِهَا الإِنْشَاءُ، بَلِ الخَبَرُ فَقَط. وَأَمَّا عَلَى الثَّانِي فَتُسَمَّى بِهَا. ثُمَّ إِنْ قُدِرَ المُتَعَلَّقُ نَحْوُ «ابْتَدِئُ» كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةً شَخْصِيَّةً؛ لِأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا مُشَخَّصٌ مُعَيَّنٌ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ القَضِيَّةِ الشَّخْصِيَّةِ. قَالَ الأَنْبَابِي: قَوْلُهُ: (يَصِحُ أَنْ تَكُونَ إِنْشَائِيَّةً) أَيْ: بِاعْتِبَارِ المُتَعَلَّقِ كَمَا هُوَ المُتَبَادَرُ، وَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ تُجْعَلَ البَاءُ لِمُجَرَّدِ التَّعْدِيَةِ مُتَعَلِّقَةً بِمَحْذُوفٍ تَقْدِيرُهُ: أَسْتَعِينُ أَوِ اسْتِعَانَتِي، وَقَصْدِ إِنْشَاءِ الاسْتِعَانَةِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (وَأَنْ تَكُونَ خَبَرِيَّةً) أَيْ: بِأَنْ يُقَدَّرَ المُتَعَلَّقُ أُؤَلِّفُ، أَوْ أَبْتَدِئُ، أَوِ ابْتِدَائِي، وَقَصْدِ الإِخْبَارِ عَنِ الابْتِدَاءِ أَوِ التَّأْلِيفِ الحَاصِلِ مِنْهُ. وَفِي المَقَامِ احْتِمَالَاتٌ أُخَرُ، وَالَّذِي اخْتَارَهُ الصَّبَّانُ وَغَيْرُهُ: أَنَّ البَاءَ إِذَا جُعِلَتْ لِلِاسْتِعَانَةِ أَوِ المُصَاحَبَةِ فَالْجُمْلَةُ خَبَرِيَّةُ الصَّدْرِ - أَعْنِي: أُؤَلِّفُ مَثَلًا - لِصِدْقِ حَدِّ الخَبَرِ عَلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ الكَلَامُ الَّذِي يَتَحَقَّقُ مَدْلُولُهُ خَارِجًا بِدُونِ ذِكْرِهِ، إِنْشَائِيَّةُ العَجْزِ لِعَدَمِ تَحَقُّقِ الاسْتِعَانَةِ بِاسْمِهِ أَوِ المُصَاحَبَةِ لَهُ بِدُونِ ذِكْرِ «بِسْمِ اللهِ». فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: الجَارُ وَالمَجْرُورُ لَيْسَ بِكَلَامٍ، فَكَيْفَ جُعِلَ إِنْشَاءً؟ قُلْتُ: هُوَ فِي مَعْنَى الكَلَامِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي مَعْنَى: أَسْتَعِينُ بِاسْمِ اللهِ تَعَالَى، أَوْ أُصَاحِبُ اسْمَ اللهِ تَعَالَى. اهـ
And know that it behooves every beginner in an art to speak about the basmala with something that suits that art; by right of the basmala, which is: that he does not leave speech about it entirely. And by right of the art undertaken, which is: that he speak about it with something that suits that art. And we are now beginners in the art of logic, so it behooves us to speak about it with something that suits it; so we say: It is well-known that the sentence of the basmala can be performative (insha'iyya) or declarative (khabariyya). On the first, that sentence is not called a proposition, because the performative is not so called, only the declarative. On the second, it is called a proposition. Then if the implied related term is something like 'I begin', it becomes a particular proposition, because the subject of judgment is specified and particular, as is the criterion of the particular proposition. Al-Anbabi said: His statement: 'can be performative' means: with respect to the related term as is the apparent meaning, and that is by making the 'ba' for mere transitivity, attached to an omitted (term) whose estimation is 'I seek help' or 'my seeking help', and intending the performance of seeking help. And his statement: 'and can be declarative' means: by estimating the related term as 'I compose' or 'I begin' or 'my beginning', and intending to inform about the commencement or composition undertaken by him. In this place there are other possibilities, and what al-Sabban and others chose is that when the 'ba' is made for seeking help or accompaniment, the sentence is declarative in its first part - I mean: 'I compose' for example - because the definition of declarative applies to it, and it is speech whose meaning is actualized externally without its mention; but the final part is performative because the seeking help by His name or accompaniment of it is not actualized without the mention of 'Bismillah'. If you say: The prepositional phrase is not speech, so how is it made performative? I say: It is in the meaning of speech, because it is in the meaning of 'I seek help by the name of Allah Most High' or 'I accompany the name of Allah Most High.' End.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and methodological discussion about the basmala (the phrase 'Bismillah') within the context of logic. It begins by stating that anyone beginning a book should speak about the basmala in a manner appropriate to the art. For logic, we say: The basmala sentence can be either insha'iyya (performative, like an act of seeking help) or khabariyya (declarative, stating a fact). If it is performative, it is not called a proposition (qadiyya) because propositions are only declarative. If declarative, it is a proposition. Furthermore, if the implied verb is 'I begin' (abtadi'), it becomes a particular proposition (qadiyya shakhsiyya) because the subject ('I') is specific. Al-Anbabi expands: When the 'ba' is for transitivity and the implied verb is 'I seek help' (asta'in) or similar, the intention is to perform the act of seeking help, making it insha'iyya. When the implied verb is 'I compose' (u'allif) or 'I begin' (abtadi'), the intention is to inform, making it khabariyya. Al-Sabban's chosen position is that if the 'ba' is for seeking help or accompaniment, the sentence is khabari in its main clause (like 'I compose') but insha'i in its final part (the prepositional phrase), because the act of seeking help or accompaniment is only actualized by uttering 'Bismillah'. This answers an objection: the prepositional phrase alone is not a complete sentence, but it functions like one in meaning.

Key Terms
إنشائية
خبرية
قضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the basmala NOT called a proposition?
A) When it is declarative (khabariyya).
B) When it is performative (insha'iyya).
C) When it is a particular proposition.
D) When the implied verb is 'I begin'.

Key Takeaway
The basmala is considered a logical proposition only when used declaratively; when used performatively, it does not have truth value and is not a proposition.

The Basmala as a Particular Proposition
Discussion of the basmala as a particular proposition with implied subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the basmala becomes a particular proposition (qadiyya shakhsiyya) and explain the role of the subject's specificity.

Core Idea
The basmala becomes a particular proposition when the subject of the implied verb is a specific individual, like 'I'.

In logic, propositions are classified as universal or particular based on their subject. The basmala, when the implied verb is 'I begin' (abtadi'), has a specific subject (the speaker), making it a particular proposition. This lesson examines the criterion for particularity and how it applies to the basmala, drawing on the definitions in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for particularity
The passage states that if the implied related term is 'I begin' (abtadi'), the basmala becomes a particular proposition.
2
Recognize the subject's specificity
The subject of 'I begin' is the speaker, a specific individual, not a general category. This matches the definition of a particular proposition: the subject is a specific entity.
3
Apply the criterion of particular proposition
The passage gives the criterion: 'the subject of judgment is specified and particular.' Check that the basmala with implied 'I begin' satisfies this.
4
Contrast with other implied verbs
What if the implied verb is 'I seek help' (asta'in)? Is the subject still specific? Yes, also 'I', so it would also be particular. The passage notes that the basmala can be particular in both cases, but the classification as insha'iyya or khabariyya differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِكُلِّ شَارِعٍ فِي فَنْ أَنْ يَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَى البَسْمَلَةِ بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُ ذَلِكَ الفَنِّ؛ بِحَقِّ البَسْمَلَةِ، وَهُوَ: أَنْ لَا يَتْرُكَ الكَلَامَ عَلَيْهَا رَأْساً. وَبِحَقِّ الفَنِّ المَشْرُوعِ فِيهِ، وَهُوَ: أَنْ يَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَيْهَا بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُ ذَلِكَ الفَنِّ. وَنَحْنُ الآنَ شَارِعُونَ فِي فَنِّ المَنْطِقِ، فَيَنْبَغِي أَنْ نَتَكَلَّمَ عَلَيْهَا بِطَرَفٍ مِمَّا يُنَاسِبُهُ؛ فَنَقُولُ: قَدِ اشْتُهِرَ أَنَّ جُمْلَةَ البَسْمَلَةِ يَصِحُ أَنْ تَكُونَ إِنْشَائِيَّةً، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ خَبَرِيَّةً؛ فَعَلَى الْأَوَّلِ لَا تُسَمَّى تِلْكَ الجُمْلَةُ قَضِيَّةٌ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى بِهَا الإِنْشَاءُ، بَلِ الخَبَرُ فَقَط. وَأَمَّا عَلَى الثَّانِي فَتُسَمَّى بِهَا. ثُمَّ إِنْ قُدِرَ المُتَعَلَّقُ نَحْوُ «ابْتَدِئُ» كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةً شَخْصِيَّةً؛ لِأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا مُشَخَّصٌ مُعَيَّنٌ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ القَضِيَّةِ الشَّخْصِيَّةِ. قَالَ الأَنْبَابِي: قَوْلُهُ: (يَصِحُ أَنْ تَكُونَ إِنْشَائِيَّةً) أَيْ: بِاعْتِبَارِ المُتَعَلَّقِ كَمَا هُوَ المُتَبَادَرُ، وَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ تُجْعَلَ البَاءُ لِمُجَرَّدِ التَّعْدِيَةِ مُتَعَلِّقَةً بِمَحْذُوفٍ تَقْدِيرُهُ: أَسْتَعِينُ أَوِ اسْتِعَانَتِي، وَقَصْدِ إِنْشَاءِ الاسْتِعَانَةِ. وَقَوْلُهُ: (وَأَنْ تَكُونَ خَبَرِيَّةً) أَيْ: بِأَنْ يُقَدَّرَ المُتَعَلَّقُ أُؤَلِّفُ، أَوْ أَبْتَدِئُ، أَوِ ابْتِدَائِي، وَقَصْدِ الإِخْبَارِ عَنِ الابْتِدَاءِ أَوِ التَّأْلِيفِ الحَاصِلِ مِنْهُ. وَفِي المَقَامِ احْتِمَالَاتٌ أُخَرُ، وَالَّذِي اخْتَارَهُ الصَّبَّانُ وَغَيْرُهُ: أَنَّ البَاءَ إِذَا جُعِلَتْ لِلِاسْتِعَانَةِ أَوِ المُصَاحَبَةِ فَالْجُمْلَةُ خَبَرِيَّةُ الصَّدْرِ - أَعْنِي: أُؤَلِّفُ مَثَلًا - لِصِدْقِ حَدِّ الخَبَرِ عَلَيْهِ، وَهُوَ الكَلَامُ الَّذِي يَتَحَقَّقُ مَدْلُولُهُ خَارِجًا بِدُونِ ذِكْرِهِ، إِنْشَائِيَّةُ العَجْزِ لِعَدَمِ تَحَقُّقِ الاسْتِعَانَةِ بِاسْمِهِ أَوِ المُصَاحَبَةِ لَهُ بِدُونِ ذِكْرِ «بِسْمِ اللهِ». فَإِنْ قُلْتَ: الجَارُ وَالمَجْرُورُ لَيْسَ بِكَلَامٍ، فَكَيْفَ جُعِلَ إِنْشَاءً؟ قُلْتُ: هُوَ فِي مَعْنَى الكَلَامِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ فِي مَعْنَى: أَسْتَعِينُ بِاسْمِ اللهِ تَعَالَى، أَوْ أُصَاحِبُ اسْمَ اللهِ تَعَالَى. اهـ
And know that it behooves every beginner in an art to speak about the basmala with something that suits that art; by right of the basmala, which is: that he does not leave speech about it entirely. And by right of the art undertaken, which is: that he speak about it with something that suits that art. And we are now beginners in the art of logic, so it behooves us to speak about it with something that suits it; so we say: It is well-known that the sentence of the basmala can be performative (insha'iyya) or declarative (khabariyya). On the first, that sentence is not called a proposition, because the performative is not so called, only the declarative. On the second, it is called a proposition. Then if the implied related term is something like 'I begin', it becomes a particular proposition, because the subject of judgment is specified and particular, as is the criterion of the particular proposition. Al-Anbabi said: His statement: 'can be performative' means: with respect to the related term as is the apparent meaning, and that is by making the 'ba' for mere transitivity, attached to an omitted (term) whose estimation is 'I seek help' or 'my seeking help', and intending the performance of seeking help. And his statement: 'and can be declarative' means: by estimating the related term as 'I compose' or 'I begin' or 'my beginning', and intending to inform about the commencement or composition undertaken by him. In this place there are other possibilities, and what al-Sabban and others chose is that when the 'ba' is made for seeking help or accompaniment, the sentence is declarative in its first part - I mean: 'I compose' for example - because the definition of declarative applies to it, and it is speech whose meaning is actualized externally without its mention; but the final part is performative because the seeking help by His name or accompaniment of it is not actualized without the mention of 'Bismillah'. If you say: The prepositional phrase is not speech, so how is it made performative? I say: It is in the meaning of speech, because it is in the meaning of 'I seek help by the name of Allah Most High' or 'I accompany the name of Allah Most High.' End.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and methodological discussion about the basmala (the phrase 'Bismillah') within the context of logic. It begins by stating that anyone beginning a book should speak about the basmala in a manner appropriate to the art. For logic, we say: The basmala sentence can be either insha'iyya (performative, like an act of seeking help) or khabariyya (declarative, stating a fact). If it is performative, it is not called a proposition (qadiyya) because propositions are only declarative. If declarative, it is a proposition. Furthermore, if the implied verb is 'I begin' (abtadi'), it becomes a particular proposition (qadiyya shakhsiyya) because the subject ('I') is specific. Al-Anbabi expands: When the 'ba' is for transitivity and the implied verb is 'I seek help' (asta'in) or similar, the intention is to perform the act of seeking help, making it insha'iyya. When the implied verb is 'I compose' (u'allif) or 'I begin' (abtadi'), the intention is to inform, making it khabariyya. Al-Sabban's chosen position is that if the 'ba' is for seeking help or accompaniment, the sentence is khabari in its main clause (like 'I compose') but insha'i in its final part (the prepositional phrase), because the act of seeking help or accompaniment is only actualized by uttering 'Bismillah'. This answers an objection: the prepositional phrase alone is not a complete sentence, but it functions like one in meaning.

Classifying Propositions: Shakhsiyya, Kulliyya, Juz'iyya
Bajuri classifies propositions based on quantifier applied to the basmala example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given proposition as shakhsiyya, kulliyya, or juz'iyya based on its subject and quantifier, using the basmala example as a reference.

Core Idea
A proposition's type is determined by its subject: individual or universal, quantified or not.

In Islamic logic, propositions (qadiyya) are classified by the nature of their subject and the quantifier (sawr) attached to it. This lesson unpacks Bajuri's classification using the well-known basmala sentence as a case study. By understanding these types, you gain a precise tool for analyzing statements in theology, law, and other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify declarative vs. performative
First, determine if the sentence is declarative (khabariyya) or performative (insha'iyya). Only declarative sentences count as propositions in logic. For the basmala, it can be either.
2
Determine the subject type
If the sentence is declarative, look at its subject. Is it a concrete individual (like 'I' or 'Zayd') or a universal term (like 'believer')? This decides whether it is shakhsiyya (singular) or a candidate for kulliyya/juz'iyya.
3
Check the quantifier
If the subject is universal, see if it has a quantifier. 'Every' (kull) gives kulliyya (universal proposition); 'some' (ba'd) gives juz'iyya (particular proposition). No quantifier makes it indefinite (muhmala), which is a separate category.
4
Apply to the basmala variants
Bajuri gives three estimated verbs for the basmala: 'I begin' (subject = 'I', individual) → shakhsiyya; 'Every believer begins' (subject = 'believer', quantifier 'every') → kulliyya; 'Some believers begin' (subject = 'believer', quantifier 'some') → juz'iyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَدِ اشْتُهِرَ أَنَّ جُمْلَةَ البَسْمَلَةِ يَصِحُ أَنْ تَكُونَ إِنْشَائِيَّةً، وَأَنْ تَكُونَ خَبَرِيَّةً؛ فَعَلَى الْأَوَّلِ لَا تُسَمَّى تِلْكَ الجُمْلَةُ «قَضِيَّةً»؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُسَمَّى بِهَا الإِنْشَاءُ، بَلِ الخَبَرُ فَقَطْ. وَأَمَّا عَلَى الثَّانِي فَتُسَمَّى بِهَا. - ثُمَّ إِنْ قُدِرَ المُتَعَلَّقُ نَحْوُ «ابْتَدِئُ» كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةً شَخْصِيَّةً؛ لِأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا مُشَخَّصٌ مُعَيَّنٌ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ القَضِيَّةِ الشَّخْصِيَّةِ. وَإِنْ قُدِرَ نَحْوُ «يَبْتَدِئُ كُلُّ مُؤْمِنٍ» كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةً كُلِّيَّةً؛ لِأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا كُلِّيٌّ، وَقَدْ سُوِّرَ بِالسُّورِ الكُلِّيِّ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ القَضِيَّةِ الكُلِّيَّةِ. وَإِنْ قُدِرَ نَحْوُ «يَبْتَدِئُ بَعْضُ المُؤْمِنِينَ» كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةً جُزْئِيَّةً؛ لِأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا كُلِّيٌّ، وَقَدْ سُوِّرَ بِالسُّورِ الجُزْئِيِّ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ القَضِيَّةِ الجُزْئِيَّةِ.
It is well-known that the sentence of the basmala can validly be performative or declarative. In the first case, that sentence is not called a proposition, because only the declarative is called by that name, not the performative. As for the second, it is called a proposition. Then, if the implicit verb is estimated as 'I begin', it becomes a particular proposition, because the subject of judgment therein is a specific individual, as is the rule for particular propositions. And if it is estimated as 'Every believer begins', it becomes a universal proposition, because the subject of judgment therein is universal and has been quantified with a universal quantifier, as is the rule for universal propositions. And if it is estimated as 'Some believers begin', it becomes a particular proposition, because the subject of judgment therein is universal and has been quantified with a particular quantifier, as is the rule for particular propositions.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage, from Bajuri's commentary, defines the three types of categorical propositions based on the nature of the subject and its quantifier. It uses the basmala as an example to illustrate how an implied verb can determine the proposition's type. First, it states that the basmala can be considered either a performative utterance (insha') or a declarative statement (khabar). Only declarative statements are called propositions (qadiyya). Then, depending on the implied verb, the proposition is classified: if the implied verb is 'I begin' (with a definite singular implied subject like 'I'), it is a particular (shakhsiyya) proposition because the subject is a specific individual. If the implied verb is 'Every believer begins', it is a universal (kulliyya) proposition because the subject 'believer' is universal and is quantified with the universal quantifier 'every'. If it is 'Some believers begin', it is a particular (juz'iyya) proposition because the subject is universal but quantified with the particular quantifier 'some'. The passage emphasizes the rule that the quantifier must be applied to the subject for the proposition to be classified accordingly. Note: In Islamic logic, propositions are categorized into shakhsiyya (singular), kulliyya (universal), and juz'iyya (particular) based on whether the subject is a concrete individual or a universal term, and if the latter, whether the quantifier is universal or particular.

A proposition's type (shakhsiyya, kulliyya, juz'iyya) is determined by whether its subject is a concrete individual or a universal term with a quantifier.

The Deviated Proposition: Quantifier on the Predicate
Anbabi defines the deviated proposition where the quantifier applies to the predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a deviated proposition (manharifa) and distinguish it from a normal proposition by checking the placement of the quantifier.

Core Idea
A quantifier that strays from the subject creates a deviated proposition.

In Islamic logic, the quantifier (sawr) normally determines the quantity of the proposition by applying to the subject. But what happens when the quantifier attaches to the predicate instead? This lesson explores the deviated proposition (manharifa), a special case that must be recognized to avoid misanalysis in logical reasoning, especially in Kalam and Fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier's proper place
Recall that in a normal categorical proposition, the quantifier (like 'every' or 'some') qualifies the subject. For example, in 'Every Muslim is just', 'every' applies to 'Muslim'.
2
Locate where the quantifier actually applies
In the proposition at hand, read the Arabic text carefully. Check whether the quantifier modifies the subject or the predicate.
If the quantifier applies to the predicate, the proposition is deviated.
3
Classify as deviated (manharifa)
If the quantifier applies to the predicate, label the proposition as manharifa. For example, 'Man is every mortal' has 'every' on 'mortal', so it is deviated.
The term 'manharifa' literally means 'deviated' from the proper place.
4
Understand the logical effect
Deviated propositions are not merely grammatical oddities; they can affect the validity of syllogisms and immediate inferences. Recognizing them prevents errors in logical analysis.
In Islamic sciences, such distinctions ensure precise reasoning in Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومحلُّ كون القضيَّة كلّيّة أو جزئيَّةٌ : إذا كان حكم السُّور مسلّطاً على الموضوع، فإن سُلّط على المحمول سميت: «منحرفة»؛ لانحراف السُّور عن محله، وهو الموضوع.
And the status of the proposition being universal or particular is when the ruling of the quantifier is applied to the subject. If it is applied to the predicate, it is called 'deviated' due to the deviation of the quantifier from its proper place, which is the subject.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage, from Anbabi's super-commentary, defines a special type of proposition called 'manharifa' (deviated). It clarifies that the typical classification of a proposition as universal or particular is based on the quantifier being applied to the subject. For example, 'Every man is mortal' is universal because 'every' qualifies 'man'. However, if the quantifier is applied to the predicate instead, as in 'Man is every mortal' (which is grammatically odd but logically considered), the proposition is called 'manharifa' because the quantifier has strayed from its proper place. This concept is important for distinguishing between normal and deviated forms in categorical propositions, especially when analyzing logical form in Arabic logic. Note: this definition is from the super-commentary, refining the main commentary's classification.

Key Terms
مُنْحَرِفَة (manharifa)
سُور (sawr)
محل (mahall)

Key Takeaway
A deviated proposition (manharifa) occurs when the quantifier applies to the predicate rather than the subject, and recognizing it is essential for correct logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

Universal, Particular, and Indefinite Propositions
Author defines universal, particular, and indefinite propositions with quantifiers and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify a proposition as universal, particular, or indefinite based on its quantifier, and distinguish deviated propositions.

Core Idea
The quantifier on the subject determines a proposition's quantity: universal, particular, or none.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by the quantity of the subject. The commentator Bannani explains how quantifiers like 'kull' (every) and 'ba'd' (some) define universal and particular propositions, while the absence of a quantifier yields an indefinite proposition. He also warns against deviated propositions where the quantifier is misplaced. This lesson will help you read and analyze propositions in theological and juristic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject and predicate
Locate the term being judged (subject) and what is judged about it (predicate) in the proposition.
The subject is the first part of the proposition; the predicate is the second.
2
Check for a quantifier on the subject
Look for words like 'kull', 'ba'd', or their equivalents that modify the subject. If none, the proposition is indefinite.
Quantifiers are called 'sawr' in Arabic.
3
Classify by quantifier type
If the quantifier is universal (e.g., kull, jami', al-istighraqiyya for affirmatives; la shay', la wahida for negatives), the proposition is universal. If particular (e.g., ba'd, wahid for affirmatives; laysa ba'd, ba'd laysa, laysa kull for negatives), it is particular.
The passage lists examples but says the list is not exhaustive.
4
Handle the indefinite case
If no quantifier appears, the proposition is indefinite (muhmala). The subject is universal but unquantified.
Example: 'The believer begins' without 'every' or 'some'.
5
Identify deviated propositions
If the quantifier governs the predicate instead of the subject, the proposition is deviated (munharifa) and is not classified as universal or particular in the standard sense.
This deviation is considered a logical mistake.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قُدِّرَ نَحْوُ: "يَبْتَدِئُ كُلُّ مُؤْمِنٍ" كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا كُلِّيٌّ، وَقَدْ سُوِّرَ بِالسُّورِ الْكُلِّيِّ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ الْقَضِيَّةِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ. وَقَدْ سُوِّرَ بِالسُّورِ الْكُلِّيِّ: هُوَ فِي الْكُلِّيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ: "كُلُّ" وَ "آلُ" الِاسْتِغْرَاقِيَّةِ، وَفِي السَّالِبَةِ: "لَا شَيْءَ" و "لَا وَاحِدَةَ". وَهُوَ غَيْرُ حَاصِرٍ؛ إِذْ مَثَلُ "كُلُّ": "جَمِيعُ" وَ "عَامَّةُ" وَنَحْوُهُمَا. وَإِنْ قُدِّرَ نَحْوُ: "يَبْتَدِئُ بَعْضُ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ" كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةٌ جُزْئِيَّةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا كُلِّيٌّ، وَقَدْ سُوِّرَ بِالسُّورِ الْجُزْئِيِّ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ الْقَضِيَّةِ الْجُزْئِيَّةِ. وَالسُّورُ الْجُزْئِيُّ فِي الْمُوجَبَةِ: "بَعْضُ" وَ "وَاحِدُ" وَنَحْوَ ذَلِكَ، وَفِي السَّالِبَةِ: "لَيْسَ بَعْضُ" و "بَعْضُ لَيْسَ" و "لَيْسَ كُلُّ". وَمَحَلُّ كَوْنِ الْقَضِيَّةِ كُلِّيَّةً أَوْ جُزْئِيَّةً: إِذَا كَانَ حُكْمُ السُّورِ مُسَلَّطًا عَلَى الْمَوْضُوعِ، فَإِنْ سُلِّطَ عَلَى الْمَحْمُولِ سُمِّيَتْ مُنْحَرِفَةً؛ لِانْحِرَافِ السُّورِ عَنْ مَحَلِّهِ، وَهُوَ الْمَوْضُوعُ. وَإِنْ قُدِّرَ نَحْوُ: "يَبْتَدِئُ الْمُؤْمِنُ" بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ وَالْجُزْئِيَّةِ كَانَتْ قَضِيَّةٌ مُهْمَلَةٌ؛ لِأَنَّ الْمَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا كُلِّيٌّ، وَقَدْ أُهْمِلَ عَنِ اعْتِبَارِ الْكُلِّيَّةِ وَالْجُزْئِيَّةِ، كَمَا هُوَ ضَابِطُ الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُهْمَلَةِ.
And if it is estimated like 'Every believer begins' then it is a universal proposition, because the subject is universal and it has been quantified with the universal quantifier, as is the condition of the universal proposition. The universal quantifier in the affirmative universal is 'kull' and 'al' for totality, and in the negative universal: 'la shay' and 'la wahida'. And it is not exhaustive; for like 'kull' are 'jami'' and 'ammah' and their likes. And if it is estimated like 'Some believers begin' then it is a particular proposition, because the subject is universal and it has been quantified with the particular quantifier, as is the condition of the particular proposition. The particular quantifier in the affirmative is 'ba'd' and 'wahid' and similar, and in the negative: 'laysa ba'd' and 'ba'd laysa' and 'laysa kull'. And the place of the proposition being universal or particular is when the ruling of the quantifier is applied to the subject; if it is applied to the predicate, it is called 'munharifa' (deviated) because of the deviation of the quantifier from its place, which is the subject. And if it is estimated like 'The believer begins' disregarding universality and particularity, then it is an indefinite proposition, because the subject is universal and it has been ignored with regard to consideration of universality and particularity, as is the condition of the indefinite proposition.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of quantified propositions: universal, particular, and indefinite. The author begins by stating that if the estimated proposition is 'Every believer begins' (with 'kull'), then it is a universal proposition because the subject (believer) is universal and is quantified with a universal quantifier. The universal quantifier in the affirmative is 'kull' and 'al' for totality, and in the negative it is 'la shay' and 'la wahida'. However, the list is not exhaustive; words like 'jami'' and 'ammah' also serve. Then the author defines the particular proposition with the example 'Some believers begin', where the subject is still universal but quantified with a particular quantifier. The particular quantifier in the affirmative is 'ba'd' and 'wahid', and in the negative it is 'laysa ba'd', 'ba'd laysa', and 'laysa kull'. The author also notes that a proposition is called universal or particular only if the quantifier governs the subject; if it governs the predicate, it is called 'munharifa' (deviated). Finally, the indefinite proposition is defined with the example 'The believer begins' (without a quantifier), where the subject is universal but not quantified. The key point is that all these propositions have a universal subject; the difference lies in the presence and type of quantifier.

Key Terms
قضية كلية
قضية جزئية
قضية مهملة
سور
منحرفة

Key Takeaway
The quantity of a proposition is determined solely by the quantifier on its subject: universal (kull), particular (ba'd), or none (indefinite).
