Determining Quantifiers from the Preposition 'Ba'
Author explains how 'ba' interpretation determines quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a proposition with the preposition 'ba' is universal, particular, or indefinite based on the interpretation of 'ba' as 'ahd, istighraq, or jins.

Core Idea
The quantifier of a proposition depends on how the preposition 'ba' is interpreted.

In Islamic logic, the quantifier of a proposition is not always self-evident; grammatical particles can shift meaning. This lesson examines the phrase 'yabtadi'u bi-llah' to see how three interpretations of 'ba'—'ahd, istighraq, and jins—lead to universal, particular, or indefinite propositions. Mastering this analysis sharpens your ability to handle similar phrases in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target phrase
The phrase under analysis is 'yabtadi'u bi-llah' (يبتدئ بالله). Note the preposition 'ba' attached to the word 'Allah'.
This phrase is a standard example in Mantiq textbooks.
2
Review the three interpretations of 'ba'
In Arabic, the preposition 'ba' can be interpreted as: (a) al-'ahd (العهد) – referring to a specific, known entity; (b) al-istighraq (الاستغراق) – indicating total inclusion of all individuals; (c) al-jins (الجنس) – referring to the genus in the sense of 'some' (ba'd).
These are the key logical possibilities discussed in the source.
3
Map each interpretation to a quantifier
Interpretation as 'ahd yields a universal proposition ('the first'), istighraq yields a particular proposition ('the second'), and jins yields an indefinite proposition ('the third').
The terms 'first', 'second', and 'third' refer to the list of three quantifier types mentioned earlier in the lesson.
4
Consider the grammatical status of 'ba'
If 'ba' is an original preposition (harf jar asli), both the object-of-verb (مُتَعَلَّق) and the annexation (إضافة) contribute possibilities, yielding 16 total. If 'ba' is redundant (za'id), only the annexation considerations apply, giving 4 possibilities.
This multiplication of possibilities is noted by al-Anbabi in the passage.
5
Apply the analysis to another phrase
Practice by analyzing 'bismillah' (بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم). Determine possible interpretations of 'ba' and the resulting quantifiers.
This reinforces the logical procedure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكَمَا يَصِحُ اعْتِبَارُ هَذِهِ الاحْتِمَالَاتِ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْمُتَعَلَّقِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى الْمَشْهُورِ مِنْ أَنَّ الْبَاءَ حَرْفُ جَرٍّ أَصْلِيٌّ، يَصِحُ اعْتِبَارُهَا بِاعْتِبَارِ إِضَافَةِ الِاسْمِ إِلَى لَفْظِ الْجَلَالَةِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى مُقَابِلِ الْمَشْهُورِ مِنْ أَنَّ الْبَاءَ حَرْفُ جَرٍّ زَائِدٌ: فَإِنْ جُعِلَتْ لِلْعَهْدِ، فَالْأَوَّلُ. وَإِنْ جُعِلَتْ لِلِاسْتِغْرَاقِ، فَالثَّانِي. وَإِنْ جُعِلَتْ لِلْجِنْسِ فِي ضِمْنِ الْبَعْضِ، فَالثَّالِثُ. ثُمَّ قَالَ الْأَنْبَابِي: وَعَلَى هَذَا فَتَصِيرُ الِاحْتِمَالَاتُ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ؛ حَاصِلَةٌ مِنْ ضَرْبِ أَرْبَعَةِ الْمُتَعَلَّقِ فِي أَرْبَعَةِ الْإِضَافَةِ إِنْ جُعِلَ الْحَرْفُ أَصْلِيًّا، وَإِلَّا فَهِيَ أَرْبَعَةٌ فَقَطْ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْإِضَافَةِ.
And just as it is valid to consider these possibilities regarding the object of the verb, based on the well-known view that the 'ba' is an original preposition, it is valid to consider them by considering the annexation of the noun to the word of majesty, based on the opposite of the well-known view that the 'ba' is a redundant preposition: if it is made for 'ahd (specific reference), then the first (universal); if it is made for istighraq (total inclusion), then the second (particular); if it is made for jins (genus) in the sense of ba'd, then the third (indefinite). Then al-Anbabi said: And on this, the possibilities become sixteen, resulting from multiplying the four concerning the object by the four concerning the annexation if the letter is original, otherwise they are only four by considering the annexation.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage explains how the interpretation of the preposition 'ba' in the phrase 'yabtadi'u bi-llah' can affect the quantifier classification. The author states that just as the possibilities (universal, particular, indefinite) can be considered based on the object of the verb (the 'ba' phrase) on the well-known view that 'ba' is an original preposition, they can also be considered based on the annexation of the noun to 'Allah' (Allah's name) on the contrary view that 'ba' is redundant. Specifically, if the 'ba' is taken for 'ahd (specific reference), then the first possibility (universal) results; if for istighraq (totality), then the second (particular); if for jins in the sense of ba'd (genus implying some), then the third (indefinite). The comment by al-Anbabi clarifies that when considering both the object and the annexation, the possibilities multiply: with the 'ba' as original, there are 4 possibilities from the object and 4 from annexation, yielding 16; if the 'ba' is redundant, only the annexation considerations apply, giving 4 possibilities.

العهد
الاستغراق
الجنس
حرف جر أصلي
حرف جر زائد

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of the preposition 'ba' as 'ahd, istighraq, or jins directly determines whether the proposition is universal, particular, or indefinite.

The Natural Proposition: Definition and the Basmala Case
Author defines the natural proposition and argues the Basmala is not one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a natural proposition and determine whether a given statement qualifies as one, using the Basmala as a case study.

Core Idea
A natural proposition judges the genus itself, disregarding individuals; the Basmala fails this test.

Among the divisions of propositions in logic is the natural proposition, which involves a judgment on the nature or genus, not on individuals. In this passage, the author clearly defines it with the example 'Man is better than woman,' and then examines whether the Basmala could be considered a natural proposition — a question with implications for how we understand the foundational statement of Islamic practice.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Identify the defining property of a natural proposition: it is a judgment made upon the genus and nature, disregarding individuals.
2
Analyse the example
See how the example 'Man is better than woman' exemplifies the definition: it compares the genera, not specific individuals.
The author notes that sometimes individual women may be better than many men, so the judgment is on the essence.
3
Test the Basmala
Apply the definition to the Basmala sentence. Consider the two possible grammatical interpretations: the object of the preposition (al-muta'allaq) and the annexation of 'the name' to 'Allah'.
4
Evaluate the reasoning
Understand why neither interpretation allows the Basmala to be a natural proposition: the intended subject (e.g., 'the believer' or 'the name') must be capable of initiation (ibtida'), but a genus or nature cannot initiate an action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقي من أقسام القضايا : القضية الطبيعية ، وهي : ما حكم فيها على الجنس والطبيعة، بقطع النظر عن الأفراد؛ كأن تقول: «الرجل خير من المرأة» ، فإن المراد : أن جنس الرجل وطبيعته خير من جنس المرأة وطبيعتها بقطع النظر عن الأفراد فيهما ، وإلا فقد يتفق أن بعض أفراد المرأة خير من كثير من أفراد الرجل. ولا يصح أن تكون جملة البسملة منها؛ لا باعتبار المتعلق، ولا باعتبار إضافة الاسم إلى لفظ الجلالة؛ إذ لا يصح أن يراد من المؤمن مثلاً الجنس والطبيعة بقطع النظر عن الأفراد؛ لأنه لا يقع منه ابتداء، ولا يصح أن يراد من الاسم الجنس والطبيعة كذلك؛ لأنه لا يقع به ابتداء. وسيأتي إيضاح ذلك إن شاء الله تعالى. والكلام على البسملة كثير وشهير، فلا نطيل بذكره.
Remains from the divisions of propositions: the natural proposition, which is: that in which judgment is made upon the genus and nature, disregarding individuals; as when you say: 'The man is better than the woman,' for the intent is that the genus of man and his nature is better than the genus of woman and her nature, disregarding individuals in both; otherwise it may happen that some individuals of the woman are better than many individuals of the man. And it is not valid for the Basmala sentence to be among them; neither with respect to the object of the preposition, nor with respect to the annexation of 'the name' to 'the word of majesty'; because it is not valid that, for example, from 'the believer' one intends the genus and nature disregarding individuals — since initiation does not occur from him — nor is it valid that from 'the name' one intends the genus and nature in such a way — since initiation does not occur with it. And clarification of that will come, God willing. And the discussion about the Basmala is extensive and famous, so we do not prolong its mention.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the natural proposition (al-qadiyya al-tabi'iyya) as one where the judgment applies to the genus or nature itself, without considering any individual. The example 'Man is better than woman' illustrates a generic judgment, not a universal one, because it compares the essences, not individual instances. The author then tests whether the Basmala could be a natural proposition. He argues it cannot, considering two interpretations of the Basmala's grammatical structure: either the object of the preposition (al-muta'allaq) or the annexation of 'name' to 'Allah'. In either case, the intended subject (e.g., 'the believer' or 'the name') must be capable of initiating an action (ibtida'), but a genus or nature cannot initiate. Therefore the Basmala is not a natural proposition. The author promises further clarification later and notes the well-known discussion on the Basmala is too extensive to repeat here.

Key Terms

Key Takeaway

Ordering Proposition Types by Proximity
Annotation classifies four proposition types in order of proximity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to order the four proposition types (shakhsiyya, juz'iyya, muhmala, kulliyya) by their closeness to intended meaning and explain the rationale behind the order.

Core Idea
Personal propositions are closest; universal propositions are farthest.

This short annotation establishes a hierarchy among proposition types based on precision. By understanding why personal propositions are considered closest to the intended meaning, you gain a tool for evaluating the scope of statements in Islamic texts. Let's unpack each type and the logic of their ordering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposition types
From the key terms, learn the Arabic names and definitions: shakhsiyya (personal), juz'iyya (particular), muhmala (indefinite), kulliyya (universal). Understand each type's scope.
Personal refers to a single individual; particular to some individuals; indefinite lacks a quantifier; universal covers all.
2
Internalize the ordering
The passage states: 'the first of them is closest, followed by the second, then the third, then the fourth.' Memorize the sequence: personal (1st), particular (2nd), indefinite (3rd), universal (4th).
3
Explain the rationale
The order is based on precision: personal is most precise (single referent), particular is less precise (some referents), indefinite is ambiguous (could be universal or particular), universal is least precise (all referents without distinction).
In interpretation, a personal proposition leaves the least ambiguity about its subject.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Given a statement from Qur'an or Hadith, classify its proposition type and note what the ordering implies. For instance, 'Zaid is standing' (personal) is more precise than 'Some believers are standing' (particular).
This skill aids in Tafsir and Fiqh when determining the scope of a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ بَعْضَ هَذِهِ الاحْتِمَالَاتِ أَقْرَبُ مِنْ بَعْضٍ. قوله : (أَقْرَبُ مِنْ بَعْضٍ) فأوَّلها أقرب، ويليه الثَّاني، ثمَّ الثَّالث، ثمَّ الرَّابع ؛ كذا قيل .
And it is not hidden that some of these possibilities are closer than others. His statement: (closer than others) — so the first of them is closest, followed by the second, then the third, then the fourth; thus it is said.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a statement that some possibilities (propositions) are closer to the intended meaning than others. It specifies the order of proximity: the first (personal/shakhsiyya) is closest, then the second (particular/juz'iyya), then the third (indefinite/muhmala), then the fourth (universal/kulliyya). This ordering is based on how restrictive the proposition is: personal refers to one specific individual, particular to some part of the universal, indefinite to an unspecified quantity, and universal to all individuals. Thus personal is the most precise, hence closest.

Key Terms
شخصية
جزئية
مهملة
كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Personal (shakhsiyya)
B) Particular (juz'iyya)
C) Indefinite (muhmala)
D) Universal (kulliyya)

Key Takeaway
The personal proposition is the most precise and closest to intended meaning; the universal is the broadest and least precise in this interpretive ordering.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Division of Utterance into Request, Report, and Alert
Author divides utterance into request, report, and emotional expressions; then enumerates three types of request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any Arabic utterance as talab, khabar, or tanbih based on its intrinsic coinage, and explain why context-dependent requests are excluded.

Core Idea
Utterances are divided by coinage, not by context of use.

In this passage, the logicians establish a fundamental classification of utterances in use. They distinguish three types: request (talab), report (khabar), and alert (tanbih). This classification is crucial because the primary object of logic is the report (khabar), which alone admits truth and falsehood. The commentator clarifies that requests and alerts are studied in other disciplines, and adds important nuances about what counts as a genuine request versus a contextual implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions of utterance
The author states that utterance is either request (talab), report (khabar), or alert (tanbih). The commentator clarifies that 'alert' covers expressions like wish, hope, and call. Locate these three in the Arabic text.
The division is based on the intrinsic function of the word, not on situational use.
2
Analyze the defining characteristics of each division
Talab: indicates a psychic request (e.g., commands and prohibitions). Khabar: admits truth and falsehood (the focus of logic). Tanbih: indicates an emotional state that necessarily implies a request (e.g., wish, hope, call). Note that logicians prioritize khabar because truth evaluation is central.
Tanbih words are coined for a psychological quality that entails request.
3
Examine the objection regarding indirect requests
The passage addresses the example 'I am thirsty' said to someone with water. Why is it not considered a genuine talab? Because its requestive meaning is derived from the context (qarinat al-maqam), not from the word's intrinsic coinage. This highlights that classification is based on the word's original coinage, not on speaker intention.
Other examples: 'Can you pass the salt?' implies a request but is not a direct imperative form.
4
Understand that talab includes both command and prohibition
The commentator says talab includes 'request for action' (command) and 'request for cessation' (prohibition). This is important because some might think talab only means command. In logic and usul al-fiqh, both are treated as requests.
Prohibition is considered a request for cessation, not a report.

قوله : (مَا دَلَّ عَلَى تَمَنٍ أَوْ تَرَجٍ) أي: فإنَّ اللَّفظ الدال على ذلك موضوع لكيفية يلزمها الطلب، وهي: ميل النفس.

His statement: (and the word) i.e., the customary word, and it is the word in use. And his statement: (either a request or a report) i.e., or an alert. (1) The first: 'what indicates a psychic request.' (2) The second: 'what admits truth and falsehood.' (3) The third: 'what indicates a wish, or a hope, or the like.' And there is no objection to the first based on your saying to someone who has water: 'I am thirsty' and the like, because its indication of request is not by its essence, but by the context of the situation. His statement: (and the first has three types... etc.) It is not hidden that the first in his speech is the request, and it includes: request for action like 'Hit!' and request for cessation like 'Do not hit!'. The apparent context of the author is that this division applies to each of them, but his saying 'command' may prevent that. Al-Anbabi: His statement: (what indicates a wish or a hope) i.e., the word indicating that is coined for a quality that entails request, which is inclination of the soul. And his statement: (or the like) i.e., like a call, for it is coined for a quality that entails request, which is the desire for approach. His statement: (and there is no objection to the first... etc.) The summary of the objection: that request includes 'I am thirsty', even though it is not called a 'command' nor a 'supplication' nor an 'imploration', whereas he restricted request to that. His statement: (not by its essence) i.e., not by virtue of its coinage.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage classifies utterances (alfaz) in use. The author first notes that the word here means the customary, actually used word. Then he divides utterance into three: (1) talab (request), which indicates a psychic request (a desire in the soul), (2) khabar (report), which can be true or false, and (3) tanbih (alert), which covers emotional expressions like wish (tamanni), hope (tarajji), and similar constructions like calls (nida'). The commentator clarifies that talab includes both commands to do (amr) and prohibitions (nahy). An important nuance is that statements like 'I am thirsty' are not considered a direct request because their requestive force comes from context (qarinat al-maqam), not from the word's intrinsic coinage. Al-Anbabi adds that words for wish, hope, and call are coined for a psychological state that necessarily implies a request (e.g., a call implies desire for approach). The objection considered is that 'I am thirsty' might be seen as a request, but it is excluded because it is not a direct verbal form of request. This division is foundational for understanding how language functions in logical discourse, as logicians primarily focus on reports (khabar) for truth evaluation, while requests are studied in other disciplines.

خبر
تنبيه

D) Because it is a call.

Key Takeaway
Utterances are classified by their intrinsic coinage: talab for psychic request, khabar for truth-apt reports, and tanbih for emotional states entailing request; context alone does not change category.

Classification of Utterances into Talab and Khabar
Bajuri classifies utterances into talab and khabar, then defines three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify utterances as talab (request) or khabar (statement) and identify the three subtypes of talab as defined by Bajuri.

Core Idea
Utterances are either demands or statements; demands include commands, prohibitions, and even wishes.

In Islamic logic, utterances are categorized by their relation to truth and demand. This lesson from Bajuri's commentary presents a classification that distinguishes between requests (talab) and statements (khabar), and then further divides requests into three types, including a surprising inclusion of truth-apt utterances under request. Understanding this classification is foundational for analyzing legal and theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial division
The passage states that utterances are either talab (request) or khabar (statement). Ask: does the utterance demand something, or does it assert a fact that can be true or false?
This division is the first level of classification.
2
Recognize the three subtypes of talab
The first type indicates a psychological request (الطلب النفسي), i.e., a direct command or prohibition. The second type is what can be true or false (مَا احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ وَالْكَذِبَ) – this seems like a statement but is included under talab. The third type expresses a wish or hope (تَمَنٍّ أو تَرَجٍّ).
This grouping is specific to Bajuri's commentary and may not be standard in all logic texts.
3
Analyze the objection about indirect requests
The objection: 'I am thirsty' seems to be a request but is not a command. Bajuri answers that it indicates a request only through context (قرينة المقام), not by its intrinsic wording.
This shows that not every utterance that implies a demand is a talab of the first type.
4
Apply the classification to examples
Practice with examples from Islamic legal texts. Determine whether each utterance is talab or khabar, and if talab, which subtype.
Command: 'اقْتُلُوا الْمُشْرِكِينَ' (Kill the polytheists) is talab first type. Statement: 'زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ' (Zaid is standing) is khabar. Wish: 'لَيْتَ الشَّبَابَ يَعُودُ' (Would that youth returns) is talab third type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٣٥) وَاللَّفْظُ : إِمَّا طَلَبٌ أَوْ خَبَرُ وَأَوَّلٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ وَقَوْلُهُ : (إِمَّا طَلَبٌ أَوْ خَبَرُ) أَيْ : أَوْ تَنْبِيه . وَالأَوَّلُ: مَا دَلَّ عَلَى الطَّلَبِ النَّفْسِي . وَالثَّانِي: مَا احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ وَالكَذِبَ. وَالثَّالِثُ : مَا دَلَ عَلَى تَمَنٍ، أَوْ تَرَجٍ، أَوْ نَحْوِ ذَلِكَ. وَلَا يَرِدُ عَلَى الأَوَّلِ : قَوْلُكَ لِمَنْ مَعَهُ مَاءٌ : «أَنَا عَطْشَانُ وَنَحْوُهُ، لأَنَّ دَلَالَتَهُ عَلَى الطَّلَب لَيْسَتْ بِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ بِقَرِينَةِ المَقَامِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَأَوَّلٌ ثَلَاثَةٌ ... إلخ) لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ الأَوَّلَ فِي كَلَامِهِ هُوَ : الطَّلَبُ، وَهُوَ يَشْمَلُ : - طَلَبَ الفِعْلِ كَ: «اضْرِبْ». - وَطَلَبَ التَّرْكِ كَ: «لَا تَضْرِبْ». وَظَاهِرُ سَيَاقِ المُصَنِّفِ : أَنَّ هَذَا التَّقْسِيمَ جَارٍ فِي كُلِّ مِنْهُمَا .
Utterance: either a request or a statement, and the first is three. His saying: 'either a request or a statement' meaning: or an exclamation. The first: what indicates the psychological request. The second: what admits truth and falsehood. The third: what indicates a wish, or a hope, or the like. And the objection to the first does not apply: your saying to someone who has water: 'I am thirsty' and the like, because its indication of the request is not by itself, but by the context of the situation. His saying: 'and the first is three ... etc.' It is not hidden that the first in his speech is the request, and it includes: requesting an action like 'Hit!' and requesting omission like 'Do not hit!' And the apparent context of the author is that this division applies to each of them.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on a matn line. It opens by stating that an utterance is either a request (talab) or a statement (khabar). The first (talab) is then divided into three types. Bajuri explains: the first type is what indicates a psychological request, meaning a demand that originates in the soul. The second type is what can be true or false, i.e., a declarative statement. The third type expresses a wish or hope, like 'Would that...' or 'Perhaps...'. Then a potential objection is raised: the utterance 'I am thirsty' seems to be a request, yet it does not fall into the first type because it is not a command. Bajuri answers that such an utterance only indicates a request indirectly, through contextual clues, not by its own wording. He further clarifies that the first type (request) includes both commands to act and prohibitions to refrain, and the commentary notes that the author's division applies to both.

Key Terms
اللفظ (al-lafẓ): An utterance, linguistic expression, considered as a unit of speech. [Note: In logic, lafẓ typically refers to meaningful expression, not mere sound.]
طلب (ṭalab): A request or demand, including both command and prohibition. [Note: In later works, talab may be divided into wājib etc., but here it is a broader term.]
خبر (khabar): A statement that can be true or false, a proposition. [Note: Stable term; in logic it corresponds to the proposition.]
الطلب النفسي (al-ṭalab al-nafsī): Psychological request: a demand that originates in the soul, not necessarily expressed directly. [Note: This term is specific to this commentary; not common in classical logic texts.]

Key Takeaway
Utterances are either requests or statements; requests include commands, prohibitions, and wishes, but indirect requests rely on context.

Understanding Amr, Du'a, and Iltimas: The Role of Superiority in Commands
Bajuri's expansion of the matn line defining command, supplication, and entreaty, focusing on the condition of superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of request based on status and evaluate the differing views on the condition of superiority in commands.

Core Idea
Superiority (isti'la') is not a necessary condition for a command; the stronger view allows any imperative utterance to be a command.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary unpacks a seemingly simple definition of amr, du'a, and iltimas. At first glance, the distinction is about status: a command is from a superior, a supplication from an inferior, and an entreaty between equals. But what does 'superiority' actually mean? Must it be real, or merely shown? Or is it not required at all? Bajuri dissects the condition of isti'la' in the matn and presents four views, ultimately favouring the one that requires nothing. This lesson will guide you through those views and their implications for defining commands in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of request in the matn
The matn states: amr with superiority, its opposite du'a, and in equality iltimas. Note that the key distinction is the status relationship.
2
Examine the two possible meanings of isti'la'
Bajuri explains that 'with superiority' (ma'a isti'la') can mean either with 'showing superiority' (if the sin and nun are for request meaning 'showing') or with 'actual superiority' (if those letters are extra). This yields two starting points.
3
Trace the three positions derived from these meanings
Combining the two meanings gives three views: (1) both actual and shown superiority required, (2) only showing superiority required, (3) only actual superiority required. Identify each in the commentary.
4
Note the fourth, stronger position
Bajuri states that a fourth view—that nothing is required—is the rajih (stronger). This means the condition of superiority is not necessary for a command.
5
Apply the same analysis to du'a and iltimas
The same three views (and the stronger fourth) apply to supplication and entreaty as well. The stronger view is that no condition of superiority is required for them either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٣٦) أَمْرٌ مَعَ اسْتِعْلَا، وَعَكْسُهُ: دُعَا وَفِي التَّسَاوِي فَ: الْتِمَاسُ وَقَعَا. قَوْلُهُ: «أَمْرُ مَعَ اسْتِعْلَا أَيْ: مَعَ إِظْهَارِ العُلُوِّ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ السِّيْنَ وَالنَّاءَ لِلطَّلَبِ؛ بِمَعْنَى الإِظْهَارِ، أَوْ : مَعَ العُلُوِّ؛ بِنَاءٌ عَلَى أَنَّهُمَا زَائِدَتَانِ. وَعَلَى الأَوَّلِ يَكُونُ المُصَنِّفُ قَدْ جَرَى عَلَى القَوْلِ بِاشْتِرَاطِ العُلُوِّ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ مَعَ إِظْهَارِهِ، وَيَحْتَمِلُ أَنْ يَكُونَ جَارِياً عَلَى القَوْلِ بِاشْتِرَاطِ إِظْهَارِ العُلُوِّ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ عَالِياً فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ. وَعَلَى الثَّانِي يَكُونُ قَدْ جَرَى عَلَى القَوْلِ بِاشْتِرَاطِ العُلُوِّ فِي نَفْسِ الْأَمْرِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُظْهِرْهُ. فَتَلَخَّصَ أَنَّ كَلَامَ المُصَنِّفِ مُحْتَمِلٌ لِثَلَاثَةِ أَقْوَالٍ وَبَقِيَ رَابِعٌ وَهُوَ القَوْلُ بِأَنَّهُ لَا يُشْتَرَطُ شَيْءٌ مِنْ ذَلِكَ، وَهَذَا القَوْلُ هُوَ الرَّاجِحُ، فَمَا جَرَى عَلَيْهِ المُصَنِّفُ طَرِيقَةٌ مَرْجُوحَةٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَعَكْسُهُ: دُعَا) يَجْرِي فِيهِ الأَقْوَالُ المَذْكُورَةُ فِيمَا مَرَّ، وَالرَّاجِحُ عَدَمُ اشْتِرَاطِ شَيْءٍ. وَهَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِي قَوْلِهِ : وَفِي التَّسَاوِي ... إلخ. قَوْلُهُ : (فَ: الْتِمَاسُ) وَقَعَا الفَاءُ فِيهِ زَائِدَةٌ، وَالأَلِفُ لِلإِطْلَاقِ.
(36) Command with superiority, and its opposite is supplication, and in equality it is entreaty. His saying: 'Command with superiority' meaning: with showing superiority, based on that the seen and the nun are for request, meaning showing, or: with superiority, based on that they are extra. On the first, the author follows the saying of requiring superiority in the command itself with showing it, and it is possible he follows the saying of requiring showing superiority even if he is not superior in actuality. On the second, he follows the saying of requiring superiority in actuality even if he does not show it. So it is concluded that the author's speech is subject to three views, and a fourth remains, which is the view that nothing of that is required, and this view is the stronger, so what the author followed is a weaker way. His saying: 'and its opposite is supplication' – the mentioned views apply to it, and the stronger is not requiring anything. And similarly it is said regarding his saying: 'and in equality ... etc.' His saying: 'fa: iltimās' – the fa' is extra, and the alif is for absolute.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on a matn line that distinguishes three types of request based on status. The matn states: 'Amr (command) is with superiority (isti'la'), and its opposite is du'a (supplication), and in equality it is iltias (entreaty).' Bajuri unpacks the condition of superiority: 'isti'la' can mean either 'showing superiority' (with the sin and nun interpreted as letters of request meaning 'showing') or 'being superior in fact' (with those letters as extra). This yields three possible interpretations: requiring both actual superiority and its showing; requiring only showing, even if not actual; requiring only actual, even if not shown. A fourth view, which Bajuri says is the stronger (rajih), requires nothing—no condition at all. He then applies the same analysis to du'a and iltias, concluding that the stronger view is that no condition is required for them either. Finally, he notes a grammatical point in the matn: the 'fa' in 'fa iltias' is extra, and the alif is for absolute form (not indicating a specific noun).

Key Terms
أمر
دعاء
التماس
استعلاء
طلب

B) The speaker must show superiority.
C) Nothing is required beyond the imperative form.
D) The listener must be inferior.

Key Takeaway
The condition of superiority in commands is not universally accepted; the stronger position is that the imperative form alone constitutes a command.

Definition of Kulliyya (Universal Proposition)
Al-Bajuri defines the universal affirmative (kulliyya) as a judgment on each individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a universal proposition (kulliyya) by recognizing its distributive judgment on every individual of the subject.

Core Idea
A universal proposition judges each individual, not the group.

Al-Bajuri opens the passage by defining the universal proposition (kulliyya) as a judgment made on every individual of the subject. This definition is foundational for understanding quantifiers in logical analysis, especially in Islamic textual interpretation. We will break down this definition and apply it to a Quranic example.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of kulliyya
Find the line 'وَحَيْتُمَا لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ حُكِمَا فَإِنَّهُ كُلِّيَّةٌ'. This is the core definition: whenever a judgment applies to every individual (kulli fard), it is a kulliyya.
The term 'kulliyya' here refers to a universal proposition, not a term.
2
Analyze the components
The definition has two parts: the judgment (hukm) and the distributive quantifier (kulli fard). The judgment must attach to each individual separately, not to the aggregate.
This distinguishes kulliyya from a collective judgment (e.g., 'the class is studying').
3
Test with a Quranic example
Apply the definition to 'كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ' (Every soul shall taste death). Does this judge each soul individually? Yes, it is a universal affirmative.
The word 'كُلُّ' (every) directly indicates distributive scope.
4
Distinguish from other proposition types
Contrast with a particular proposition like 'بَعْضُ النَّاسِ كَرِيمٌ' (Some people are generous) which does not cover every individual.
Also different from a whole-part judgment like 'الإنسان حيوان' which predicates the universal of the species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٣٨) وَحَيْتُمَا لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ حُكِمَا فَإِنَّهُ: «كُلِّيَّةٌ» قَدْ عُلِمَا
وَأَيْضاً المُقَرَّرُ أَنَّ السُّؤَالَ بِـ «أَمْ» لِطَلَبِ تَعْيِينِ أَحَدِ الأَمْرَيْنِ المُعْتَقَدِ ثُبُوتُ أَحَدِهِمَا، وَجَوَابُ ذَلِكَ إِمَّا بِالتَّعْيِينِ أَوْ بِنَفْي كُلِّ مِنَ الأَمْرَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ، لَا بِنَفْيِ المَجْمُوعِ، وَلَيْسَ فِي الحَدِيثِ تَعْيِينٌ، فَوَجَبَ أَنْ يَكُونَ نَفْياً لِكُلِّ مِنْهُمَا، وَيُؤَيِّدُ مَا ذُكِرَ مَا هُوَ القَاعِدَةُ - وَإِنْ كَانَتْ أَغْلَبِيَّةٌ - مِنْ «أَنَّ تَاخُرَ النَّفْيِ عَنْ أَدَاةِ التَّعْمِيمِ لِعُمُومِ السَّلْبِ»، بِخِلَافِ تَقَدُّمِهَا عَلَيْهَا. هَذَا؛ وَقَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ: البَحْثُ فِي المَثَلِ لَيْسَ مِنْ دَأْبِ الفُحُولِ، وَيَنْبَغِي أَنَّ مَحَلَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ يَتَرَتَّبْ عَلَى التَّمْثِيلِ ارْتِكَابُ خِلَافِ الوَاقِعِ فِي كَلَامِ اللهِ تَعَالَى أَوْ كَلَامِ رَسُولِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم كَمَا هُنَا، فَاحْفَظْهُ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَحَيْتُمَا لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ ... إلخ) اللَّامُ فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى: عَلَى، وَهِيَ مُتَعَلِّقَةٌ بِقَوْلِهِ: «حُكِمَا»، وَذَلِكَ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٨٥] وَكَمَا فِي الكَلِمَةِ المُشَرَّفَةِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهَا سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ؛ لِعُمُومِ السَّلْبِ فِيهَا لِجَمِيعِ أَفْرَادِ الإِلَهِ غَيْرِ الذَّاتِ العَلِيَّةِ المُسْتَثْنَاةِ اسْتِثْنَاءً مُتَّصِلاً؛ لِدُخُولِ المُسْتَثْنَى فِي المُسْتَثْنَى مِنْهُ بِحَسَبِ الوَضْعِ.
(38) And whenever a judgment is made on every individual, then it is known as 'kulliyya' (universal). And also it is established that the question with 'am' is for requesting the specification of one of two matters when it is believed that one of them is established. And the answer to that is either by specification or by negating each of the two mentioned matters, not by negating the aggregate. And there is no specification in the hadith, so it must be a negation of each of them. And what supports what was mentioned is the principle - even if it is only predominant - that 'the postponement of the negation after the universal quantifier indicates the universality of the negation', contrary to its preposing before it. This; and some said: discussion about the example is not from the practice of the great scholars, and its occasion should be when there does not result from the example a violation of reality in the speech of Allah Most High or the speech of His Messenger (peace be upon him), as is the case here, so memorize it. His saying: (and whenever on every individual judgment is made ... etc.) the lam in it is in the meaning of 'ala (upon), and it is connected to his saying 'hukima' (is judged). And that is as in His statement Most High: 'Every soul shall taste death' (Quran 3:185). And similarly in the honored kalima (shahada), based on it being a universal negative, because of the generality of the negation in it for all individuals of deity other than the Most High essence which is excepted by a continuous exception, because the excepted item enters into the thing from which exception is made according to the linguistic convention.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory lesson from al-Bajuri's commentary, continuing the discussion of universal propositions. It begins by defining 'kulliyya' as a judgment made on each individual (kull fard) of the subject—this is the distributive universal. Then it returns to the hadith analysis: the question with 'am' (as in 'Was the prayer shortened or did you forget?') asks for specification of one of two alternatives when one is believed true. The answer must either specify one or negate each individually, not negate the aggregate. Since the Prophet did not specify one, his reply 'All of that did not happen' must be a negation of each individually (i.e., universal negative). This is supported by a logical rule: when the negation (nafy) comes after the universal quantifier (adatu al-ta'mim), it produces a universal negative (salb kulli); when it precedes, it produces a particular negative. This rule is general, though it acknowledges it is predominant. The author then notes a caution: some scholars say that engaging in such detailed analysis of examples is not the practice of great scholars, unless necessary to avoid attributing falsehood to Allah or His Messenger—so this analysis is justified. Finally, the passage illustrates the rule with the Quranic verse 'Every soul shall taste death' (kullu nafsin dha'iqatu al-mawt) as an example of a universal affirmative (kulliyya), and the shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah' as a universal negative with a continuous exception. The shahada is interpreted as a universal negative (salb kulli), meaning 'No deity exists except Allah', with the exception of Allah. This shows the application of the rule: the negation is after the quantifier (adatu al-ta'mim is implied in 'la ilaha'), yielding a universal negative.

Key Terms
كُلِّيَّة
فرد
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) A judgment that applies to the majority of individuals
B) A judgment that applies to every individual
C) A judgment that applies to no individual
D) A judgment that applies to the group collectively

Key Takeaway
A universal proposition (kulliyya) is defined by its distributive judgment on every individual of the subject.

Propositions
intermediate
7 min
The Rule of Negation Position and Universal Negation
Al-Bajuri explains the negation position rule and applies it to the shahada and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when negation follows a universal quantifier, the result is a universal negative, and when it precedes, it is a particular negative, using Islamic textual examples.

Core Idea
Negation after 'kull' gives universal negation; negation before gives particular negation.

Al-Bajuri introduces a crucial rule of quantification: the position of negation relative to the universal quantifier determines the scope of negation. He applies this rule to the analysis of the hadith 'Ma kana min dhalika shay'' and the shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah'. We will examine this rule and its implications for understanding these sacred texts.

Guided Path
1
Find the statement of the rule
Look for the phrase 'أَنَّ تَاخُرَ النَّفْيِ عَنْ أَدَاةِ التَّعْمِيمِ لِعُمُومِ السَّلْبِ'. This is the principle: postponement of negation after the universal quantifier yields universal negation.
The universal quantifier (adatu al-ta'mim) includes words like 'kull', 'jami', 'kaffah', etc.
2
Contrast the two positions
Al-Bajuri adds 'بِخِلَافِ تَقَدُّمِهَا عَلَيْهَا' – contrary to when negation precedes the quantifier. In that case, the negation is particular (salb juz'i). For example, 'ما كل عالم فاضل' (Not every scholar is virtuous) is a particular negative.
The rule is described as 'aghlabiyyah' (predominant) rather than absolute, but generally holds.
3
Apply to the shahada (La ilaha illa Allah)
The shahada is interpreted as a universal negative: negation of all deities except Allah. Al-Bajuri says it is 'salibah kulliyyah' due to 'umum al-salb' covering all individuals of deity except the excepted one (Allah).
The negation 'la' attached to the generic noun 'ilaha' implies a universal quantifier through linguistic convention.
4
Relate to the hadith analysis
In the earlier hadith example (not quoted in full here), the Prophet's reply 'ما كان من ذلك شيء' is shown to be a universal negative. The rule about negation position supports the interpretation that the negation covers each individual matter.
The analysis of 'am' question also contributes, but the rule here provides additional justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٣٨) وَحَيْتُمَا لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ حُكِمَا فَإِنَّهُ: «كُلِّيَّةٌ» قَدْ عُلِمَا
وَأَيْضاً المُقَرَّرُ أَنَّ السُّؤَالَ بِـ «أَمْ» لِطَلَبِ تَعْيِينِ أَحَدِ الأَمْرَيْنِ المُعْتَقَدِ ثُبُوتُ أَحَدِهِمَا، وَجَوَابُ ذَلِكَ إِمَّا بِالتَّعْيِينِ أَوْ بِنَفْي كُلِّ مِنَ الأَمْرَيْنِ المَذْكُورَيْنِ، لَا بِنَفْيِ المَجْمُوعِ، وَلَيْسَ فِي الحَدِيثِ تَعْيِينٌ، فَوَجَبَ أَنْ يَكُونَ نَفْياً لِكُلِّ مِنْهُمَا، وَيُؤَيِّدُ مَا ذُكِرَ مَا هُوَ القَاعِدَةُ - وَإِنْ كَانَتْ أَغْلَبِيَّةٌ - مِنْ «أَنَّ تَاخُرَ النَّفْيِ عَنْ أَدَاةِ التَّعْمِيمِ لِعُمُومِ السَّلْبِ»، بِخِلَافِ تَقَدُّمِهَا عَلَيْهَا. هَذَا؛ وَقَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ: البَحْثُ فِي المَثَلِ لَيْسَ مِنْ دَأْبِ الفُحُولِ، وَيَنْبَغِي أَنَّ مَحَلَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ يَتَرَتَّبْ عَلَى التَّمْثِيلِ ارْتِكَابُ خِلَافِ الوَاقِعِ فِي كَلَامِ اللهِ تَعَالَى أَوْ كَلَامِ رَسُولِهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم كَمَا هُنَا، فَاحْفَظْهُ. قَوْلُهُ: (وَحَيْتُمَا لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ ... إلخ) اللَّامُ فِيهِ بِمَعْنَى: عَلَى، وَهِيَ مُتَعَلِّقَةٌ بِقَوْلِهِ: «حُكِمَا»، وَذَلِكَ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿كُلُّ نَفْسٍ ذَائِقَةُ الْمَوْتِ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٨٥] وَكَمَا فِي الكَلِمَةِ المُشَرَّفَةِ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّهَا سَالِبَةٌ كُلِّيَّةٌ؛ لِعُمُومِ السَّلْبِ فِيهَا لِجَمِيعِ أَفْرَادِ الإِلَهِ غَيْرِ الذَّاتِ العَلِيَّةِ المُسْتَثْنَاةِ اسْتِثْنَاءً مُتَّصِلاً؛ لِدُخُولِ المُسْتَثْنَى فِي المُسْتَثْنَى مِنْهُ بِحَسَبِ الوَضْعِ.

And also it is established that the question with 'am' is for requesting the specification of one of two matters when it is believed that one of them is established. And the answer to that is either by specification or by negating each of the two mentioned matters, not by negating the aggregate. Commentary

It begins by defining 'kulliyya' as a judgment made on each individual (kull fard) of the subject—this is the distributive universal. The answer must either specify one or negate each individually, not negate the aggregate. Since the Prophet did not specify one, his reply 'All of that did not happen' must be a negation of each individually (i.e., universal negative). This is supported by a logical rule: when the negation (nafy) comes after the universal quantifier (adatu al-ta'mim), it produces a universal negative (salb kulli); when it precedes, it produces a particular negative. This rule is general, though it acknowledges it is predominant. The author then notes a caution: some scholars say that engaging in such detailed analysis of examples is not the practice of great scholars, unless necessary to avoid attributing falsehood to Allah or His Messenger—so this analysis is justified. Finally, the passage illustrates the rule with the Quranic verse 'Every soul shall taste death' (kullu nafsin dha'iqatu al-mawt) as an example of a universal affirmative (kulliyya), and the shahada 'La ilaha illa Allah' as a universal negative with a continuous exception. The shahada is interpreted as a universal negative (salb kulli), meaning 'No deity exists except Allah', with the exception of Allah. This shows the application of the rule: the negation is after the quantifier (adatu al-ta'mim is implied in 'la ilaha'), yielding a universal negative.

Propositions

Why is it Called 'Qadiyya'? The Logic of Propositions
Author explains the meaning, derivation, and rulings of qadiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the linguistic meaning and morphological derivation of 'qadiyya' and identify its two inherent rulings: contradiction and simple conversion.

Core Idea
A proposition is called 'qadiyya' because it contains a judgment linking its two terms—it is not about external occurrence.

This passage opens the chapter on propositions (qadaya). The author dissects the term 'qadiyya' both semantically and morphologically, showing how its plural 'qadaya' evolves through four phonetic changes. He also pinpoints the two logical rulings that every proposition carries: contradiction and simple conversion.

Guided Path
1
Parse 'qadiyya' as a patterned noun
Notice the pattern 'fa‘īla'. The author gives two possibilities: passive ('that which is judged') or active ('that which judges') via metaphorical attribution. Identify which interpretation matches the context of a proposition containing a judgment.
The passive sense is more apt because the proposition is the vehicle of judgment.
2
Understand the reason for naming
The term is derived from 'qaḍā’’ (judgment). But the judgment here is the relational link (ḥukm) between subject and predicate, not the mental perception of occurrence.
This distinction is crucial: the proposition itself only states the relation; the truth value is perceived separately.
3
Trace the plural derivation of 'qadaya'
Follow the four steps: original 'qadāyī' → replace first yā’ with hamza (qadā’ī) → open hamza for ease → change second yā’ to alif → change hamza to yā’ because it falls between two alifs. The result is 'qadāyā'.
This morphological breakdown is standard in Arabic grammar; understanding it helps recognize the word's root.
4
Identify the two rulings of propositions
The author mentions 'their rulings' (ahkāmihā) as contradiction (tanāquḍ) and simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawī). These are operations that preserve or alter truth values.
The plural is used for two because in this art plural often covers a pair.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابٌ فِي القَضَايَا وَأَحْكَامِهَا
قَوْلُهُ : بَابٌ فِي القَضَايَا جَمْعُ: «قَضِيَّةٍ»، «فَعِيْلَةٍ بِمَعْنَى : مَفْعُولَةٍ»؛ أَيْ: مَقْضِيٌّ فِيهَا أَوْ بِمَعْنَى فَاعِلَةٍ» ؛ أَيْ: قَاضِيَّةٍ، عَلَى الإِسْنَادِ المَجَازِيِّ
وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ بِذَلِكَ ; لِأَنَّهَا تَتَضَمَّنُ القَضَاءَ ; بِمَعْنَى : الحُكْمِ ، المُرَادُ بِهِ : النِّسْبَةُ بَيْنَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ، لا الإيقاع وَالانْتِزَاعَ; أَيْ: إِدْرَاكَ الوُقُوعِ أَوْ عَدَمِ الوُقُوعِ ; لِأَنَّهَا لَمْ تَتَضَمَّنُ ذَلِكَ; لأَنَّهُ قَائِمٌ بِنَفْسِ المُدْرِكِ، كَمَا سَيَأْتِي. وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ وَزْنَ قَضَايَا) بِاعْتِبَارِ الأَصْلِ : «فَعَايِلَ» ; لِأَنَّ أَصْلَهَا : «قَضَايي» بِيَاءَيْنِ، فَأُبْدِلَتِ الأُوْلَى هَمْزَةٌ عَلَى القِيَاسِ فِي نَحْوِ : (صَحَائِفِ ، ورَسَائِلِ»، ثُمَّ فُتِحَتِ الهَمْزَةُ) لِلتَّخْفِيفِ، ثُمَّ قُلِبَتِ الثَّانِيَةُ أَلِفاً لِتَحَرُّكِهَا وَانْفِتَاحِ مَا قَبْلَهَا ، ثُمَّ قُلِبَتِ الهَمْزَةُ يَاءَ» ; لِوُقُوعِهَا بَيْنَ أَلِفَيْنِ، فَصَارَ : قَضَايَا» بَعْدَ أَرْبَعَةِ أَعْمَالٍ .
وَقَوْلُهُ : (وَأَحْكَامِهَا) أَي : الَّتِي هِيَ : التَّناقُضُ، وَالعَكْسُ المُسْتَوِي . وَإِنَّمَا جَمَعَهَا المُصَنِّفُ; لأَنَّ الجَمْعَ يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الاثْنَيْنِ كَثِيراً خُصُوصاً فِي هَذَا الفَنِّ، أَوْ لِأَنَّهُ اعْتَبَرَ الأَفْرَادَ .
A chapter on propositions and their rulings. His statement: 'A chapter on propositions' is the plural of 'qadiyya', a pattern of 'fa‘īla' meaning 'that which is judged' i.e. that in which a judgment is made, or meaning 'that which judges' i.e. a judging one, in a metaphorical attribution. And it is only called that because it entails judgment, meaning the ruling, intended to mean the relation between the two terms, not the affirmation and extraction, i.e. the perception of occurrence or non-occurrence, because it does not entail that; for that resides in the perceiver itself, as will come. And know that the measure of 'qadaya' by the original is 'fa‘āyil', because its original is 'qadāyī' with two ya's, so the first was replaced with a hamza by analogy in examples like 'ṣaḥā’if' and 'rasā’il', then the hamza was opened for ease, then the second was turned into an alif because it is moving and what precedes it is open, then the hamza was turned into a ya' because it falls between two alifs, so it became 'qadāyā' after four operations. And his statement: 'and their rulings' i.e. which are: contradiction and simple conversion. And the author only pluralized them because the plural is often applied to two, especially in this art, or because he considered the individuals.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and philological explanation of the chapter title. The author begins by noting that 'qadaya' is the plural of 'qadiyya', which follows the pattern 'fa‘īla'. He gives two possible interpretations: either as a passive meaning ('that which is judged') or an active meaning via metaphorical attribution ('that which judges'). The term is called 'qadiyya' because it contains a judgment (al-qaḍā'), meaning a ruling (al-ḥukm) that relates the two terms of a proposition. This judgment is a relation, not the mental act of affirming or denying occurrence, which is a separate mental act. The author then provides a detailed morphological derivation of the plural form 'qadaya', tracing its origin through four phonetic changes: from 'qadāyī' to 'qadā'if' to 'qadāyā'. Finally, he explains that 'ahkāmihā' (their rulings) refers to the two standard logical operations on propositions: contradiction (tanāquḍ) and simple conversion (al-‘aks al-mustawī). The author notes that the plural could cover just these two, as it is common to use the plural for two in this discipline.

Key Terms
قَضِيَّة
قَضَاء
حُكْم
تَنَاقُض
عَكْس مُسْتَوِي

Key Terms from Source Passage

A. Because it is a court case.
B. Because it entails a judgment (hukm) between its two terms.

Key Takeaway
A qadiyya is a proposition that contains a relational judgment; its plural derives through four phonetic changes, and it carries two rulings: contradiction and simple conversion.

Definition of Proposition as What Admits Truth for Its Own Sake
Author defines proposition with exclusions and inclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the logical definition of a proposition and distinguish it from performative utterances.

Core Idea
A proposition is a statement that can be true or false by its own content.

The author unpacks the classic definition of a proposition (qadiyya) as 'what admits truth for its own sake'. He explains that the genus is 'utterance' and the differentia is 'admits truth for itself', which excludes terms and performatives like commands. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly identifying which statements can serve as premises in logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus (jins)
The author says 'ma' (what) covers all utterances, so the genus is 'utterance' (lafz). This includes both terms and statements.
The genus is broad; the differentia will narrow it down.
2
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is 'admits truth for its own sake'. This separates propositions from non-declarative utterances.
The condition 'for its own sake' is key.
3
Analyze the exclusions
First exclusion: words that do not admit truth at all, like proper names ('Zayd') and possessive phrases ('Gulam Zayd'). Second exclusion: performatives (commands, prohibitions) that admit truth only via implication.
Performatives are not propositions by themselves.
4
Understand the inclusion of certainties
Statements known with certainty (e.g., divine reports, self-evident truths) are included because they still admit truth inherently, even if we are certain.
Certainty does not remove the inherent potential for truth/falsity.
5
Recognize that 'truth' implies falsity
The author notes that mentioning truth alone suffices because admitting truth logically implies admitting falsity.
The definition covers both truth and falsity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (مَا احْتَمَلَ ... إلخ) «مَا » : وَاقِعَةٌ عَلَى اللَّفْظِ الشَّامِلِ لِجَمِيعِ الْأَلْفَاظِ ، فَهِيَ جِنْسٌ. وَخَرَجَ بِقَوْلِهِ : «احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ : مَا لَمْ يَحْتَمِلُهُ كَـ زَيْدٍ وَعَمْرِوا، وَكَ: (غُلَامُ زَيْدِ» . وَبِقَوْلِهِ : «لِذَاتِهِ» : مَا احْتَمَلَهُ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِلَازِمِهِ كَـ الإِنْشَاءَاتِ مِنَ الْأَمْرِ وَالنَّهْيِ وَغَيْرِهِمَا فَإِنَّ قَوْلَكَ : «اسْقِنِي مَثَلاً وَإِنِ احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لَكِنْ لَا لِذَاتِهِ، بَلْ لِمَا اسْتَلْزَمَهُ مِنْ قَوْلِكَ : «أَنَا طَالِبٌ لِلسُّقْيَا مِنْكَ».
وَدَخَلَ بِهَذَا القَيْدِ : مَا قُطِعَ بِصِدْقِهِ أَوْ بِكَذِبِهِ :
- فَالأَوَّلُ كَـ: أَخْبَارِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى، وَأَخْبَارِ رُسُلِهِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ، وَالأَخْبَارِ المَعْلُومِ صِدْقَهَا بِضَرُورَةِ العَقْلِ ؛ نَحْوِ : «الوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الاِثْنَيْنِ».
- وَالثَّانِي كَـ أَخْبَارِ مُسَيلِمَةَ الكَذَّابِ فِي دَعْوَاهُ النُّبُوَّةِ، وَالأَخْبَارِ المَعْلُومِ كَذِبَهَا بِضَرُورَةِ العَقْلِ؛ نَحْوِ : «الوَاحِدُ نِصْفُ الأَرْبَعَة»؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ يَحْتَمِلُ الصِّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ، وَإِنْ قُطِعَ بِصِدْقِهِ أَوْ كَذِبِهِ لِشَيْءٍ آخَرَ، وَبِهَذَا تَعْلَمْ أَنَّ القَيْدَ المَذْكُورَ لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْإِخْرَاجِ وَالإِدْخَالِ. قَوْلُهُ : (الصِّدْقَ) أَيْ: وَالكَذِبَ، وَإِنَّمَا لَمْ يُصَرِّحُ بِهِ لِلعِلْمِ بِهِ؛ إِذْ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ كَوْنِهِ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلصِّدْقِ كَوْنُهُ مُحْتَمِلاً لِلكَذِبِ.
His statement: 'What admits... etc.' 'Ma' is applied to the expression that includes all utterances, so it is a genus. And by his statement 'admits truth' is excluded what does not admit it, like 'Zayd' and 'Amr' and like 'Gulam Zayd'. And by his statement 'for its own sake' is excluded what admits it not for itself but for its concomitant, like the performatives (insha'at) of command, prohibition, and others. For your statement 'Give me a drink' for example, though it admits truth, it is not for itself but for what it entails of your statement 'I am a seeker of a drink from you'. And by this condition enters what is known to be true or false with certainty: the first like the reports of Allah the Exalted and the reports of His messengers (peace be upon them) and reports known to be true by necessity of reason, such as 'one is half of two'; the second like the reports of Musaylima the liar in his claim of prophethood, and reports known to be false by necessity of reason, such as 'one is half of four'; because that admits truth for its own sake, even if it is known to be true or false due to something else. And by this you know that the mentioned condition applies to both exclusion and inclusion. His statement: 'truth' meaning also falsity, but he did not state it explicitly because it is known; for from being admitting truth necessarily follows being admitting falsity.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the standard logical definition of a proposition (qadiyya) as 'what admits truth for its own sake'. The author, commenting on the matn, breaks down each part of the definition. The word 'ma' (what) is a generic term covering all possible utterances, serving as the genus. The first condition 'admits truth' excludes words that cannot be true or false, such as proper names like 'Zayd' and 'Amr' and possessive phrases like 'Zayd's slave' — these are not statements but terms. The second condition 'for its own sake' excludes performative utterances (insha'at) like commands and prohibitions. For example, the imperative 'Give me a drink' does admit truth implicitly — it implies the statement 'I am requesting a drink' — but that truth is not in the imperative itself but in its logical implication. So the definition ensures that only declarative statements (khabar) properly qualify as propositions, not performatives. The author then clarifies that the definition also includes statements that are known to be certainly true or false, such as divine reports or self-evident truths (e.g., 'one is half of two') — these still admit truth for their own sake, even though we are certain about them, because the possibility of truth or falsity is inherent in the statement itself, not dependent on our knowledge. Similarly, statements known to be certainly false, like Musaylima's claims or 'one is half of four', are also propositions because they could be true or false by themselves. The definition uses 'truth' alone, but this implies falsity as its necessary correlate. Thus the definition is comprehensive: it includes all declarative statements that by their nature can be evaluated for truth or falsity, regardless of actual certainty.

Key Terms
قَضِيَّة (qadiyya) – proposition
إِنْشَاء (inshā') – performative utterance
صِدْق (ṣidq) – truth (implying falsity)
لَازِم (lāzim) – necessary implication

A. Because it is not a statement.
B. Because it does not admit truth at all.
C. Because it admits truth not for itself but for its concomitant.
D. Because it is not grammatically complete.

Key Takeaway
A proposition is any declarative statement that by its own content can be true or false, regardless of our certainty.

Truth vs Reality: Direction of Correspondence
Distinguishes sidq as correspondence of propositional relation to real relation, haqq as opposite, and addresses circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish truth (sidq) from reality (haqq) based on the direction of correspondence and explain why using truth to define assertion does not involve circularity.

Core Idea
Truth and reality are mirror-image correspondences: sidq goes from speech to reality, haqq from reality to speech.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies the precise relationship between truth and reality, showing they are not the same concept but two directions of a single relation. He also defends the standard definition of assertion (khabar) against a charge of circularity.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
Find the sentences defining sidq and haqq. Note key difference: sidq is mutabaqat al-nisba al-mafhumati min al-khabar lil-nisba allati fi al-waqi'; haqq is mutabaqat al-nisba allati fi al-waqi' lil-nisba al-mafhumati min al-khabar.
2
Understand directionality
The term mutabaqa is mutual, but it is attributed differently. For sidq, the propositional relation corresponds to the real relation; for haqq, the real relation corresponds to the propositional relation. Think of it as a vector.
Direction of attribution is key.
3
Examine the alternative view
Some equate sidq and haqq by always measuring speech against reality, using an analogy of the sultan and vizier. Reflect on why this view might be appealing, but note that the well-known view preserves the distinct directions.
Analogy: 'The vizier sat with the sultan' is proper; the reverse is not.
4
Analyze the circularity objection
The objection is that if khabar is defined using sidq, and sidq is defined using khabar, there is a circle. The reply is that sidq is already well-known, so it does not need definition; hence it can be used in defining khabar.
This is a common move in Islamic logic: appeal to everyday knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَيْضاً : فِي اقْتِصَارِهِ عَلَى الصِّدْقِ تَأَدُّبٌ فِي حَقّ كَلَامِ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَكَلَامِ رُسُلِهِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ . وَمَعْنَى الصِّدْقِ : مُطَابَقَةُ النِّسْبَةِ المَفْهُومَةِ مِنَ الخَبَرِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ الَّتِي فِي الوَاقِعِ، وَضِدُّهُ: الكَذِبُ، بِخِلَافِ الحَقِّ فَإِنَّهُ مُطَابَقَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الَّتِي فِي الوَاقِع لِلنِّسْبَةِ المَفْهُومَةِ مِنَ الخَبَرِ، وَضِدُّهُ : البَاطِلُ، فَالمُطَابَقَةُ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُفَاعَلَةٌ مِنَ الجَانِبَيْنِ، لَكِنَّهَا تُسْنَدُ فِي تَفْسِيرِ الصِّدْقِ إِلَى النِّسْبَةِ الخَبَرِيَّةِ، وَفِي تَفْسِيرِ الحَقِّ إِلَى النِّسْبَةِ الوَاقِعِيَّةِ ؛ هَذَا هُوَ الَّذِي اشْتُهِرَ . وَقَدِ اخْتَارَ بَعْضُهُمْ : أَنَّ الصِّدْقَ وَالحَقَّ شَيْءٌ وَاحِدٌ، وَهُوَ : مُطَابَقَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الخَبَرِيَّةِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ الوَاقِعِيَّةِ ، قَالَ : لأَنَّ مَا فِي الوَاقِعِ أَمْرٌ ثَابِتٌ، فَالأَنْسَبُ أَنْ يُقَاسَ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرُهُ، لَا العَكْسُ ; بِأَنْ يُلَاحَظ مُطَابَقَةَ غَيْرِهِ لَهُ ، لَا مُطَابَقَتَهُ لِغَيْرِهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتِ المُفَاعَلَةُ مِنَ الجَانِبَيْنِ ; أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ يَحْسُنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: جَالَسَ الوَزِيرُ السُّلْطَانَ، وَلَا يَحْسُنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ : جَالَسَ السُّلْطَانُ الوَزِيرَ». وَاعْتُرِضَ أَخْذُ الصِّدْقِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الخَبَرِ بـ : أَنَّهُمْ قَدْ أَخَذُوا «الخَبَرَ» فِي تَعْرِيفِ «الصِّدْقِ، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ; لِتَوَقُفِ كُلّ عَلَى الآخَرِ. وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ الصِّدْقَ كَالكَذِبِ اشْتُهِرَ فِي المُحَاوَرَاتِ، فَلَا يَحْتَاجُ لِلتَّعْرِيفِ، فَصَحَّ أَخْذُهُ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الخَبَرِ.
And also: in his confining [the definition] to truth there is politeness regarding the speech of Allah Most High and the speech of His messengers, peace be upon them. And the meaning of truth (sidq) is: the correspondence of the relation understood from the assertion to the relation that is in reality; its opposite is falsehood. Contrary to reality (haqq), for it is the correspondence of the relation that is in reality to the relation understood from the assertion; its opposite is falsehood (batil). So correspondence, though it is mutual from both sides, is attributed in the interpretation of truth to the propositional relation, and in the interpretation of reality to the real relation. This is what is well-known. And some have chosen that truth and reality are one thing, namely: the correspondence of the propositional relation to the real relation. He said: because what is in reality is a fixed matter, so it is more appropriate that others be measured against it, not the reverse; namely, that the correspondence of others to it is considered, not its correspondence to others, even if the mutuality is from both sides. Do you not see that it is proper to say 'the vizier sat with the sultan', and it is not proper to say 'the sultan sat with the vizier'? And the taking of truth in the definition of assertion was objected to: that they have taken 'assertion' in the definition of 'truth', and then there would be a circle, because each depends on the other. And it was answered: that truth, like falsehood, is well-known in dialogues, so it does not need definition; therefore taking it in the definition of assertion is valid.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of truth (sidq) and distinguishes it from reality (haqq). The commentator al-Bajuri begins by noting that the matn's limiting of the definition to truth (rather than including falsehood explicitly) is a matter of politeness when speaking of divine and prophetic speech. He then defines truth: correspondence of the relation understood from the assertion (khabar) to the relation existing in reality. Falsehood (kidhb) is the opposite. In contrast, reality (haqq) is the correspondence of the real relation to the propositional relation; its opposite is falsehood (batil). Thus, correspondence has two directions: from speech to reality (truth) and from reality to speech (reality). Although correspondence is mutual, in truth the reference is to the propositional relation, while in reality the reference is to the real relation. Al-Bajuri then presents an alternative view: some scholars equate truth and reality, defining both as the correspondence of speech to reality, arguing that reality is the fixed standard against which others are measured—like saying 'the vizier sat with the sultan' is proper, but not the reverse. The passage concludes with an objection: defining proposition (khabar) using truth (sidq) leads to circularity, because truth is defined using proposition. The answer is that truth is well-known in everyday discourse and does not require a technical definition; thus it can be used in defining proposition without circularity.

Key Terms
صِدْق (sidq): Truth: correspondence of the relation expressed in the assertion to the relation in reality. [Note: In later texts, sidq may be defined purely semantically; here it is relational.]
حَق (haqq): Reality: correspondence of the real relation to the relation expressed in the assertion; opposite of batil. [Note: In theology, haqq often means 'truth' or 'real' as an attribute of Allah; here restricted to logical correspondence.]
مُطَابَقَة (mutabaqa): Correspondence between the propositional relation and the real relation, considered from either side. [Note: Stable across the logical tradition.]
دَوْر (dawr): Circular definition; a logical fallacy where term A is defined by term B which in turn depends on A. [Note: Common concept in logic and usul al-fiqh.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known view, how does truth (sidq) differ from reality (haqq)?
A) Truth is correspondence of speech to reality; reality is correspondence of reality to speech.
B) Truth and reality are exactly the same definition.
C) Truth applies only to divine speech; reality applies to human speech.
D) Truth is about necessary propositions; reality is about contingent ones.

Key Takeaway
Truth and reality are two sides of the same correspondence: sidq is speech matching reality, haqq is reality matching speech, and the circularity objection fails because 'truth' is already known in everyday speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Given the proposition 'The sun rises from the east,' determine whether the truth (sidq) or reality (haqq) is being considered when you say 'That is true.' Explain the direction of correspondence.
?
2. Some early texts defined khabar as 'a statement that can be true or false.' Explain how this definition avoids the circularity objection raised in the passage.

Defining the Proposition: Sidq and Haqq Distinguished
Author defines proposition and distinguishes sidq from haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sidq and haqq and explain why the definition of proposition is restricted to sidq.

Core Idea
Truth matches statement to reality; rightness matches reality to statement.

The definition of a proposition as 'what admits truth' uses a specific type of truth: sidq. This lesson unpacks that distinction and shows its logical and theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined: 'proposition' (qadiyya/khabar). The Arabic phrase is 'ما احتمل الصدق لذاته'.
The definiendum is the common concept shared by both terms.
2
Extract the genus
The definition uses 'what admits truth by its essence' as the genus. Note that this is a simple description (rasm) rather than a genus+differentia definition.
3
Distinguish sidq and haqq
Sidq is correspondence of the statement's relation to reality; haqq is correspondence of reality to the statement's relation. They are directional opposites.
Both involve mutual correspondence but differ in attribution.
4
Understand the politeness reason
The restriction to sidq in the definition is out of politeness for divine speech, which is always true and never false.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition 'Allah is One.' Its sidq is that the statement matches reality. Its haqq is that reality matches the statement. Which direction is primary?
The author notes reality is fixed, so sidq is usually the focus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا احْتَمَلَ الصِّدْقَ لِذَاتِهِ جَرَى بَيْنَهُمُ: «قَضِيَّةٌ» و«خَبَرَا». وَأَيْضاً: فِي اقْتِصَارِهِ عَلَى الصِّدْقِ تَأَدُّبٌ فِي حَقِّ كَلَامِ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَكَلَامِ رُسُلِهِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ. وَمَعْنَى الصِّدْقِ: مُطَابَقَةُ النِّسْبَةِ المَفْهُومَةِ مِنَ الخَبَرِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ الَّتِي فِي الوَاقِعِ، وَضِدُّهُ: الكَذِبُ، بِخِلَافِ الحَقِّ فَإِنَّهُ مُطَابَقَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الَّتِي فِي الوَاقِعِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ المَفْهُومَةِ مِنَ الخَبَرِ، وَضِدُّهُ: البَاطِلُ، فَالمُطَابَقَةُ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُفَاعَلَةً مِنَ الجَانِبَيْنِ، لَكِنَّهَا تُسْنَدُ فِي تَفْسِيرِ الصِّدْقِ إِلَى النِّسْبَةِ الخَبَرِيَّةِ، وَفِي تَفْسِيرِ الحَقِّ إِلَى النِّسْبَةِ الوَاقِعِيَّةِ؛ هَذَا هُوَ الَّذِي اشْتُهِرَ. وَقَدِ اخْتَارَ بَعْضُهُمْ: أَنَّ الصِّدْقَ وَالحَقَّ شَيْءٌ وَاحِدٌ، وَهُوَ: مُطَابَقَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الخَبَرِيَّةِ لِلنِّسْبَةِ الوَاقِعِيَّةِ، قَالَ: لأَنَّ مَا فِي الوَاقِعِ أَمْرٌ ثَابِتٌ، فَالأَنْسَبُ أَنْ يُقَاسَ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرُهُ، لَا العَكْسُ؛ بِأَنْ يُلَاحَظَ مُطَابَقَةُ غَيْرِهِ لَهُ، لَا مُطَابَقَتُهُ لِغَيْرِهِ، وَإِنْ كَانَتِ المُفَاعَلَةُ مِنَ الجَانِبَيْنِ؛ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ يَحْسُنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: جَالَسَ الوَزِيرُ السُّلْطَانَ، وَلَا يَحْسُنُ أَنْ يُقَالَ: جَالَسَ السُّلْطَانُ الوَزِيرَ. وَاعْتُرِضَ أَخْذُ الصِّدْقِ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الخَبَرِ بِأَنَّهُمْ قَدْ أَخَذُوا «الخَبَرَ» فِي تَعْرِيفِ «الصِّدْقِ»، وَحِينَئِذٍ يَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ؛ لِتَوَقُفِ كُلٍّ عَلَى الآخَرِ. وَأُجِيبَ: بِأَنَّ الصِّدْقَ كَالكَذِبِ اشْتُهِرَ فِي المُحَاوَرَاتِ، فَلَا يَحْتَاجُ لِلتَّعْرِيفِ، فَصَحَّ أَخْذُهُ فِي تَعْرِيفِ الخَبَرِ.
What admits truth by its essence circulates between them: 'proposition' and 'statement'. Also: In restricting it to truth there is politeness regarding the speech of Allah the Exalted and the speech of His messengers, peace be upon them. And the meaning of truth (sidq) is: the correspondence of the relation understood from the statement to the relation that is in reality, and its opposite is falsehood; unlike al-haqq (right/truth), for it is the correspondence of the relation that is in reality to the relation understood from the statement, and its opposite is falsehood. Correspondence, although it is an interaction from both sides, is attributed in the interpretation of sidq to the statement's relation, and in the interpretation of haqq to the real relation. This is what is well-known. And some chose that sidq and haqq are one thing, namely the correspondence of the statement's relation to the real relation. He said: Because what is in reality is a fixed matter, so it is more appropriate that something else be measured by it, not the opposite; so that one takes into account the correspondence of something else to it, not its correspondence to something else, even though the interaction is from both sides. Don't you see that it is appropriate to say 'The minister sat with the sultan,' but not appropriate to say 'The sultan sat with the minister.' And the taking of sidq in the definition of khabar was objected to, because they took 'khabar' in the definition of 'sidq', and then circularity follows, due to each depending on the other. And it was answered: That sidq, like falsehood, is well-known in common discourse, so it does not need definition; thus taking it in the definition of khabar is valid.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on the matn line 'What admits truth by its essence circulates between them: proposition and statement.' The author (Bajuri) first notes a subtle point: the restriction of the definition to truth (sidq) is a form of politeness (ta'ddub) with respect to the speech of Allah and His messengers, since their speech is always true and never false. He then defines sidq as the correspondence of the relation understood from the statement to the relation in reality; its opposite is falsehood (kadhib). In contrast, haqq (right/truth) is defined as the correspondence of the real relation to the statement's relation; its opposite is falsehood (batil). Both involve a mutual correspondence, but the direction of attribution differs: for sidq, the correspondence is attributed to the statement's relation; for haqq, it is attributed to the real relation. Some scholars, however, argue that sidq and haqq are identical, both meaning the correspondence of the statement's relation to the real relation. Their reasoning is that reality is fixed and therefore should be the standard against which the statement is measured, not vice versa. They illustrate with an analogy: it is appropriate to say 'the minister sat with the sultan' because the sultan is the higher authority, just as reality should be the reference. An objection is raised: taking sidq in the definition of khabar (statement) leads to circularity because khabar is also used in the definition of sidq. The answer is that sidq is so well-known in everyday discourse that it does not require definition, so no circularity occurs.

Key Terms
الصدق
الحق
الخبر
القضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known view in this passage, what is the difference between al-sidq and al-haqq?
A) Al-sidq is correspondence of the statement's relation to reality; al-haqq is correspondence of reality to the statement's relation.
B) Al-sidq applies only to divine speech; al-haqq applies to human speech.
C) They are synonyms with no difference.
D) Al-sidq is about correspondence; al-haqq is about coherence.

Key Takeaway
Sidq and haqq differ in the direction of correspondence, though some scholars equate them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the statement 'Allah is just', determine whether the correspondence is sidq or haqq according to the well-known view. Explain your reasoning.
?
Why does the author argue that the definition of proposition is restricted to sidq? Connect this to the example of divine speech.

Propositions
intermediate
7 min
The Circularity Objection in Defining Proposition and Its Resolution
Author presents the circularity objection and its resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the circularity objection to defining khabar using sidq and evaluate the response provided by the logicians.

Core Idea
The definition of sidq uses khabar, and the definition of khabar uses sidq – but sidq is well-known, so no circularity.

A common logical concern is that defining a proposition using truth involves circularity, because truth itself is defined using propositions. This lesson examines how classical logicians resolved this objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions involved
Khabar is defined as 'what admits sidq'. Sidq is defined as 'correspondence of the statement's relation to reality'. Notice that 'khabar' and 'sidq' appear in each other's definitions.
2
Understand the logical fallacy
Circularity (dawr) occurs when term A depends on B and B depends on A, making the definitions interdependent and potentially uninformative.
3
Locate the response
The logicians answer that sidq is so well-known in common discourse that it does not need definition, so taking it in the definition of khabar is valid.
This is the key move: sidq is treated as a primitive term.
4
Evaluate the response
Does this solve the circularity? It does if we accept that sidq is self-evident. In logic, primitive terms need not be defined.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
In usul al-fiqh, the definition of 'hukm' might use 'taklif', which itself uses 'hukm'. A defender could argue that 'taklif' is well-known.

And the meaning of truth (sidq) is: the correspondence of the relation understood from the statement to the relation that is in reality, and its opposite is falsehood; unlike al-haqq (right/truth), for it is the correspondence of the relation that is in reality to the relation understood from the statement, and its opposite is falsehood.
