Multiple Names of the Proposition Based on Different Considerations
Author lists the various names given to the proposition from al-Talwih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different names of a proposition based on the perspective from which it is considered, and identify which name applies in a given context.

Core Idea
One logical entity, many names depending on how you look at it.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the terms 'qadiyya' and 'khabar' refer to the same thing but emphasize different aspects. He then expands from al-Talwih to show that a single proposition can be called by five additional names depending on the consideration: as a judgment, as a component of a proof, as a sought conclusion, etc. Understanding this helps you navigate logical and theological texts where the same statement might be referred to by different technical terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common essence
Notice that the text begins by stating that 'qadiyya' and 'khabar' have the same meaning. Recognize that they refer to the same logical entity, differing only in the aspect emphasized.
2
Distinguish the two primary perspectives
Understand that 'qadiyya' is used when focusing on the inclusion of a judgment (hukm), while 'khabar' is used when focusing on the potential for truth or falsehood. This is the first distinction.
3
Enumerate the five additional names from al-Talwih
From al-Talwih, the commentator adds: ikhbar (informing) from conveying judgment, muqaddima (premise) as part of a proof, matlub (sought) as what is proven, natija (conclusion) as the result of a proof, and mas'ala (question) as what is investigated in a science.
The passage lists these explicitly.
4
Connect each name to its corresponding consideration
For each name, identify the specific consideration (i'tibar) that gives rise to it. The essence (dhat) is one, but the expressions differ by the consideration.
Review the table: Name -> Consideration.
5
Apply the principle to a logical context
Given a statement used in a specific way (e.g., as a premise, as a conclusion), determine which name is appropriate. This consolidates your understanding.
Try the tasks in apply_the_tool.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : جَرَى بَيْنَهُمُ ... إلخ) عُلِمَ مِنْهُ : أَنَّ القَضِيَّةَ وَالخَبَرَ بِمَعْنَى وَاحِدٍ، وَهُوَ : مَا احْتَمَلَ ... إلخ » ، لَكِنْ تَسْمِيَّتُهُ: «قَضِيَّةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ اشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الحُكْمِ، وَتَسْمِيَّتُهُ : خَبَراً» مِنْ حَيْثُ احْتِمَالِهِ الصِّدْقَ . وَفِي التَّلْوِيحِ: أَنَّهُ يُسَمَّى: «إِخْبَاراً مِنْ حَيْثُ إِفَادَتِهِ الحُكْمَ، وَ: «مُقَدِّمَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنِهِ جُزْءاً مِنَ الدَّلِيلِ، وَ : «مَظْلُوباً » مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنِهِ يُطْلَبُ بِالدَّلِيلِ، وَ نَتِيجَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنِهِ نَتِيجَةَ الدَّلِيلِ، وَ: «مَسْأَلَةٌ مِنْ حَيْثُ كَوْنِهِ يُسْأَلُ عَنْهُ فِي العِلْم ; قَالَ : فَالذَّاتُ وَاحِدَةٌ، وَاخْتِلَافُ العِبَارَاتِ بِاخْتِلَافِ الاعْتِبَارَاتِ . اهـ. وَقَوْلُهُ : ((قَضِيَّةٌ) وَ(خَبَرَا» مَنْصُوبَانِ عَلَى الحَالِ مِنَ الضَّمِيرِ المُسْتَتِرِ فِي قَوْلِهِ : جَرَى».
His saying: 'circulates between them' etc. It is known from it that qadiyya and khabar have the same meaning, namely 'what admits truth' etc., but it is called 'qadiyya' from the perspective of its inclusion of judgment, and 'khabar' from the perspective of its admitting truth. And in al-Talwih: that it is called 'ikhbar' (informing) from the perspective of its conveying judgment, and 'muqaddima' (premise) from the perspective of its being part of the proof, and 'matlub' (demanded) from the perspective of its being sought by the proof, and 'natija' (conclusion) from the perspective of its being the result of the proof, and 'mas'ala' (question) from the perspective of its being asked about in the science. He said: So the essence is one, and the variation of expressions is by variation of considerations. End. And his saying: 'qadiyya and khabar' are both accusative as a condition (hal) from the implicit pronoun in his saying 'circulates'.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage explains that the terms 'qadiyya' and 'khabar' refer to the same entity but are used from different perspectives: 'qadiyya' emphasizes inclusion of a judgment (hukm), while 'khabar' emphasizes potential truth. The author then quotes from 'al-Talwih' (a well-known commentary) to enumerate additional names for the same logical entity: (1) ikhbar (informing) from conveying judgment, (2) muqaddima (premise) as a component of a proof, (3) matlub (sought) as that which is proven by a proof, (4) natija (conclusion) as the result of a proof, and (5) mas'ala (question) as that which is investigated in a science. Each name is based on a different consideration (i'tibar), while the underlying essence (dhat) is one. The passage concludes with a grammatical note: the two words 'qadiyya' and 'khabar' in the matn are in the accusative case as a hal (circumstantial clause) related to the implicit pronoun in 'jara' (circulates).

Key Terms
القضية
الخبر
المقدمة
المطلوب
النتيجة
المسألة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Talwih as quoted in this passage, what is the name of a proposition when considered as a component of a proof?
A) Qadiyya
B) Khabar
C) Muqaddima
D) Mas'ala

Key Takeaway
A single proposition has multiple names based on the perspective: judgment, truth, proof component, sought conclusion, or subject of inquiry, but its essence remains one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statement 'Alcohol is prohibited.' Identify its name as 'qadiyya' (from judgment aspect) and 'khabar' (from truth-potential aspect). Then, if this statement is used as a premise in a proof for another ruling, what would it be called?
?
In a kalam debate, a mutakallim says 'The world is originated.' If this statement is the result of a proof, what is its name?

The Division of Categorical Propositions: Universal, Particular, Quantified, Unquantified
Author subdivides categorical propositions into universal and particular, and universal into quantified and unquantified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition as universal (kulliyya) or particular (shakhsiyya), and if universal, identify whether it is quantified (musawwar) or unquantified (muhmal).

Core Idea
Universal propositions are defined by a universal subject, not by the presence of a quantifier.

In this passage, the author and commentator clarify a key distinction in categorical propositions: a proposition is universal if its subject is a universal term, regardless of whether a quantifier is present. This distinction is crucial for understanding how propositions are classified in Islamic logic, and it avoids confusion with the later technical meaning of 'kulliyya' as quantified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject term
Look at the proposition and find the term that is the subject (what the proposition is about).
2
Determine if the subject is universal or particular
A universal subject is a general term that can apply to many individuals (e.g., 'man', 'world'). A particular subject is a specific individual or definite description (e.g., 'Zayd', 'this house').
Examples: 'Every man is mortal' has subject 'man' (universal). 'Zayd is standing' has subject 'Zayd' (particular).
3
For universal propositions, check for an explicit quantifier
If the proposition has a quantifier like 'every', 'all', 'none', it is quantified (musawwar). If it lacks a quantifier, it is unquantified (muhmal).
Example: 'Man is an animal' (no quantifier) is muhmal; 'Every man is an animal' is musawwar.
4
Classify the proposition
Based on steps 1-3, assign the proposition to one of three categories: universal quantified, universal unquantified, or particular.
Recall that 'particular' here means the subject is a specific individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَالأَوَّلُ : إِمَّا مُسَوَّرٌ، وَإِمَّا مُهْمَلُ
قَوْلُهُ : (كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ) لَيْسَ المُرَادُ بِـ «الكُلِّيَّةِ» هُنَا : مَا دَخَلَ عَلَيْهَا السُّورُ الكُلّيُّ كَمَا هُوَ المُصْطَلَحُ عَلَيْهِ، بَلْ: مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيَّا»؛ بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ السُّورِ، كَمَا يُرْشِدُ لِذَلِكَ مُقَابَلَتُهَا بـ «الشَّخْصِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ : مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا مُشَخَّصاً ،مُعَيَّنَا وَلِذَلِكَ صَحَ التَّقْسِيمُ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ بِقَوْلِهِ :
وَالأَوَّلُ ... إلخ».
الأنبابي
وإنَّما نسبت إلى ما يؤخذ من صفة المحمول دون ما يؤخذ من صفة الموضوع؛ بأن يقال: «وضعية»
دون الموضوع؛
لأنه محط الفائدة .
فإن قلت : هذا إنَّما يتحقق في الموجبة، وأما السالبة فلا حمل فيها
فالجواب : أنَّه في السَّالبة يلاحظ الإيجاب، ثمَّ دخول حرف السَّلب، ففيها حمل بحسب التقدير، أو أنَّه كثيراً ما تسمَّى الأعدام باسم المَلَكَات؛ هذا توضيح ما أشار إليه المحشّي بقوله : (ولذلك سميت . .. إلخ».
Universal and particular. And the first: either quantified or unquantified. His statement: (Universal and particular) The intended meaning of 'kulliyya' here is not that which is entered by the universal quantifier as is the terminology, but rather that whose subject is universal, disregarding the quantifier, as indicated by its being contrasted with 'shakhsiyya' which is that whose subject is specified, determinate. Therefore the division he mentioned by his statement 'and the first... etc.' is correct. Al-Annababi: It is only attributed to what is taken from the description of the predicate, not from the description of the subject; such that one says 'wad'iyya' not from the subject; because it is the locus of benefit. If you say: This is only realized in the affirmative, but the negative has no predication, the answer: In the negative, affirmation is considered, then the negative particle enters, so it has predication by estimation, or that non-existences are often named by the name of their opposites. This is an explanation of what the commentator indicated by his statement: (and therefore it is called... etc.).
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage further divides categorical propositions. The author states that categorical propositions are either universal (kulliyya) or particular (shakhsiyya). Then the first, i.e., the universal, is either quantified (musawwar) i.e., having an explicit quantifier like 'every' or 'none', or unquantified (muhmal) i.e., without a quantifier. The commentator clarifies that 'kulliyya' here does not mean 'having a universal quantifier' as is the usual technical term; rather it means the subject of the proposition is a universal term, regardless of whether a quantifier is present. This is clear from its contrast with 'shakhsiyya', which means the subject is a specific individual. So a universal proposition in this context is one whose subject is a general term, like 'man' in 'Man is an animal', whether you say 'Every man is an animal' (quantified) or just 'Man is an animal' (unquantified). The division then proceeds: the universal is either quantified (with a quantifier) or unquantified (without). Al-Annababi adds an explanation: The categorical proposition is called 'hamliyya' (predicative) because it is attributed to the predicate, not the subject; the predicate is the locus of benefit. One might object that only affirmatives have predication; negatives do not. The answer is that in a negative proposition, one first considers the affirmation then adds the negation, so there is predication implicitly. Or, non-existences are often named after their opposites, so the negative still has a kind of predication. This clarifies the earlier statement about why it is called 'hamliyya'.

Key Terms
كلية
شخصية
مسور
مهمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what kind of proposition is 'Man is an animal' if no quantifier is used?
A) A particular proposition because it lacks a quantifier.
B) A universal proposition, but unquantified (muhmal).
C) A conditional proposition.
D) A quantified universal proposition.

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition is classified as universal or particular based on its subject, not the presence of a quantifier; universal propositions are further divided into quantified and unquantified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following propositions as universal quantified, universal unquantified, or particular: (a) 'Every believer is righteous.' (b) 'Man is mortal.' (c) 'Zayd is coming.'
?
Take the proposition 'The world is temporally originated' from kalam. Is it universal or particular? If universal, is it quantified or unquantified? Explain.

Why Categorical Propositions Are Called Predicative (Hamliyya) and the Treatment of Negatives
Al-Annababi explains the reason for calling categorical propositions hamliyya and addresses the issue of negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why categorical propositions are called 'hamliyya' (predicative) and how negative propositions are still considered predicative despite lacking an explicit predicate.

Core Idea
Categorical propositions are named after the predicate because it carries the benefit; negatives are treated as implicit affirmations.

Al-Annababi addresses a subtle point: why do we call categorical propositions 'hamliyya' (predicative) when they include negatives that seem to have no predication? He provides two solutions: either we consider the underlying affirmation in a negative, or we note that non-existences are often named after their opposites. This clarification is important for understanding the structure of propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the standard naming
Categorical propositions are called 'hamliyya' because they are attributed to the predicate, i.e., the predicate is the locus of benefit (the new information). It is not named after the subject.
2
Identify the problem with negatives
In a negative proposition like 'Zayd is not standing', there is no positive predicate; it seems to lack the predication that gives the proposition its name.
3
Learn the first solution: implicit affirmation
One answer is that in a negative proposition, one first considers the affirmative 'Zayd is standing', then adds the negation. So there is a predication by estimation.
4
Learn the second solution: naming after opposites
Another answer is that non-existences are often named by the name of their opposites (e.g., 'blindness' is named from 'sight'). Thus the negative still has some relation to a predicate.
This is a common principle in Islamic logic and legal theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

قَوْلُهُ : (كُلِّيَّةٌ شَخْصِيَّةٌ) لَيْسَ المُرَادُ بِـ «الكُلِّيَّةِ» هُنَا : مَا دَخَلَ عَلَيْهَا السُّورُ الكُلّيُّ كَمَا هُوَ المُصْطَلَحُ عَلَيْهِ، بَلْ: مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيَّا»؛ بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ السُّورِ، كَمَا يُرْشِدُ لِذَلِكَ مُقَابَلَتُهَا بـ «الشَّخْصِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ : مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا مُشَخَّصاً ،مُعَيَّنَا وَلِذَلِكَ صَحَ التَّقْسِيمُ الَّذِي ذَكَرَهُ بِقَوْلِهِ :

The Quantifier: Definition and Four Types
Al-Bajuri defines the quantifier and enumerates its four types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier in any categorical proposition and classify it as universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative using al-Bajuri's definition and examples.

Core Idea
The quantifier (al-sūr) determines scope: universal or particular, affirmative or negative.

Quantifiers are the gatekeepers of logical propositions—they tell us whether a claim covers all or some instances, and whether it affirms or denies. In this lesson, al-Bajuri defines the quantifier and lays out its four essential types, using clear examples from everyday knowledge. Mastering this classification is the first step toward analyzing arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read al-Bajuri's definition: the quantifier indicates encompassing all individuals (or some) in categorical propositions, and all possible states (or some) in conditional propositions. Ponder the metaphor of a city wall (sūr) that surrounds the whole city or a part.
2
Distinguish universal vs. particular
Universal quantifiers (e.g., كُلّ 'every') claim the predicate applies to every individual of the subject. Particular quantifiers (e.g., بَعْض 'some') claim it applies to at least one but not necessarily all.
3
Distinguish affirmative vs. negative
Affirmative quantifiers assert a connection (e.g., 'every human is an animal'). Negative quantifiers deny it (e.g., 'no human is a stone'). Each universal and particular can be affirmative or negative, giving four types.
4
Memorize the four types with examples
List the four: (1) universal affirmative – 'Every human is an animal'; (2) particular affirmative – 'Some animals are human'; (3) universal negative – 'No human is a stone'; (4) particular negative – 'Not some animal is human' (i.e., some animals are not human).
The particular negative example may be phrased as 'Some animals are not human' in modern English.
5
Apply to a new proposition
Take a proposition like 'All intoxicants are forbidden.' Identify the quantifier ('all' = universal affirmative). Then try 'Some contracts are invalid' (particular affirmative). Practice until the classification becomes automatic.
Use Islamic examples throughout.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥٣) وَالسُّورُ: كُلِّيَّا، وَجُزْئِيَّا يُرَى وَأَرْبَعُ أَقْسَامُهُ حَيْثُ جَرَى. قَالَ الباجوري: مَا دَلَّ عَلَى الإِحَاطَةِ بِجَمِيعِ الأَفْرَادِ، أَوْ بِبَعْضِهَا فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ؛ كَـ: «كُلّ» وَ: «بَعْضِ»، وَمَا دَلَّ عَلَى الإِحَاطَةِ بِجَمِيعِ الأَوْضَاعِ أَيْ الأَحْوَالِ المُمْكِنَةِ، أَوْ بِبَعْضِهَا فِي الشَّرْطِيَّةِ؛ كَـ: كُلَّمَا وَ: «قَدْ يَكُونُ». سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ تَشْبِيهاً لَهُ بِسُورِ البَلَدِ المُحِيطِ بِكُلِّهَا أَوْ بَعْضِهَا. قَوْلُهُ: (كُليًّا وَجُزْنِيًّا) وَكُلٌّ مِنْهُمَا إِمَّا إِيجَابِيٌّ وَإِمَّا سَلْبِيٌّ فَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ. مُحَصَّلُ الأَقْسَامِ الأَرْبَعَةِ: (١) السُّورُ الكُلِّيُّ الإِيجَابِيُّ وَهُوَ كُلُّ وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ. (٢) السُّورُ الجُزْئِيُّ الإِيجَابِيُّ وَهُوَ بَعْضُ وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ. (٣) السُّورُ الكُلِّيُّ السَّلْبِيُّ وَهُوَ لَا شَيْءَ وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ. (٤) السُّورُ الجُزْئِيُّ السَّلْبِيُّ وَهُوَ لَيْسَ بَعْضَ وَمَا أَشْبَهَهُ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ: لَيْسَ بَعْضَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ.
And the quantifier: universal, particular, is seen, and its four divisions wherever it occurs. Al-Bajuri said: What indicates encompassing all individuals or some of them in categorical propositions, like 'every' and 'some'; and what indicates encompassing all states, i.e. possible conditions, or some of them in conditional propositions, like 'whenever' and 'it may be'. It is named thus as a metaphor likening it to the wall of a city that surrounds all of it or some of it. His statement: 'universal and particular' - each of them is either affirmative or negative, so its divisions are four. The summary of the four divisions: (1) The universal affirmative quantifier, which is 'every' and its likes, as in your saying: 'Every human is an animal.' (2) The particular affirmative quantifier, which is 'some' and its likes, as in your saying: 'Some animals are human.' (3) The universal negative quantifier, which is 'none' and its likes, as in your saying: 'No human is a stone.' (4) The particular negative quantifier, which is 'not some' and its likes, as in your saying: 'Not some animal is human.'
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the quantifier (al-sūr) and divides it into four types. It begins with the matn line: 'And the quantifier: universal, particular, is seen, and its four divisions wherever it occurs.' Al-Bajuri then provides the technical definition: a quantifier is that which indicates encompassing all individuals or some in categorical propositions (e.g., كُل 'every', بَعْض 'some'), and in conditional propositions it indicates encompassing all possible states or some (e.g., كُلَّمَا 'whenever', قَدْ يَكُونُ 'it may be'). The term 'sūr' is used metaphorically, likening it to a city wall that surrounds the whole city or part of it. Next, he explains that each quantifier is either affirmative or negative, yielding four divisions. He then enumerates them: (1) universal affirmative (e.g., كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ 'Every human is an animal'), (2) particular affirmative (e.g., بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ 'Some animals are human'), (3) universal negative (e.g., لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ 'No human is a stone'), (4) particular negative (e.g., لَيْسَ بَعْضَ الحَيَوَانِ بِإِنْسَانٍ 'Not some animal is human' meaning 'Some animals are not human'). The enumeration follows the standard classification in Islamic logic, providing clear examples from common knowledge.

Key Terms
السور
السور الكلي الإيجابي
السور الجزئي الإيجابي
السور الكلي السلبي
السور الجزئي السلبي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the four types of quantifiers (al-suur) in categorical propositions according to al-Bajuri?
A) Universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
B) Universal, particular, indefinite, quantified.
C) Affirmative, negative, universal, particular.
D) Necessary, possible, impossible, contingent.

Key Takeaway
The quantifier (al-sūr) is the key to determining the logical scope of a proposition; it is divided into four types based on quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the quantifier type in the following Islamic propositions: a) 'كل مسكر حرام' (Every intoxicant is forbidden). b) 'بعض الحجاج مكة' (Some pilgrims are in Mecca). c) 'لا شيء من الخمر بحلال' (No wine is permissible). d) 'ليس بعض الأطعمة حلالا' (Not some foods are permissible, i.e., some foods are not permissible). For each, state whether it is universal/particular and affirmative/negative.
?
Construct your own proposition about a fiqh ruling and state its quantifier type. For example, 'كل عقد بيع صحيح' (Every sale contract is valid) is universal affirmative. Then try a particular negative.

From Four to Eight: The Quality Division of Categorical Propositions
Author explains that the four categorical propositions divide into affirmative and negative, returning to eight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the eight categorical propositions from the four basic ones by applying the quality division (affirmative/negative).

Core Idea
Four propositions x two qualities = eight: a foundational expansion for logical precision.

In the previous lesson, you learned the four categorical propositions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Now, the author reminds us that each of these is either affirmative or negative. But this is not just a trivial observation — it is the key to an expanded classification. By considering the four quantifiers (all, no, some, not some) and applying both affirmative and negative forms, we arrive at eight distinct proposition types. This expansion is crucial for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic logic and avoiding ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four basic propositions
Write down the four known propositions with their Arabic quantifiers: Kullu wājib (A), Lā shay' wājib (E), Ba'ḍ wājib (I), Ba'ḍ laysa wājib (O). Note that each has a fixed quality: A and I are affirmative, E and O are negative.
2
Understand the quality split
The matn says 'and all of them: affirmative and negative'. This means that each proposition belongs to one of these two categories. The author then says 'so they then return to eight'. Why? Because we can take each quantifier and combine it with both qualities, obtaining two propositions per quantifier.
3
Generate the eight propositions
Using the four quantifiers (Kull, Lā shay', Ba'ḍ with affirmation, Ba'ḍ with negation), write down the affirmative and negative versions for each. For example: Kull + affirmative = Kullu S wājib (A); Kull + negative = Kullu S laysa wājib (new); Lā shay' + affirmative = Lā shay' min S wājib (E); Lā shay' + negative = Lā shay' min S laysa wājib (new); Ba'ḍ + affirmative = Ba'ḍu S wājib (I); Ba'ḍ + negative = Ba'ḍu S laysa wājib (O); Laysa ba'ḍ + affirmative = Laysa ba'ḍu S wājib (new); Laysa ba'ḍ + negative = Laysa ba'ḍu S laysa wājib (new). Note that some are logically equivalent but syntactically distinct.
The eight forms cover all possible combinations of quantifier and quality.
4
Verify with the poetic analogy
The commentator cites a verse about teeth: four plus four become eight. Similarly, the four propositions, when considered with both qualities, become eight. This emphasizes that the classification is a genuine expansion, not a mere renaming.
The poetic analogy helps remember the count.

متن: (٥٥) وَكُلُّهَا : مُوجَبَةٌ، وَسَالِبَهُ فَهيَ إِذا إِلَى الثَّمَانِ آيِبَةٌ. قَوْلُهُ : (وَكُلُّهَا) أَي : القَضَايَا الْأَرْبَعُ المَعْلُومَةُ مِمَّا تَقَدَّمَ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (مُوجَبَةٌ وَسَالِبَهُ) : - يَصِحُ قِرَاءَةُ مُوجَبَةٌ) بِفَتْحِ الجِيمِ كَمَا هُوَ الشَّائِعُ ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَالأَصْلُ : «مُوجَبٌ فِيهَا»، فَدَخَلَهُ الحَذْفُ وَالإِيصَالُ ؛ أَعْني : حَذْفَ الجَارُ وَإِيصَالَ الضَّمِيرِ . - وَيَصِحُ قِرَاءَتُهَا بِكَسْرِهَا عَلَى الإِسْنَادِ المَجَازِيِّ، وَهَذَا هُوَ المُنَاسِبُ لِلمُقَابَلَةِ بِـ «السَّالِبَةِ». قَوْلُهُ : (فَهْيَ إِذا إِلَى الثَّمَانِ آبِبَهُ أَيْ: فَهِيَ إِذَا كَانَتْ مُنْقَسِمَةً إِلَى المُوجَبَةِ وَالسَّالِبَةِ رَاجِعَةٌ إلَى الثَّمَانِ، بِحَذْفِ اليَاءِ تَخْفِيفاً ، وَجَعَلِ الإِعْرَابِ مُقَدَّراً عَلَيْهَا ، أَوْ ظَاهِراً عَلَى النُّونِ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِ الشَّاعِرِ : [من الرَّجز] لَهَا ثَنَايَا أَرْبَعُ حِسَانٌ وَأَرْبَعُ (۳) فَتَغْرُهَا ثَمَانُ
Text: (55) And all of them: affirmative and negative, so they then return to eight. His saying: (and all of them) i.e., the four known propositions from what preceded. And his saying: (affirmative and negative): It is permissible to read 'mujaba' with a fatha on the jim as is common, and thus the original is 'mujabun fiha' (affirmed in them), so deletion and connection occurred; meaning: deletion of the particle and connection of the pronoun. And it is permissible to read it with a kasra on metaphorical attribution, and this is suitable for the contrast with 'al-saliba'. His saying: (so they then to the eight returning) i.e., they, when divided into affirmative and negative, return to eight, with the ya' deleted for lightness, and the i'rab made implied on it, or apparent on the nun; as in the poet's verse: [rajaz] 'She has four beautiful front teeth and four, so they became eight.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage explains a key expansion of the four categorical propositions. The matn states: 'And all of them: affirmative and negative, so they then return to eight.' The commentator (Bajuri) clarifies: 'all of them' refers to the four known propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) that were previously introduced. Each of these is either affirmative or negative, but the phrase 'affirmative and negative' means that when you consider the quality (affirmative/negative) separately from the quantifier, the four basic propositions multiply into eight. For example, the universal affirmative (A) is affirmative; the universal negative (E) is negative; the particular affirmative (I) is affirmative; the particular negative (O) is negative. But the commentator hints that this classification is not trivial: each quantifier ('kull', 'ba‘d', 'la shay'', 'laysa ba‘d') can be combined with either quality, yielding 2×4=8. The commentary then discusses two valid readings of 'mujaba': with fatha (indicating a deleted preposition) or with kasra (as metaphorical attribution). The latter is more appropriate because it directly contrasts with 'saliba'. Finally, the commentator clarifies the phrase 'so they then to the eight returning' by citing a poetic verse about teeth: just as four plus four make eight, the four propositions when split into affirmative and negative become eight. Thus, the student learns that the standard four propositions are not the final count; by considering quality separately, we obtain eight types.

The Eight Types and the Forty-Eight: How Negation Creates Variety
Classification of propositions into eight and forty-eight types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify propositions as 'adula (modified) or muhassala (unmodified) in subject, predicate, or both, and explain why 16 of the 48 resulting types are duplicates.

Core Idea
Negation can be part of the term or the copula, creating distinct proposition types.

In classical Islamic logic, propositions are not just affirmative or negative. They can be 'modified' by making the negation part of the subject or predicate. This lesson unpacks the eight basic proposition types and shows how they expand to 48 when we consider these modifications. You'll learn to spot the pattern and avoid the common trap of thinking all 48 are distinct.

Guided Path
1
Understand the eight basic types
The eight basic types come from crossing quantity (universal/particular) with quality (affirmative/negative). The author states that propositions 'return to eight'. Start by recalling the four categorical propositions (A, E, I, O) in both affirmative and negative? Actually, the eight here are the four quantified types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) each considered in two forms: 'adula and muhassala? No, the eight are the basic types before modification. The passage says the eight are from dividing into affirmative and negative. It might be: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative – that's four – plus their modified counterparts? Let's read: 'so it, when divided into affirmative and negative, returns to eight.' That implies that for each of the four quantifiers there are two qualities, making eight. So the eight are the standard four with quality distinguished. Then the modifications add six variations per proposition, not per type. Actually, the passage says 'these eight are divided into modified in predicate only, subject only, both, and unmodified of what was mentioned' – that gives six subcategories for each of the eight, hence 48. So step one: list the eight basic propositions: e.g., 'All S is P', 'No S is P', 'Some S is P', 'Some S is not P', and then their analogues with 'adula? No, better to follow the text: the eight are the standard four quantified propositions in both affirmative and negative? The text is a bit ambiguous, but the key is that the eight are the foundational types that then undergo modification. Just accept that the eight are the typical quantified ones.
The eight basic types are not explicitly listed here, but they are assumed from earlier sections: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
2
Define 'adula (modified) and muhassala (unmodified)
'Adula means the negative particle is made part of a term (subject or predicate). Muhassala means it is not made part. The passage defines three types of 'adula: predicate-only, subject-only, and both. Similarly, three types of muhassala: predicate-only, subject-only, and both. Each of the eight basic propositions can appear in any of these six forms, giving 8×6=48.
Muhassala predicate-only is identical to 'adula subject-only, and vice versa, hence 16 duplicates.
3
Identify the six forms with examples
Read the examples for each type and note how the negation attaches. For 'adula predicate-only: 'Every human is not a stone' (the 'not' is part of the predicate 'not a stone'). For 'adula subject-only: 'Every not-animal is inanimate'. For 'adula both: 'Every not-animal is not human'. For muhassala: the negation is not part of the term; e.g., muhassala both: 'Every human is animal' (no negation). The other muhassala types mirror the 'adula ones due to duplication.
The duplication is explicitly explained: muhassala predicate-only = 'adula subject-only.
4
Calculate the unique types
Since 16 of the 48 are duplicates, the actual number of distinct types is 48 - 16 = 32. But note that the duplicates are not merely repeated; they are logically equivalent forms. This teaches us that classification must avoid counting the same logical form twice under different names.
The author warns: 'let it be considered' – a reminder to avoid error.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فهي إذا إلى الثمان آببة) أي: فهي إذا كانت منقسمة إلى الموجبة والسالبة راجعة إلى الثمان، بحذف الياء تخفيفاً، وجعل الإعراب مقدراً عليها، أو ظاهراً على النون؛ كما في قول الشاعر : [من الرجز] لها ثنايا أربع حسان وأربع فثغرها ثمان . واعلم أنه إذا اعتبر أن هذه الثمانية تنقسم إلى : معدولة المحمول فقط، أو الموضوع كذلك، أو هما، وإلى محصلة ما ذكر؛ كان المجموع ثمانية وأربعين، قائمة من ضرب ثمانية في ستة. - ومعدولة المحمول فقط هي : ما جعلت أداة النفي جزءاً من محمولها دون موضوعها؛ مثالها موجبة نحو قولك : «كل إنسان هو لا حجر »، وسالبة نحو قولك : زيد ليس هو لا عالم»، وسميت بذلك؛ لأنه عدل فيها بأداة النفي عن أصل وضعها، وهو رفع النسبة، فهو على الحذف والإيصال، وهكذا يقال فيما بعد. - ومعدولة الموضوع فقط هي : ما جعلت أداة النفي جزءاً من موضوعها دون محمولها»؛ مثالها موجبة نحو قولك : كل لا حيوان جماد» ، وسالبة نحو قولك : لا شيء من لا حيوان بإنسان». - ومعدولتهما هي : ما جعلت أداة النفي جزءاً منهما ؛ مثالها موجبة نحو قولك : كل لا حيوان » هو لا إنسان»، وسالبة نحو قولك : لا شيء من لا حيوان لا بجماد». - ومحصلة المحمول فقط هي : ما لم تجعل أداة النفي جزءاً من محمولها، مع جعلها جزءاً من موضوعها، فهي عين معدولة الموضوع فقط ، وسميت بذلك؛ لأنه جعل المحمول فيها محصلاً - أي: ثبوتياً - لا سلبياً، فهو على الحذف والإيصال، وهكذا يقال فيما بعد. - ومحصلة الموضوع فقط هي : ما لم تجعل أداة النفي جزءاً من موضوعها، مع جعلها جزءاً من محمولها، فهي عين معدولة المحمول فقط . - ومحصلتهما هي : ما لم تجعل أداة النفي جزءاً منهما »؛ مثالها موجبة نحو : كل إنسان حيوان، وسالبة نحو : «لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر» . وبهذا يعلم أن بعض الثمانية والأربعين المذكورة مكرر ، وهو ستة عشر؛ لأن محصلة المحمول فقط هي عين معدولة الموضوع فقط ومحصلة الموضوع فقط هي عين معدولة المحمول فقط، فليتأمل .
His statement: (So it, when, to the eight returning) meaning: So it, when divided into affirmative and negative, returns to eight, with the ya omitted for lightness, and the inflection made estimated on it, or apparent on the nun; as in the poet's verse: [from rajaz] 'She has four beautiful incisors and four, so her mouth is eight.' And know that if it is considered that these eight are divided into: modified in the predicate only, or the subject likewise, or both, and into unmodified of what was mentioned; the total becomes forty-eight, resulting from multiplying eight by six. - And modified in the predicate only is: that in which the negative particle is made part of its predicate without its subject; its example affirmative is your saying: 'Every human is not a stone', and negative is your saying: 'Zayd is not not knowledgeable', and it was named thus because the negative particle was diverted from its original placement, which is negating the relation, so it is on deletion and connection, and likewise is said subsequently. - And modified in the subject only is: that in which the negative particle is made part of its subject without its predicate; its example affirmative is your saying: 'Every not-animal is inanimate', and negative is your saying: 'Nothing of not-animal is human'. - And modified in both is: that in which the negative particle is made part of them; its example affirmative is your saying: 'Every not-animal is not human', and negative is your saying: 'Nothing of not-animal is not inanimate'. - And unmodified in the predicate only is: that in which the negative particle is not made part of its predicate, while making it part of its subject, so it is identical to modified in the subject only, and it was named thus because the predicate is made unmodified - i.e., positive - not negative, so it is on deletion and connection, and likewise is said subsequently. - And unmodified in the subject only is: that in which the negative particle is not made part of its subject, while making it part of its predicate, so it is identical to modified in the predicate only. - And unmodified in both is: that in which the negative particle is not made part of them; its example affirmative is: 'Every human is an animal', and negative is: 'Nothing of human is a stone'. And by this it is known that some of the mentioned forty-eight are repeated, and they are sixteen; because unmodified in the predicate only is identical to modified in the subject only, and unmodified in the subject only is identical to modified in the predicate only, so let it be considered.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory passage from Bajuri's commentary on al-Sullam. It begins by explaining the matn line that when propositions are divided into affirmative and negative, they go back to eight types. The commentator clarifies that this 'return' means the eight types resulting from considering the four quantified propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) in both their affirmative and negative forms? Actually the eight types here refer to the eight categories formed by cross-classifying propositions by quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) plus the modified/unmodified distinction? The text then states that if we consider that these eight are further divided into those modified in the predicate only, modified in the subject only, modified in both, and the unmodified counterparts, the total becomes 48 (8 × 6). The six come from the six modifications: three types of 'adula (predicate-only, subject-only, both) and three types of muhassala (same three). The commentator then defines each type with examples. He explains that 'adula means the negative particle is made part of the term (subject or predicate), while muhassala means it is not made part, so the term is positive. Importantly, he notes that some of the 48 are repetitive: muhassala in the predicate only is the same as 'adula in the subject only, and vice versa, so 16 are duplicates. This guards against the misunderstanding that the 48 are all distinct; in reality, only 32 are unique. The lesson uses examples from logic (e.g., 'Every human is not a stone') to illustrate the concepts.

The Three Parts of a Categorical Proposition and the Three Types of Subject
Author identifies the subject, predicate, and relation as parts of a proposition, and enumerates three types of subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three parts of a categorical proposition (subject, predicate, relation) and distinguish the three types of logical subject in Arabic (mubtada', fa'il, na'ib fa'il) from grammatical positions.

Core Idea
The logical subject is what is being described, not necessarily the first word in the sentence.

This lesson explores the anatomy of a categorical proposition, focusing on its parts and how the subject is understood in logic vs. grammar. We will see why the subject is always conceptually first, even if it appears later in speech. The passage introduces the three types of logical subject from Arabic grammar and clarifies that the subject and predicate refer to meanings, not just words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts of a categorical proposition
From the passage, the three parts are the subject (mawḍū'), the predicate (maḥmūl), and the relation (al-nisba) which is the copula. The commentator notes that in verbal propositions, if the copula is not expressed, only two parts appear.
The copula is the word that links subject and predicate, e.g., 'is' or 'huwa' in Arabic.
2
Define the logical subject
The subject is 'that which is judged upon' (al-mahkum 'alayh). It is the described (al-mawṣūf). The predicate is the description (al-ṣifa). Thus, the subject is conceptually prior.
This conceptual priority holds even if the subject word appears later in the sentence.
3
Enumerate the three types of logical subject
According to the passage, the logical subject is confined to three grammatical items: mubtada' (topic of nominal sentence), fa'il (agent of verbal sentence), and na'ib fa'il (deputy agent, i.e., subject of passive verb).
These are the only elements that can serve as the subject of a judgment in Arabic.
4
Understand why they are called 'first' in rank
The passage explains that even though mubtada' may be postponed and fa'il/na'ib fa'il come after the verb, they are first in rank and intellection because the described (subject) is prior to the description (predicate).
Rank here means conceptual order, not positional order in the sentence.
5
Distinguish the logical proposition from the verbal proposition
The commentator clarifies that 'subject' and 'predicate' here refer to meanings (the concepts), not the words. The verbal proposition may have only two parts if the copula is omitted, or three if the copula is stated. The logical proposition always has three parts in meaning.
This distinction prevents confusion between grammatical structure and logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والأول الموضوع بالحملية والآخر المحمول بالسوية) تكلم المصنف على جزعين من أجزاء القضية، وهما: الموضوع، والمحمول: (1) - والأول هو: المحكوم عليه»، وينحصر في ثلاثة وهي: المبتدأ، والفاعل، ونائبه. وإنما كانت هذه الثلاثة أولاً، مع أن بعضها وهو المبتدأ قد يذكر جوازاً أو وجوباً آخراً، والبعض الآخر وهو الفاعل أو نائب الفاعل يلتزم ذكره آخراً؛ لأن المراد الأول في الرتبة وفي التعقل؛ لأن الموضوع هو الموصوف في المعنى، والمحمول وصف له، ورتبة الموصوف التقدم في التعقل، ورتبة الوصف التأخر فيه. والمراد بـ «الموضوع، والمحمول» هنا: مدلول اللفظين؛ بقرينة جعل النسبتين جزءاً منها، وهما من المعاني، فيلزم أن الجزءين الأخيرين كذلك، فيكون المراد من القضية»: مدلولها، وأما القضية اللفظية فأجزاء ثلاثة إن صرح بلفظ الرابطة، وإلا فاثنان.
His statement: (And the first is the subject in the categorical, and the other is the predicate equally) The author spoke about two parts of the proposition: the subject and the predicate: (1) - And the first is: that which is judged upon, and it is confined to three: the mubtada', the fa'il, and its deputy (na'ib fa'il). And these three are called 'first' despite that some of them, the mubtada', may be mentioned optionally or obligatorily last, and the others, the fa'il or its deputy, are necessarily mentioned last; because what is meant is 'first' in rank and in intellection; because the subject is the described in meaning, and the predicate is a description of it, and the rank of the described is priority in intellection, and the rank of the description is posteriority in it. And what is meant by 'subject' and 'predicate' here is: the meaning of the two words; by the evidence of making the two relations part of them, and they are from meanings, so it follows that the other two parts are likewise, so what is meant by 'proposition' is: its meaning. As for the verbal proposition, its parts are three if the copula is expressed, otherwise two.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a definitional remark on the components of a categorical proposition. It begins by quoting the matn: 'And the first is the subject in the categorical, and the other is the predicate equally.' The commentator explains that the author (of the matn) is discussing two parts of the proposition: subject and predicate. He then specifies that the subject is 'that which is judged upon' (al-mahkum 'alayh), and it is limited to three types in Arabic grammar: mubtada' (the topic of a nominal sentence), fa'il (the agent of a verbal sentence), and na'ib fa'il (the deputy agent when the verb is passive). The commentator notes that although in actual speech the mubtada' sometimes comes last (like in 'Zaid is standing' where 'Zaid' is mubtada' at the beginning, but can be postponed), and the fa'il/na'ib fa'il always come after the verb, they are still considered 'first' in terms of rank and intellection because the subject is the thing being described (al-mawṣūf) and the predicate is the description (al-ṣifa). The described is conceptually prior to the description. The commentator then clarifies that the terms 'subject' and 'predicate' here refer to the meanings (the concepts themselves), not the words, because the two relations (subject-predicate relation) are meanings. If we consider the verbal proposition (the actual uttered sentence), it will have three parts if the copula (e.g., 'is' or 'huwa') is expressed; otherwise only two (subject and predicate). This guards against confusing the syntactic subject with the logical subject. The lesson emphasizes that the logical subject is what the judgment is about, regardless of its position in the sentence.

Key Takeaway

Three Types of Muhassala Propositions
Bajuri defines the three types of muhassala propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the three types of muhassala propositions and distinguish them from ma'dula propositions.

Core Idea
Muhassala propositions are defined by where the negation particle does NOT go.

In this lesson, we examine the concept of muhassala (positive) propositions. The passage from al-Bajuri defines three specific types based on whether the negation particle is excluded from the predicate, the subject, or both. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly classifying categorical propositions in logic and for identifying repetitions in the larger system of 48 proposition types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term 'muhassala' and its three sub-types in the Arabic. The passage defines 'muhassala of the predicate only', 'muhassala of the subject only', and 'muhassala of both'.
The Arabic phrases are: مُحَصَّلَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ فَقَطْ, مُحَصَّلَةُ الْمَوْضُوعِ فَقَطْ, مُحَصَّلَتُهُمَا.
2
Parse the definition structure
Each type is defined by where the negation tool (أداة النفي) is NOT made part of the predicate or subject. For example, in 'muhassala of the predicate only', negation is part of the subject but not the predicate.
The definition uses 'ma lam tuj'al' (that which is not made) to indicate absence.
3
Relate to ma'dula
Notice that the author explicitly identifies two types as identical to ma'dula types. This is a key insight: muhassala of predicate only = ma'dula of subject only, and vice versa.
This identity is the basis for the later point about repetitions in the 48 types.
4
Find the examples
The passage gives an affirmative example (كل إنسان حيوان) and a negative example (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر) for muhassala of both. Verify that neither contains a negation particle within the subject or predicate.
The negative example uses 'لا شيء' which is the negation of the quantifier, not part of the predicate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمُحَصَّلَةُ الْمَحْمُولِ فَقَطْ هِيَ : مَا لَمْ تُجْعَلْ أَدَاةُ النَّفْيِ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَحْمُولِهَا، مَعَ جَعْلِهَا جُزْءاً مِنْ مَوْضُوعِهَا، فَهِيَ عَيْنُ مَعْدُولَةِ الْمَوْضُوعِ فَقَطْ، وَسُمِّيَتْ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ جُعِلَ الْمَحْمُولُ فِيهَا مُحَصَّلاً - أَيْ : ثُبُوتِيّاً - لَا سَلْبِيّاً، فَهُوَ عَلَى الْحَذْفِ وَالْإِيصَالِ، وَهَكَذَا يُقَالُ فِيمَا بَعْدُ. - وَمُحَصَّلَةُ الْمَوْضُوعِ فَقَطْ هِيَ : مَا لَمْ تُجْعَلْ أَدَاةُ النَّفْيِ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَوْضُوعِهَا، مَعَ جَعْلِهَا جُزْءاً مِنْ مَحْمُولِهَا، فَهِيَ عَيْنُ مَعْدُولَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ فَقَطْ. - وَمُحَصَّلَتُهُمَا هِيَ : مَا لَمْ تُجْعَلْ أَدَاةُ النَّفْيِ جُزْءاً مِنْهُمَا »؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ نَحْوُ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ، وَسَالِبَةٌ نَحْوُ : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ». وَبِهَذَا يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ بَعْضَ الثَّمَانِيَةِ وَالْأَرْبَعِينَ الْمَذْكُورَةِ مُكَرَّرٌ، وَهُوَ سِتَّةَ عَشَرَ؛ لِأَنَّ مُحَصَّلَةَ الْمَحْمُولِ فَقَطْ هِيَ عَيْنُ مَعْدُولَةِ الْمَوْضُوعِ فَقَطْ، وَمُحَصَّلَةَ الْمَوْضُوعِ فَقَطْ هِيَ عَيْنُ مَعْدُولَةِ الْمَحْمُولِ فَقَطْ، فَلْيُتَأَمَّلْ.
The muhassala of the predicate only is: that which the negation tool is not made a part of its predicate, while making it a part of its subject; so it is identical to the ma'dula of the subject only. It is named thus because the predicate in it is made positive (i.e., affirmative) not negative, so it is an ellipsis and connection. And similarly is said in what follows. The muhassala of the subject only is: that which the negation tool is not made a part of its subject, while making it a part of its predicate; so it is identical to the ma'dula of the predicate only. The muhassala of both is: that which the negation tool is not made a part of either of them. Its example as an affirmative is: 'Every human is an animal'; as a negative: 'Nothing of the human is a stone.' By this it is known that some of the mentioned forty-eight types are repeated, and they are sixteen; because the muhassala of the predicate only is identical to the ma'dula of the subject only, and the muhassala of the subject only is identical to the ma'dula of the predicate only. So let it be contemplated.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the concept of 'muhassala' (positive) propositions, which are part of the classification of categorical propositions. The author first defines three sub-types: (1) 'muhassala of the predicate only', where the negation particle is attached to the subject but not the predicate; (2) 'muhassala of the subject only', where the negation particle is attached to the predicate but not the subject; and (3) 'muhassala of both', where the negation particle is not attached to either. These are contrasted with 'ma'dula' (diverted) propositions. The author gives examples: an affirmative muhassala like 'Every human is an animal', and a negative muhassala like 'No human is a stone'. Then he points out that of the 48 types of propositions (the total of categorical, conditional, etc., multiplied by quantity, quality, etc.), 16 are actually repetitions because the muhassala of the predicate only is identical to the ma'dula of the subject only, and vice versa. This insight reduces the total distinct types from 48 to 32.

مَعْدُولَة
مَوْضُوع
مَحْمُول
أَدَاة النَّفْي

Key Takeaway
Muhassala propositions are defined by the absence of the negation particle in specific parts, creating an identity with certain ma'dula types and leading to repetitions in the total count of proposition types.

The 48 Types and the 16 Repetitions
Al-Bajuri notes the repetition in the 48 types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the number of distinct proposition types in the classification of categorical and conditional propositions, accounting for the repetitions identified by al-Bajuri.

Core Idea
Of the famous 48 proposition types, 16 are duplications because of the overlap between muhassala and ma'dula.

Al-Bajuri's passage not only defines muhassala but also uses these definitions to demonstrate that the widely cited 48 types of propositions contain 16 repetitions. This is a practical application of the definitional distinctions. Understanding this repetition is crucial for working with later logical taxonomies.

Guided Path
1
Recall the 48 types
In post-Razi logic, propositions are classified by quality, quantity, modality, etc. The total comes to 48 when all combinations are considered. Familiarize yourself with the framework if needed.
The 48 types typically include 8 categorical (4 quantity × 2 quality) times 3 muhassala/ma'dula types? Actually, the exact breakdown is not given, but the number is standard.
2
Identify the identities
From the first lesson, recall that muhassala of predicate only = ma'dula of subject only, and muhassala of subject only = ma'dula of predicate only.
These identities are explicitly stated in the passage.
3
Calculate the overlaps
Each identity means that two originally distinct types are actually the same. Since there are two such identities, and each identity removes a duplicate from the 48, the total distinct ones are 48 - 16 = 32. The passage says 16 are repeated.
The reason it is 16 rather than a smaller number likely relates to how many sub-types each identity affects (e.g., across different quantities and qualities). The author does not elaborate, but the fact is stated.
4
Apply to actual examples
Show that a proposition like 'Every human is not-a-stone' (ma'dula of subject) is actually the same as a muhassala of predicate type, so they should not be counted twice.
This demonstrates the practical consequence of the identity.

The Four Parts of a Categorical Proposition
Bajuri explains the four parts of a categorical proposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four parts of a categorical proposition and distinguish the internal relation from the external relation.

Core Idea
A proposition is not just subject and predicate; it includes two relations: mental and real.

In this passage, Bajuri builds on the basic definition of a categorical proposition by showing that it has not two but four essential parts. Understanding these parts is crucial for analyzing any statement in Islamic logic, from theological proofs to legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic two parts
Bajuri starts by quoting the author: the first part is the subject, the last is the predicate. Note that the subject is 'that which is judged upon' and the predicate is 'that which is judged by'.
The subject can be mubtada', fa'il, or na'ib. The predicate can be khabar or fi'l.
2
Add the internal relation
The third part is the internal relation (al-nisba al-kalamiyya). This is the affirmation or negation of the predicate to the subject as expressed in the proposition.
It is the mental link, not the actual occurrence.
3
Add the external relation
The fourth part is the external relation (al-nisba al-kharijiyya). This is the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of that link in external reality.
The external relation is what makes the proposition true or false.
4
Verify the total parts
Combine all four: subject, predicate, internal relation, external relation. This is the full account according to the later logicians.
Bajuri notes that some Maghribi scholars limit to three, but the established view is four.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالأَوَّلُ الْمَوضُوعُ بِالْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالآخِرُ الْمَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّةِ) تَكَلَّمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ عَلَى جُزْءَيْنِ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ، وَهُمَا: الْمَوْضُوعُ، وَالْمَحْمُولُ: (1) - وَالأَوَّلُ هُوَ: «الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ»، وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي ثَلَاثَةٍ وَهِيَ: الْمُبْتَدَأُ، وَالْفَاعِلُ، وَنَائِبُهُ (1). (2) - وَالثَّانِي هُوَ: «الْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ» وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي اثْنَيْنِ وَهُمَا: الْخَبَرُ، وَالْفِعْلُ. - وَتَرَكَ جُزْءَيْنِ آخَرَيْنِ، وَهُمَا: (3) - النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: «ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ»؛ أَيْ: تَعَلُّقُهُ وَارْتِبَاطُهُ بِهِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الثُّبُوتِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ، أَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الِانْتِفَاءِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. (4) - وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: وُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ فِي الْأُولَى، أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوعِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ. فَجُمْلَةُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ وَغَيْرِهِ. قَالَ بَعْضُ مُحَقِّقِي الْمَغَارِبَةِ: إِنَّ ذَلِكَ طَرِيقَةُ الْعَجَمِ، وَأَمَّا طَرِيقَةُ الْعَرَبِ فَأَجْزَاؤُهَا: الثَّلَاثَةُ الْأُوَلُ فَقَطْ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يُتَابِعْهُ الْأَشْيَاخُ كَمَا قَالَهُ شَيْخُنَا. وَجَعْلُ الْجُزْءِ الرَّابِعِ مَا ذَكَرَ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ الْمُتَبَادَرُ، وَأَمَّا مَا فِي كَلَامِ بَعْضِهِمْ مِنْ أَنَّهُ الْإِيقَاعُ أَوِ الِانْتِزَاعُ؛ أَيْ: إِدْرَاكُ الْوُقُوعِ أَوْ عَدَمِ الْوُقُوعِ، فَفِيهِ نَظَرُ؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ وَصْفٌ لِلْمُدْرَكِ، فَلَا يَصِحُّ جَعْلُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ. وَبِهَذَا بِعَيْنِهِ اعْتَرَضَ مُلَّا أَحْمَدَ عَلَى الْفَنَّارِيِّ فِي جَعْلِهِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهَا. وَقَدْ وَضَعَ الْمَنَاطِقَةُ لَفْظاً يَدُلُّ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ مُطَابَقَةً، وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً.
His statement: 'And the first is the subject by predication and the last is the predicate by equality.' The author spoke about two parts of the parts of the proposition, which are: the subject and the predicate: (1) The first is 'that which is judged upon', and it is limited to three: the mubtada', the fa'il, and its na'ib. (2) The second is 'that which is judged by', and it is limited to two: the khabar and the fi'l. And he left two other parts, which are: (3) The internal relation, which is 'the affirmation of the predicate for the subject'; i.e., its connection and attachment to it in the mode of affirmation in the affirmative proposition, or in the mode of negation in the negative proposition. (4) The external relation, which is the occurrence of that in the first, or its non-occurrence in the second. So the total of the parts of the proposition is four, as is taken from the Sharh al-Shamsiyya and others. Some verifying scholars of the Maghrib said: That is the method of the non-Arabs; as for the method of the Arabs, its parts are only the first three; however, the masters did not follow him, as our shaykh said. And making the fourth part what was mentioned is the apparent and clear meaning. As for what is in the speech of some of them, that it is al-iqa' or al-intiza', i.e., the perception of occurrence or non-occurrence, there is consideration in it, because that is a description of the perceived, so it is not valid to make it a part of the proposition. And by this very same reasoning, Mulla Ahmad objected to al-Fanari when he made that a part of it. And the logicians have coined a word that indicates the external relation by direct implication and the internal relation by indirect implication.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the parts (ajza') of a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). Bajuri begins by quoting the author of al-Sullam: 'And the first is the subject by predication and the last is the predicate by equality.' He then explains that the author only discussed two parts, but there are actually four. The first part, the subject (mawdu'), is 'that which is judged upon' and is limited to three grammatical categories: the mubtada' (topic/subject), the fa'il (agent), and its na'ib (substitute, i.e., the substitute subject in passive constructions). The second part, the predicate (mahmul), is 'that which is judged by' and is limited to two: the khabar (predicate nominative) and the fi'l (verb). Bajuri then adds two more parts that the author omitted: the internal relation (al-nisba al-kalamiyya) and the external relation (al-nisba al-kharijiyya). The internal relation is the affirmation or negation of the predicate to the subject—it is the mental link as expressed in the proposition. The external relation is the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of that link in reality. Thus, a complete proposition has four parts. Bajuri notes a disagreement: some Maghribi scholars claim that only the first three parts exist according to the Arab method, but the established scholars (ashyakh) did not follow this view. He then critiques an alternative view that the fourth part is 'iqa'' (affirmation/negation) or 'intiza'' (taking away), arguing that these are descriptions of the perception of the relation, not parts of the proposition itself. He cites Mulla Ahmad's objection to al-Fanari on this point. Finally, he notes that logicians have coined a word (presumably 'iqa'' or something similar) that directly indicates the external relation and indirectly indicates the internal relation.

Key Terms
الموضوع
المحمول
النسبة الكلامية
النسبة الخارجية

C) Four: subject, predicate, internal relation, and external relation.
D) Five: adding the copula.

Key Takeaway
A complete categorical proposition requires four parts: subject, predicate, internal relation, and external relation.

The Dispute Over the Fourth Part: Iqa' vs. External Relation
Bajuri critiques the view that the fourth part is al-iqa'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Bajuri rejects making al-iqa' (the act of affirming) a part of the proposition.

Core Idea
The relation in reality is not the same as the act of perceiving it.

Bajuri not only adds the external relation as the fourth part, but also refutes an alternative view that the fourth part is al-iqa' (the act of affirmation/negation) or al-intiza' (the perception of occurrence). This debate sharpens the distinction between the external relation and our mental awareness of it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternative view
Some scholars claim that the fourth part of a proposition is al-iqa' or al-intiza', meaning the act of affirming/negating or the perception of occurrence/non-occurrence.
This view is attributed to al-Fanari and others.
2
Understand Bajuri's objection
Bajuri objects because al-iqa' is a description of the perceiver's act, not a part of the proposition itself. The proposition is the statement, not the mental act.
He says 'that is a description of the perceived, so it is not valid to make it a part of the proposition.'
3
Follow the citation of Mulla Ahmad
Bajuri supports his objection by noting that Mulla Ahmad made the same criticism of al-Fanari.
This shows scholarly consensus on the issue.
4
Distinguish direct and indirect indication
Bajuri concludes that logicians have coined a term that directly indicates the external relation and indirectly indicates the internal relation. The term (likely 'iqa') is thus not a part but a sign.
The external relation is the primary meaning; the internal relation is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (وَالأَوَّلُ الْمَوضُوعُ بِالْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالآخِرُ الْمَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّةِ) تَكَلَّمَ الْمُصَنِّفُ عَلَى جُزْءَيْنِ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ، وَهُمَا: الْمَوْضُوعُ، وَالْمَحْمُولُ: (1) - وَالأَوَّلُ هُوَ: «الْمَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ»، وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي ثَلَاثَةٍ وَهِيَ: الْمُبْتَدَأُ، وَالْفَاعِلُ، وَنَائِبُهُ (1). (2) - وَالثَّانِي هُوَ: «الْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ» وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي اثْنَيْنِ وَهُمَا: الْخَبَرُ، وَالْفِعْلُ. - وَتَرَكَ جُزْءَيْنِ آخَرَيْنِ، وَهُمَا: (3) - النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: «ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ»؛ أَيْ: تَعَلُّقُهُ وَارْتِبَاطُهُ بِهِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الثُّبُوتِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ، أَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الِانْتِفَاءِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. (4) - وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: وُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ فِي الْأُولَى، أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوعِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ. فَجُمْلَةُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ وَغَيْرِهِ. قَالَ بَعْضُ مُحَقِّقِي الْمَغَارِبَةِ: إِنَّ ذَلِكَ طَرِيقَةُ الْعَجَمِ، وَأَمَّا طَرِيقَةُ الْعَرَبِ فَأَجْزَاؤُهَا: الثَّلَاثَةُ الْأُوَلُ فَقَطْ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يُتَابِعْهُ الْأَشْيَاخُ كَمَا قَالَهُ شَيْخُنَا. وَجَعْلُ الْجُزْءِ الرَّابِعِ مَا ذَكَرَ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ الْمُتَبَادَرُ، وَأَمَّا مَا فِي كَلَامِ بَعْضِهِمْ مِنْ أَنَّهُ الْإِيقَاعُ أَوِ الِانْتِزَاعُ؛ أَيْ: إِدْرَاكُ الْوُقُوعِ أَوْ عَدَمِ الْوُقُوعِ، فَفِيهِ نَظَرُ؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ وَصْفٌ لِلْمُدْرَكِ، فَلَا يَصِحُّ جَعْلُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ. وَبِهَذَا بِعَيْنِهِ اعْتَرَضَ مُلَّا أَحْمَدَ عَلَى الْفَنَّارِيِّ فِي جَعْلِهِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهَا. وَقَدْ وَضَعَ الْمَنَاطِقَةُ لَفْظاً يَدُلُّ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ مُطَابَقَةً، وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً.
His statement: 'And the first is the subject by predication and the last is the predicate by equality.' The author spoke about two parts of the parts of the proposition, which are: the subject and the predicate: (1) The first is 'that which is judged upon', and it is limited to three: the mubtada', the fa'il, and its na'ib. (2) The second is 'that which is judged by', and it is limited to two: the khabar and the fi'l. And he left two other parts, which are: (3) The internal relation, which is 'the affirmation of the predicate for the subject'; i.e., its connection and attachment to it in the mode of affirmation in the affirmative proposition, or in the mode of negation in the negative proposition. (4) The external relation, which is the occurrence of that in the first, or its non-occurrence in the second. So the total of the parts of the proposition is four, as is taken from the Sharh al-Shamsiyya and others. Some verifying scholars of the Maghrib said: That is the method of the non-Arabs; as for the method of the Arabs, its parts are only the first three; however, the masters did not follow him, as our shaykh said. And making the fourth part what was mentioned is the apparent and clear meaning. As for what is in the speech of some of them, that it is al-iqa' or al-intiza', i.e., the perception of occurrence or non-occurrence, there is consideration in it, because that is a description of the perceived, so it is not valid to make it a part of the proposition. And by this very same reasoning, Mulla Ahmad objected to al-Fanari when he made that a part of it. And the logicians have coined a word that indicates the external relation by direct implication and the internal relation by indirect implication.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the parts (ajza') of a categorical proposition (qadiyya hamliyya). Bajuri begins by quoting the author of al-Sullam: 'And the first is the subject by predication and the last is the predicate by equality.' He then explains that the author only discussed two parts, but there are actually four. The first part, the subject (mawdu'), is 'that which is judged upon' and is limited to three grammatical categories: the mubtada' (topic/subject), the fa'il (agent), and its na'ib (substitute, i.e., the substitute subject in passive constructions). The second part, the predicate (mahmul), is 'that which is judged by' and is limited to two: the khabar (predicate nominative) and the fi'l (verb). Bajuri then adds two more parts that the author omitted: the internal relation (al-nisba al-kalamiyya) and the external relation (al-nisba al-kharijiyya). The internal relation is the affirmation or negation of the predicate to the subject—it is the mental link as expressed in the proposition. The external relation is the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of that link in reality. Thus, a complete proposition has four parts. Bajuri notes a disagreement: some Maghribi scholars claim that only the first three parts exist according to the Arab method, but the established scholars (ashyakh) did not follow this view. He then critiques an alternative view that the fourth part is 'iqa'' (affirmation/negation) or 'intiza'' (taking away), arguing that these are descriptions of the perception of the relation, not parts of the proposition itself. He cites Mulla Ahmad's objection to al-Fanari on this point. Finally, he notes that logicians have coined a word (presumably 'iqa'' or something similar) that directly indicates the external relation and indirectly indicates the internal relation.

Key Terms
الإيقاع
الانتزاع
النسبة الخارجية
النسبة الكلامية

C) Because al-iqa' is the same as the internal relation.
D) Because al-iqa' only applies to negative propositions.

The external relation is the actual occurrence in reality, not the mental act of affirming it.

The Four Parts of a Categorical Proposition
Author defines the four parts of a proposition and critiques an alternative view of the fourth part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and differentiate the four parts of a categorical proposition in Islamic logic: subject, predicate, conceptual relation, and external relation.

Core Idea
A proposition has four parts: the terms, the mental connection, and the real fact.

In this lesson, we dissect the anatomy of a categorical proposition. The author lists the first two parts as the subject and predicate, then adds the crucial conceptual and external relations. Grasping these four components is essential for understanding how propositions convey truth or falsehood and for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In an Arabic categorical proposition like 'العالم حادث' (The world is temporally originated), the subject is 'العالم' and the predicate is 'حادث'. These are the explicit terms.
2
Recognize the conceptual relation
The conceptual relation is the mental affirmation or negation linking subject and predicate. In an affirmative proposition, it is the judgment that the predicate belongs to the subject; in a negative, it is the judgment of separation.
This relation exists solely in the mind.
3
Distinguish the external relation
The external relation is the actual occurrence or non-occurrence in reality. For a true proposition like 'The world is temporally originated', the external relation is the real temporal origination of the world.
This is the objective truth-conditions.
4
List all four parts
The four parts are: subject, predicate, conceptual relation, and external relation. The copula (رابطة) is not a separate part but a word that indicates the external relation directly and the conceptual relation indirectly.
Some scholars mistakenly treat the copula as a part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها

(٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: الْمَوْضُوعُ بِالْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالآخِرُ الْمَحْمُولُ، بِالسَّوِيَّةِ. (الباجوري) (۲) - وَالثَّانِي هُوَ: الْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ، وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي اثْنَيْنِ وَهُمَا: الْخَبَرُ، وَالْفِعْلُ.

وَتَرَكَ جُزْعَيْنِ آخَرَيْنِ، وَهُمَا: (۳) - النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ؛ أَيْ: تَعَلُّقُهُ وَارْتِبَاطُهُ بِهِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الثُّبُوتِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ، أَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الِانْتِفَاءِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. (٤) - وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: وُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ فِي الْأُولَى، أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوعِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ، فَجُمْلَةُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ وَغَيْرِهِ.

قَالَ بَعْضُ مُحَقِّقِي الْمَغَارِبَةِ: إِنَّ ذَلِكَ طَرِيقَةُ الْعَجَمِ، وَأَمَّا طَرِيقَةُ الْعَرَبِ فَأَجْزَاؤُهَا: الثَّلَاثَةُ الْأُوَلُ فَقَط، لَكِنْ لَمْ يُتَابِعْهُ الْأَشْيَاخُ كَمَا قَالَهُ شَيْخُنَا.

وَجَعْلُ الْجُزْءِ الرَّابِعِ مَا ذَكَرَ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ الْمُتَبَادَرُ، وَأَمَّا مَا فِي كَلَامِ بَعْضِهِمْ مِنْ أَنَّهُ الْإِيقَاعُ أَوِ الِانْتِزَاعُ؛ أَيْ: إِدْرَاكُ الْوُقُوعِ أَوْ عَدَمِ الْوُقُوعِ، فَفِيهِ نَظَرُ؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ وَصْفٌ لِلْمُدْرَكِ، فَلَا يَصِحُّ جَعْلُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ، وَبِهَذَا بِعَيْنِهِ اعْتَرَضَ مُلَّا أَحْمَدَ عَلَى الْفَنْرِي فِي جَعْلِهِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهَا.

وَقَدْ وَضَعَ الْمَنَاطِقَةُ لَفْظاً يَدُلُّ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ مُطَابَقَةً، وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً؛ كَمَا فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ. وَسَمَّوْا ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظَ: رَابِطَةً.

(الأنبابي) قوله: (وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً) أَيْ: لِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْوُقُوعِ أَوِ اللَّا وُقُوعِ الْمُضَافَ كُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا لِلنِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ دُونَ الْعَكْسِ، فَالْجُزْآنِ [أ ٢٠] أَدَّيَا بِعِبَارَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ.

(الشرشيمي) قوله: (مُطَابَقَةٌ) لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ مِنَ الْقَضَايَا إِفَادَةُ مَعَانِيهَا الْخَارِجِيَّةِ، وَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ إِفَادَةِ تِلْكَ النِّسْبَةِ إِفَادَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ، فَلِذَلِكَ كَانَتِ الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَيْهَا التِزَامِيَّةً، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُقُوعِ النِّسْبَةِ أَوْ لَا وُقُوعِهَا تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ الِارْتِبَاطُ؛ أَيْ: يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَحَقُّقِ النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ؛ أَيْ: تَحَقُّقُهَا فِي نَفْسِهَا، لَا فِي الْكَلَامِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ أَنْ يُنْطَقَ بِالْكَلَامِ الدَّالِّ عَلَى الْكَلَامِيَّةِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَحَقُّقِ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ فِي الْكَلَامِ تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ فِي الْخَارِجِ.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. (56) The first part: the subject in a categorical proposition, and the last part: the predicate, equally. The second part: that which is judged, and it reduces to two: the predicate (khabar) and the verb (fi'l). And the teacher left two other parts, which are: (3) the conceptual relation, which is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, i.e., its connection and attachment to it in a positive manner in an affirmative proposition, or in a negative manner in a negative proposition. (4) The external relation, which is the occurrence of that in the first, or its non-occurrence in the second. So the total parts of the proposition are four, as is taken from Sharh al-Shamsiyya and others. Some verifying scholars of the Maghrib said: that is the way of the non-Arabs; as for the way of the Arabs, its parts are only the first three, but the shaykhs did not follow him as our shaykh said. And making the fourth part what was mentioned is the apparent and immediate understanding. As for what is in the words of some, that it is the act of positing or abstracting, i.e., cognizing the occurrence or non-occurrence, there is objection to it because that is a description of the perceived object, so it is not valid to make it part of the proposition. And with this very objection, Mulla Ahmad objected to al-Fanari in his making that part of it. And the logicians coined a word that indicates the external relation by correspondence, and the conceptual relation by implication, as in Sharh al-Shamsiyya, and they called that word the copula (rabitah). (al-Anbabi) His saying: 'and on the conceptual relation by implication' means: because from the occurrence or non-occurrence, each of them belonging to the conceptual relation, the conceptual relation follows without the reverse, so the two parts [A 20] are expressed with a single phrase. (al-Shirshimi) His saying: 'by correspondence' because the purpose of propositions is to convey their external meanings, and from conveying that relation the conveying of the conceptual relation necessarily follows, therefore the denotation of it is by implication, and that is because from the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, the realization of the conceptual relation, which is the connection, necessarily follows; i.e., from the realization of the external relation the realization of the conceptual relation necessarily follows, i.e., its realization in itself, not in speech, because the external does not entail that one utters the word indicating the conceptual, and from the realization of the conceptual relation in speech the realization of the external relation in the external world does not necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on propositions and defines their components. It identifies the first two parts as the subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول) of a categorical proposition, and the second part as the thing judged (محكوم به), which is further divided into two types: the predicate (خبر) in a nominal sentence and the verb (فعل) in a verbal sentence. The author then adds two additional parts omitted by some: the conceptual relation (النسبة الكلامية), which is the affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject, and the external relation (النسبة الخارجية), which is the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of that affirmation in reality. Thus there are four parts in total. A dispute is mentioned: some Maghribi scholars claim that according to Arab method only three parts exist (excluding the external relation), but this view is not widely accepted. The author rejects the idea that the fourth part is the act of positing or abstracting (الإيقاع أو الانتعاع), i.e., the mental cognition of occurrence, because that is a characterization of the perceiver, not a part of the proposition itself. He references Mulla Ahmad's objection to al-Fanari on this point. Finally, the logicians coined the term 'rabitah' (copula) for a word that denotes the external relation directly (مطابقة) and the conceptual relation indirectly (الزاما). The marginal notes by al-Anbabi and al-Shirshimi clarify the logical relationship: the external relation implies the conceptual relation, and the copula word primarily signifies the external but necessarily indicates the conceptual as well. For example, in the proposition 'The world is hadith (temporally originated)', the word 'is' (copula) indicates the actual occurrence of the hadith property in the world (external relation) while also implying the conceptual relation of affirmation.

النسبة الكلامية
النسبة الخارجية

Key Takeaway
A categorical proposition has four parts: the subject term, the predicate term, the conceptual relation (mental judgment), and the external relation (real-world fact).

The Dispute over the Fourth Part: External Relation or Act of Cognition?
Author rejects the view that the fourth part is an act of cognition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the debate about whether the fourth part of a proposition is the external relation or an act of cognition, and articulate the author's critique.

Core Idea
The fourth part is the external relation, not the mental act of perceiving it.

Some logicians argued that the fourth part of a proposition is the act of positing or abstracting (al-iqa' or al-intiza'), i.e., the cognition of occurrence. The author rejects this, because such an act characterizes the knower, not the known. He explicitly sides with the view that the fourth part is the external relation. This debate has implications for how we understand propositions as bearers of truth and falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
The standard view: the fourth part is the external relation (النسبة الخارجية). The alternative view: it is the act of cognizing the occurrence (الإيقاع أو الانتعاع), i.e., the mental act of positing or abstracting.
2
Understand the objection to the cognition view
The author argues that the act of cognition is a description (وصف) of the perceiver (المدرك), not a part of the proposition itself. The proposition is about reality, not about the mind's activity.
Mulla Ahmad raised the same objection against al-Fanari.
3
Test both views with a false proposition
Consider a false proposition like 'The world is eternal'. Its external relation is the non-occurrence of eternity in the world (i.e., the world is not eternal). The conceptual relation is the mental affirmation. The act of cognition view would say the fourth part is the mental act of positing eternity. But that act is the same regardless of truth-value; it doesn't distinguish true from false.
Thus, the cognition view fails to capture the truth-conditional aspect of propositions.
