(٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: الْمَوْضُوعُ بِالْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالآخِرُ الْمَحْمُولُ، بِالسَّوِيَّةِ. (الباجوري) (۲) - وَالثَّانِي هُوَ: الْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ، وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي اثْنَيْنِ وَهُمَا: الْخَبَرُ، وَالْفِعْلُ.

وَتَرَكَ جُزْعَيْنِ آخَرَيْنِ، وَهُمَا: (۳) - النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ؛ أَيْ: تَعَلُّقُهُ وَارْتِبَاطُهُ بِهِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الثُّبُوتِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ، أَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الِانْتِفَاءِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. (٤) - وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: وُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ فِي الْأُولَى، أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوعِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ، فَجُمْلَةُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ وَغَيْرِهِ.

قَالَ بَعْضُ مُحَقِّقِي الْمَغَارِبَةِ: إِنَّ ذَلِكَ طَرِيقَةُ الْعَجَمِ، وَأَمَّا طَرِيقَةُ الْعَرَبِ فَأَجْزَاؤُهَا: الثَّلَاثَةُ الْأُوَلُ فَقَط، لَكِنْ لَمْ يُتَابِعْهُ الْأَشْيَاخُ كَمَا قَالَهُ شَيْخُنَا.

وَجَعْلُ الْجُزْءِ الرَّابِعِ مَا ذَكَرَ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ الْمُتَبَادَرُ، وَأَمَّا مَا فِي كَلَامِ بَعْضِهِمْ مِنْ أَنَّهُ الْإِيقَاعُ أَوِ الِانْتِزَاعُ؛ أَيْ: إِدْرَاكُ الْوُقُوعِ أَوْ عَدَمِ الْوُقُوعِ، فَفِيهِ نَظَرُ؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ وَصْفٌ لِلْمُدْرَكِ، فَلَا يَصِحُّ جَعْلُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ، وَبِهَذَا بِعَيْنِهِ اعْتَرَضَ مُلَّا أَحْمَدَ عَلَى الْفَنْرِي فِي جَعْلِهِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهَا.

وَقَدْ وَضَعَ الْمَنَاطِقَةُ لَفْظاً يَدُلُّ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ مُطَابَقَةً، وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً؛ كَمَا فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ. وَسَمَّوْا ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظَ: رَابِطَةً.

(الأنبابي) قوله: (وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً) أَيْ: لِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْوُقُوعِ أَوِ اللَّا وُقُوعِ الْمُضَافَ كُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا لِلنِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ دُونَ الْعَكْسِ، فَالْجُزْآنِ [أ ٢٠] أَدَّيَا بِعِبَارَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ.

(الشرشيمي) قوله: (مُطَابَقَةٌ) لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ مِنَ الْقَضَايَا إِفَادَةُ مَعَانِيهَا الْخَارِجِيَّةِ، وَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ إِفَادَةِ تِلْكَ النِّسْبَةِ إِفَادَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ، فَلِذَلِكَ كَانَتِ الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَيْهَا التِزَامِيَّةً، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُقُوعِ النِّسْبَةِ أَوْ لَا وُقُوعِهَا تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ الِارْتِبَاطُ؛ أَيْ: يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَحَقُّقِ النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ؛ أَيْ: تَحَقُّقُهَا فِي نَفْسِهَا، لَا فِي الْكَلَامِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ أَنْ يُنْطَقَ بِالْكَلَامِ الدَّالِّ عَلَى الْكَلَامِيَّةِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَحَقُّقِ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ فِي الْكَلَامِ تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ فِي الْخَارِجِ.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. (56) The first part: the subject in a categorical proposition, and the last part: the predicate, equally. The second part: that which is judged, and it reduces to two: the predicate (khabar) and the verb (fi'l). And the teacher left two other parts, which are: (3) the conceptual relation, which is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, i.e., its connection and attachment to it in a positive manner in an affirmative proposition, or in a negative manner in a negative proposition. (4) The external relation, which is the occurrence of that in the first, or its non-occurrence in the second. So the total parts of the proposition are four, as is taken from Sharh al-Shamsiyya and others. Some verifying scholars of the Maghrib said: that is the way of the non-Arabs; as for the way of the Arabs, its parts are only the first three, but the shaykhs did not follow him as our shaykh said. And making the fourth part what was mentioned is the apparent and immediate understanding. As for what is in the words of some, that it is the act of positing or abstracting, i.e., cognizing the occurrence or non-occurrence, there is objection to it because that is a description of the perceived object, so it is not valid to make it part of the proposition. And with this very objection, Mulla Ahmad objected to al-Fanari in his making that part of it. And the logicians coined a word that indicates the external relation by correspondence, and the conceptual relation by implication, as in Sharh al-Shamsiyya, and they called that word the copula (rabitah). (al-Anbabi) His saying: 'and on the conceptual relation by implication' means: because from the occurrence or non-occurrence, each of them belonging to the conceptual relation, the conceptual relation follows without the reverse, so the two parts [A 20] are expressed with a single phrase. (al-Shirshimi) His saying: 'by correspondence' because the purpose of propositions is to convey their external meanings, and from conveying that relation the conveying of the conceptual relation necessarily follows, therefore the denotation of it is by implication, and that is because from the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, the realization of the conceptual relation, which is the connection, necessarily follows; i.e., from the realization of the external relation the realization of the conceptual relation necessarily follows, i.e., its realization in itself, not in speech, because the external does not entail that one utters the word indicating the conceptual, and from the realization of the conceptual relation in speech the realization of the external relation in the external world does not necessarily follow.

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on propositions and defines their components. It identifies the first two parts as the subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول) of a categorical proposition, and the second part as the thing judged (محكوم به), which is further divided into two types: the predicate (خبر) in a nominal sentence and the verb (فعل) in a verbal sentence. The author then adds two additional parts omitted by some: the conceptual relation (النسبة الكلامية), which is the affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject, and the external relation (النسبة الخارجية), which is the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of that affirmation in reality. Thus there are four parts in total. A dispute is mentioned: some Maghribi scholars claim that according to Arab method only three parts exist (excluding the external relation), but this view is not widely accepted. The author rejects the idea that the fourth part is the act of positing or abstracting (الإيقاع أو الانتعاع), i.e., the mental cognition of occurrence, because that is a characterization of the perceiver, not a part of the proposition itself. He references Mulla Ahmad's objection to al-Fanari on this point. Finally, the logicians coined the term 'rabitah' (copula) for a word that denotes the external relation directly (مطابقة) and the conceptual relation indirectly (الزاما). The marginal notes by al-Anbabi and al-Shirshimi clarify the logical relationship: the external relation implies the conceptual relation, and the copula word primarily signifies the external but necessarily indicates the conceptual as well. For example, in the proposition 'The world is hadith (temporally originated)', the word 'is' (copula) indicates the actual occurrence of the hadith property in the world (external relation) while also implying the conceptual relation of affirmation.

The Copula (Rabitah) and Its Dual Denotation
Author explains the logicians' rationale for the copula's dual denotation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the copula (rabitah) directly indicates the external relation and indirectly indicates the conceptual relation.

Core Idea
The copula word directly points to the real fact and indirectly implies the mental judgment.

Logicians coined a special particle called the copula (rabitah) to express the external relation. This word, like 'huwa' in Arabic, indicates the external relation by direct correspondence (mutabaqa) and the conceptual relation by implication (iltizaam). The marginal comments by al-Anbabi and al-Shirshimi clarify that the external relation necessarily entails the conceptual relation, but not vice versa. This lesson explains the logical priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the copula in a proposition
In the proposition 'العالم هو حادث' (The world is temporally originated), the word 'هو' is the copula. In verbal sentences, it may be implied in the verb form.
The copula is often omitted in Arabic but can be explicit.
2
Understand direct denotation (mutabaqa)
The copula directly signifies the external relation, i.e., the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of the predicate for the subject. For a true proposition, it signifies that the predicate really holds for the subject.
3
Understand implied denotation (iltizaam)
From the external relation, the conceptual relation necessarily follows: if the predicate actually holds for the subject, then the mental judgment of affirmation is true. So the copula implies the conceptual relation.
Al-Shirshimi notes that the implication is from external to conceptual, not the reverse.
4
Note the asymmetry
The conceptual relation does not imply the external relation: one can mentally affirm something false. Therefore, the copula's primary denotation is the external relation; the conceptual is secondary and implied.
This asymmetry is crucial for understanding how language relates to reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب في القضايا وأحكامها

(٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: الْمَوْضُوعُ بِالْحَمْلِيَّةِ وَالآخِرُ الْمَحْمُولُ، بِالسَّوِيَّةِ. (الباجوري) (۲) - وَالثَّانِي هُوَ: الْمَحْكُومُ بِهِ، وَيَنْحَصِرُ فِي اثْنَيْنِ وَهُمَا: الْخَبَرُ، وَالْفِعْلُ.

وَتَرَكَ جُزْعَيْنِ آخَرَيْنِ، وَهُمَا: (۳) - النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: ثُبُوتُ الْمَحْمُولِ لِلْمَوْضُوعِ؛ أَيْ: تَعَلُّقُهُ وَارْتِبَاطُهُ بِهِ عَلَى وَجْهِ الثُّبُوتِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ الْمُوجَبَةِ، أَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ الِانْتِفَاءِ فِي الْقَضِيَّةِ السَّالِبَةِ. (٤) - وَالنِّسْبَةُ الْخَارِجِيَّةُ الَّتِي هِيَ: وُقُوعُ ذَلِكَ فِي الْأُولَى، أَوْ عَدَمُ وُقُوعِهِ فِي الثَّانِيَةِ، فَجُمْلَةُ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ أَرْبَعَةٌ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ وَغَيْرِهِ.

قَالَ بَعْضُ مُحَقِّقِي الْمَغَارِبَةِ: إِنَّ ذَلِكَ طَرِيقَةُ الْعَجَمِ، وَأَمَّا طَرِيقَةُ الْعَرَبِ فَأَجْزَاؤُهَا: الثَّلَاثَةُ الْأُوَلُ فَقَط، لَكِنْ لَمْ يُتَابِعْهُ الْأَشْيَاخُ كَمَا قَالَهُ شَيْخُنَا.

وَجَعْلُ الْجُزْءِ الرَّابِعِ مَا ذَكَرَ هُوَ الظَّاهِرُ الْمُتَبَادَرُ، وَأَمَّا مَا فِي كَلَامِ بَعْضِهِمْ مِنْ أَنَّهُ الْإِيقَاعُ أَوِ الِانْتِزَاعُ؛ أَيْ: إِدْرَاكُ الْوُقُوعِ أَوْ عَدَمِ الْوُقُوعِ، فَفِيهِ نَظَرُ؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ وَصْفٌ لِلْمُدْرَكِ، فَلَا يَصِحُّ جَعْلُهُ مِنْ أَجْزَاءِ الْقَضِيَّةِ، وَبِهَذَا بِعَيْنِهِ اعْتَرَضَ مُلَّا أَحْمَدَ عَلَى الْفَنْرِي فِي جَعْلِهِ ذَلِكَ مِنْ أَجْزَائِهَا.

وَقَدْ وَضَعَ الْمَنَاطِقَةُ لَفْظاً يَدُلُّ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ مُطَابَقَةً، وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً؛ كَمَا فِي شَرْحِ الشَّمْسِيَّةِ. وَسَمَّوْا ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظَ: رَابِطَةً.

(الأنبابي) قوله: (وَعَلَى النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الْتِزَاماً) أَيْ: لِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْوُقُوعِ أَوِ اللَّا وُقُوعِ الْمُضَافَ كُلٍّ مِنْهُمَا لِلنِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ النِّسْبَةُ الْكَلَامِيَّةُ دُونَ الْعَكْسِ، فَالْجُزْآنِ [أ ٢٠] أَدَّيَا بِعِبَارَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ.

(الشرشيمي) قوله: (مُطَابَقَةٌ) لِأَنَّ الْمَقْصُودَ مِنَ الْقَضَايَا إِفَادَةُ مَعَانِيهَا الْخَارِجِيَّةِ، وَيَلْزَمُ مِنْ إِفَادَةِ تِلْكَ النِّسْبَةِ إِفَادَةُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ، فَلِذَلِكَ كَانَتِ الدَّلَالَةُ عَلَيْهَا التِزَامِيَّةً، وَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ يَلْزَمُ مِنْ وُقُوعِ النِّسْبَةِ أَوْ لَا وُقُوعِهَا تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ الَّتِي هِيَ الِارْتِبَاطُ؛ أَيْ: يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَحَقُّقِ النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ؛ أَيْ: تَحَقُّقُهَا فِي نَفْسِهَا، لَا فِي الْكَلَامِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَلْزَمُ مِنَ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ أَنْ يُنْطَقَ بِالْكَلَامِ الدَّالِّ عَلَى الْكَلَامِيَّةِ، وَلَا يَلْزَمُ مِنْ تَحَقُّقِ النِّسْبَةِ الْكَلَامِيَّةِ فِي الْكَلَامِ تَحَقُّقُ النِّسْبَةِ الْخَارِجِيَّةِ فِي الْخَارِجِ.
Chapter on Propositions and Their Rulings. (56) The first part: the subject in a categorical proposition, and the last part: the predicate, equally. The second part: that which is judged, and it reduces to two: the predicate (khabar) and the verb (fi'l). And the teacher left two other parts, which are: (3) the conceptual relation, which is the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, i.e., its connection and attachment to it in a positive manner in an affirmative proposition, or in a negative manner in a negative proposition. (4) The external relation, which is the occurrence of that in the first, or its non-occurrence in the second. So the total parts of the proposition are four, as is taken from Sharh al-Shamsiyya and others. Some verifying scholars of the Maghrib said: that is the way of the non-Arabs; as for the way of the Arabs, its parts are only the first three, but the shaykhs did not follow him as our shaykh said. And making the fourth part what was mentioned is the apparent and immediate understanding. As for what is in the words of some, that it is the act of positing or abstracting, i.e., cognizing the occurrence or non-occurrence, there is objection to it because that is a description of the perceived object, so it is not valid to make it part of the proposition. And with this very objection, Mulla Ahmad objected to al-Fanari in his making that part of it. And the logicians coined a word that indicates the external relation by correspondence, and the conceptual relation by implication, as in Sharh al-Shamsiyya, and they called that word the copula (rabitah). (al-Anbabi) His saying: 'and on the conceptual relation by implication' means: because from the occurrence or non-occurrence, each of them belonging to the conceptual relation, the conceptual relation follows without the reverse, so the two parts [A 20] are expressed with a single phrase. (al-Shirshimi) His saying: 'by correspondence' because the purpose of propositions is to convey their external meanings, and from conveying that relation the conveying of the conceptual relation necessarily follows, therefore the denotation of it is by implication, and that is because from the occurrence of the relation or its non-occurrence, the realization of the conceptual relation, which is the connection, necessarily follows; i.e., from the realization of the external relation the realization of the conceptual relation necessarily follows, i.e., its realization in itself, not in speech, because the external does not entail that one utters the word indicating the conceptual, and from the realization of the conceptual relation in speech the realization of the external relation in the external world does not necessarily follow.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on propositions and defines their components. It identifies the first two parts as the subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول) of a categorical proposition, and the second part as the thing judged (محكوم به), which is further divided into two types: the predicate (خبر) in a nominal sentence and the verb (فعل) in a verbal sentence. The author then adds two additional parts omitted by some: the conceptual relation (النسبة الكلامية), which is the affirmation or negation of the predicate for the subject, and the external relation (النسبة الخارجية), which is the actual occurrence or non-occurrence of that affirmation in reality. Thus there are four parts in total. A dispute is mentioned: some Maghribi scholars claim that according to Arab method only three parts exist (excluding the external relation), but this view is not widely accepted. The author rejects the idea that the fourth part is the act of positing or abstracting (الإيقاع أو الانتعاع), i.e., the mental cognition of occurrence, because that is a characterization of the perceiver, not a part of the proposition itself. He references Mulla Ahmad's objection to al-Fanari on this point. Finally, the logicians coined the term 'rabitah' (copula) for a word that denotes the external relation directly (مطابقة) and the conceptual relation indirectly (الزاما). The marginal notes by al-Anbabi and al-Shirshimi clarify the logical relationship: the external relation implies the conceptual relation, and the copula word primarily signifies the external but necessarily indicates the conceptual as well. For example, in the proposition 'The world is hadith (temporally originated)', the word 'is' (copula) indicates the actual occurrence of the hadith property in the world (external relation) while also implying the conceptual relation of affirmation.

Key Terms
رابطة
دلالة مطابقة
دلالة التزام

Key Takeaway
The copula directly signifies the external relation and indirectly implies the conceptual relation, establishing the priority of objective reality over mental judgment.

The Copula in Nominal and Verbal Forms
The author defines the two copula forms and refines the understanding of the nominal copula against grammatical objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the nominal and verbal copula and defend the logicians’ position that 'huwa' functions as a relational copula against grammatical objections.

Core Idea
The copula is the logical connector between subject and predicate; even pronouns like 'huwa' can serve this function.

This lesson examines the two forms of the copula (rabitah) in Arabic logical propositions: the nominal form using 'huwa' and the verbal form using 'kana'. You will learn how the logicians refine the grammatical concept of the 'pronoun of separation' (damir al-fasl) to treat it as a genuine copula indicating the relation between subject and predicate. By understanding this refinement, you will see how logic and grammar differ in their analysis of the same linguistic structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two forms of the copula
In the Arabic text, find the examples of the nominal copula ('huwa') and the verbal copula ('kana'). Note the terms used: 'رابطة غير زمانية' (non-temporal) and 'رابطة زمانية' (temporal).
2
Understand the grammatical objection
Extract the objector's argument that 'huwa' does not indicate the relation; it is either a second subject or a pronoun of separation (damir al-fasl) used for emphasis, not for binding.
3
Analyze the logicians' response
The answer in the text: 'huwa' serves to verify that what follows is the predicate, thus it necessitates a binding relation. Note that logicians insist on a copula in meaning even if not explicit, unlike grammarians.
4
Compare the logician and grammarian perspectives
The grammarians (nahwiyyun) only use damir al-fasl when needed to avoid confusion with adjectives, while logicians (mantiqiyyun) always require a relational link either explicitly or implicitly.

وَهُوَ تَارَةً يَكُونُ فِي قَالَبِ الِاسْمِ كَـ: «هُوَ» فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: «زَيْدُ هُوَ قَائِمٌ». وَتَارَةً يَكُونُ فِي قَالَبِ الْفِعْلِ كَـ: «كَانَ» فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: «زَيْدٌ كَانَ قَائِماً».

(الأنبابي) قوله: (فِي قَالَبِ الِاسْمِ) وَحِينَئِذٍ تُسَمَّى: «رَابِطَةً غَيْرَ زَمَانِيَّةٍ»، بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا كَانَتْ فِي قَالَبِ الْفِعْلِ تُسَمَّى: رَابِطَةً زَمَانِيَّةً كَـ (هُوَ) فِي نَحْوِ قَوْلِكَ: «زَيْدٌ هُوَ قَائِمٌ». اسْتُشْكِلَ: بِأَنَّ لَفْظَةَ «هُوَ» فِي نَحْوِ هَذَا الْمِثَالِ لَا دَلَالَةَ لَهُ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ أَصْلاً، بَلْ هُوَ عَائِدٌ عَلَى «زَيْدٍ» عِبَارَةٌ عَنْهُ مُبْتَدَأٌ ثَانٍ، فَإِنْ كَانَ الْمُرَادُ مَا يُسَمَّى ضَمِيرَ الْفَصْلِ، فَهُوَ لَا يَكُونُ فِي نَحْوِ هَذَا الْمِثَالِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يُذْكَرُ إِلَّا بَيْنَ جُزْأَيِ ابْتِدَاءٍ مَعْرِفَتَيْنِ، أَوْ نَكِرَتَيْنِ كَالْمَعْرِفَتَيْنِ فِي امْتِنَاعِ دُخُولِ «أَلْ»، وَعَلَى تَقْدِيرِ دُخُولِهِ فَلَا دَلَالَةَ لَهُ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ أَصْلاً، وَإِنَّمَا يُفِيدُ الْحَصْرَ أَوِ التَّأْكِيدَ، وَتَحْقِيقُ أَنَّ مَا بَعْدَ خَبَرُ «لَا» نَعْتٌ، وَالَّذِي يُفْهَمُ مِنْهُ الرَّبْطُ إِنَّمَا هُوَ الْحَرَكَةُ الْإِعْرَابِيَّةُ.

وَأُجِيبَ: بِاخْتِيَارِ الثَّانِي، وَمَنْعُ أَنَّهُ لَا دَلَالَةَ لَهُ عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ أَصْلاً بِأَنَّهُ يُحَقِّقُ أَنَّ مَا بَعْدَهُ خَبَرُ «لَا» نَعْتٌ؛ وَهَذَا يَسْتَلْزِمُ رَبْطَ مَا بَعْدَهُ بِالْمَوْضُوعِ وَنِسْبَتَهُ إِلَيْهِ؛ لِإِفَادَتِهِ أَنَّ مَا بَعْدَهُ خَبَرٌ، فَيُفِيدُ أَنَّهُ مُسْنَدٌ إِلَى مَوْضُوعٍ. وَلَا يُقَالُ: إِنَّ ضَمِيرَ الْفَصْلِ لَا يُوجَدُ فِي كُلٍّ مَحَلٍّ كَمَا عَلِمْتَ؛ لِأَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ لِلطَّرَفَيْنِ مِنْ وُجُودِ رَابِطَةٍ فِي الْمَعْنَى عِنْدَ الْمَنَاطِقَةِ، فَالْتَزَمُوهُ فِي كُلِّ مَوْضُوعٍ فِيهِ؛ سَوَاءٌ ذُكِرَ أَمْ لَمْ يُذْكَرْ، بِخِلَافِ أَهْلِ الْعَرَبِيَّةِ، فَيَذْكُرُونَهُ إِذَا كَانَ الْمَحْمُولُ يَلْتَبِسُ بِالتَّابِعِ؛ لِلْفَرْقِ بَيْنَهُمَا، وَإِلَّا فَيَمْنَعُوا ذَلِكَ لَفْظاً، وَلَمْ يَلْتَفِتُوا لِلْمَعْنَى. وَلَا فَرْقَ فِي هَذَا الضَّمِيرِ بَيْنَ أَنْ يَكُونَ لِلْمُتَكَلِّمِ، أَوِ الْمُخَاطَبِ، أَوِ الْغَائِبِ. اهـ (صبان).

قوله: (فِي قَالَبِ الْفِعْلِ) أَيْ: النَّاقِصِ؛ تَقَدَّمَ، أَوْ تَأَخَّرَ، أَوْ تَوَسَّطَ. وَلَا فَرْقَ بَيْنَ «كَانَ» وَغَيْرِهَا إِلَّا مَا يَنْقَلِبُ الْكَلَامُ مَعَهُ إِنْشَاءً؛ نَحْوَ: «عَسَى»، وَنَظَرَ فِي كَوْنِ هَذِهِ الْأَفْعَالِ...

(الشرشيمي) قوله: (فِي قَالَبِ الِاسْمِ) أَيْ: فِي صُورَةِ (الِاسْمِ)، وَلَيْسَ بِاسْمٍ حَقِيقَةً، فَهُوَ نَظِيرُ قَوْلِ النُّحَاةِ: «هُوَ ضَمِيرُ فَصْلٍ»، فَلَمْ يَدُلَّ هَذَا اللَّفْظُ إِلَّا عَلَى النِّسْبَةِ، وَكَذَلِكَ لَفْظُ «كَانَ» لَا يَدُلُّ إِلَّا عَلَيْهَا، فَلَا يَدُلُّ عَلَى زَمَانٍ وَلَكِنْ يَعْمَلُ عَمَلَ النَّاسِخِ؛ إِعْطَاءً لِلْحِكَايَةِ مَا لِلْمَحْكِيِّ.
And it (the rabitah) sometimes occurs in the form of a noun, like 'huwa' in your saying 'Zayd is (huwa) standing', and sometimes in the form of a verb, like 'kana' in your saying 'Zayd was (kana) standing'. (al-Anbabi) His saying: 'in the form of a noun' — then it is called a non-temporal rabitah, unlike when it is in the form of a verb, it is called a temporal rabitah. It was objected: that the word 'huwa' in such an example has no indication of the relation at all, but it is a pronoun referring back to 'Zayd', a second subject. If what is meant is what is called the pronoun of separation (damir al-fasl), then it does not occur in such an example because it is only mentioned between two parts of an initial statement that are both definite or both indefinite, like the two definite in preventing the entry of 'al'. And if it is assumed that it enters, it has no indication of the relation at all; it only indicates restriction or emphasis. And the verification that what follows is the predicate of 'la' is an adjective, and what is understood from it as binding is only the inflectional vowel. And it was answered: by choosing the second (interpretation?) and denying that it has no indication of the relation at all, because it verifies that what follows it is the predicate of 'la' (i.e., an adjective), and this necessitates binding what follows it to the subject and attributing it to it, since it indicates that what follows is a predicate, so it indicates that it is predicated of a subject. And it cannot be said that the pronoun of separation does not occur in every place as you know, because there must be for the two extremes a binding relation in meaning according to the logicians, so they commit to it in every subject matter; whether it is mentioned or not, unlike the grammarians who mention it when the predicate would be confused with the descriptive phrase, to differentiate between them, otherwise they forbid it verbally and do not pay attention to the meaning. And there is no difference in this pronoun between being for the speaker, the addressee, or the absent. (End from Suban). His saying: 'in the form of a verb', i.e., the defective verb; whether it precedes, follows, or is in between. And there is no difference between 'kana' and others except what turns the statement into a performative (insha') like 'asa', and he considered the status of these verbs... (al-Shirshimi) His saying: 'in the form of a noun', i.e., in the form of a noun, and it is not a noun in reality, so it is similar to the grammarians' saying 'huwa is a pronoun of separation', so this word only indicates the relation, and similarly the word 'kana' only indicates it, so it does not indicate time but works as a rector (naskh) giving the narration the status of the narrated.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage details the two forms of the copula (rabitah): the nominal form using 'huwa' and the verbal form using 'kana'. The marginal note by al-Anbabi explains that the nominal copula is called 'non-temporal' and the verbal one 'temporal'. An objection is then raised: the word 'huwa' in examples like 'Zayd huwa qa'im' is not a copula; it is either a second subject (مبتدأ ثان) or a pronoun of separation (ضمير الفصل) that grammarians use for emphasis or restriction, and it does not indicate the relation—the relation is actually indicated by the inflectional vowel (حركة إعرابية). The answer given: the logicians choose the second view (that it is a pronoun of separation) but deny that it lacks relational meaning, because it serves to clarify that what follows is the predicate, thereby binding it to the subject. The logicians insist on a copula in meaning even if not explicitly stated, unlike grammarians who only use it to avoid confusion with adjectives. Al-Shirshimi adds that 'huwa' and 'kana' are not true nouns or verbs but are forms that exclusively signify the relation. For example, in 'Zayd is standing', the 'is' (huwa) ties the predicate 'standing' to the subject 'Zayd', just as 'kana' does in past tense. The logicians' perspective prioritizes the logical function over the grammatical classification.

Key Terms
رابطة غير زمانية
رابطة زمانية
ضمير الفصل

Checkpoint
What is the logicians' view on the word 'huwa' in 'Zayd huwa qa'im'?
A) It is a second subject with no relational function.
B) It is a pronoun of separation that indicates restriction only.
C) It functions as a copula that indicates the relation between subject and predicate.
D) It is a temporal copula like 'kana'.

Key Takeaway
The copula in logical propositions can take nominal or verbal form; even words like 'huwa' that grammarians treat as emphasis are considered by logicians to fulfill the essential relational function.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'العالم مخلوق' (The world is created). Identify whether the copula is explicitly stated and what form it would take if made explicit. Then argue from the logicians' perspective that even without an explicit word, a copula in meaning is present. How would a grammarian differ?

Consider the sentence 'زيد هو القائم' (Zayd is the standing one). Explain how the logicians would defend the presence of a copula in 'huwa' despite the grammarian objection that it is just a pronoun of separation. Use the response from the passage.

Propositions

The Four Modalities of Propositional Relation
Introduces the four modalities of propositional relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish and rank the four modalities (kayfiyyat) according to their generality as presented in the tradition.

Core Idea
Not every propositional relation is equal — its modality tells us whether the predicate holds necessarily, permanently, actually, or merely possibly.

In this lesson, we examine the fundamental concept of kayfiyyah (modality) that qualifies the relation between subject and predicate in a proposition. The passage from al-Bajuri (with al-Anbabi's commentary) introduces four modalities — necessity, permanence, absoluteness, and possibility — and crucially, orders them from most general to most specific. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for correctly interpreting the strength of claims in Islamic theology and legal theory, where the modality of a statement determines the certainty of the judgment.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the concept of modality
Understand that every proposition has a qualitative mode (kayfiyyah) that describes how the predicate relates to the subject. The passage states that this modality is 'in reality' (fi nafs al-amr).
The modality is not about the truth value but about the nature of the relation.
2
Identify the four modalities listed
The text lists: darurah (necessity), dawam (permanence), itlaq (absoluteness), and imkan (possibility). Note that the first is called 'necessity' and also 'obligation' (wujub).
These are the four standard modalities in Aristotelian/Islamic logic.
3
Grasp the hierarchical order explained by the commentator
Al-Anbabi explains that possibility is the most general, then absoluteness (which requires actuality), then permanence (which requires continuity in time), then necessity (which adds inevitability).
Each subsequent modality adds a condition, making it more specific.
4
Apply the hierarchy to Islamic examples
Practice by assigning modalities to familiar statements: 'God is eternal' (necessity), 'The night follows the day' (permanence), 'Zayd is standing' (absoluteness), 'A human is a writer' (possibility).
This skill is crucial for accurately interpreting theological and juridical texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه لا بد لنسبة القضية من كيفية تتكيف بها في نفس الأمر، وهي: إما الضرورة ; أي: الوجوب.
الأنبابي
قوله : (لِنِسْبَةِ القَضِيَّةِ) المراد منها : ما يشمل النِّسبتين الكلامية والخارجيَّة .
قوله : (إِمَّا الضَّرُورَةُ ... إلخ ) اعلم أنَّ أعمها : الإمكان، ثمَّ الإطلاق، ثمَّ الدّوام، ثمَّ الضَّرورة؛ لأنَّ الممكن قد يكون حاصلاً وقد لا يكون، والمطلقُ الَّذي هو حاصل بالفعل قد يكون دائماً وقد لا يكون، والدائم قد يكون ضروريا وقد لا يكون .
And know that the relation (nisbah) of a proposition necessarily has a modality (kayfiyyah) by which it is characterized in reality, and it is: either necessity (darurah), i.e., obligation (wujub). Al-Anbabi: His saying: 'for the relation of the proposition' — what is intended includes both the linguistic relation and the external relation. His saying: 'either necessity... etc.' — know that the most general of them is possibility, then absoluteness, then permanence, then necessity; because what is possible may be actual or not, and what is absolute which is actually realized may be permanent or not, and what is permanent may be necessary or not.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the four modalities that characterize the relation (nisbah) between subject and predicate in a proposition. It begins by stating that every propositional relation in reality has a modality (kayfiyyah). The first modality mentioned is 'necessity' (darurah), also called 'obligation' (wujub). Al-Anbabi then clarifies that the phrase 'relation of the proposition' includes both the linguistic relation (in speech) and the external relation (in the mind or reality). He then explains the hierarchical order of these modalities: the most general is 'possibility' (imkan), then 'absoluteness' (itlaq), then 'permanence' (dawam), then 'necessity' (darurah). The reasoning is: something possible may exist or not; something absolute (actually existing) may be permanent or not; something permanent may be necessary or not. Thus, each subsequent modality is more specific. This hierarchy is crucial for understanding the strength of affirmations in logical propositions, as applied in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and theology (kalam). For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' has a necessity relation (the prohibition is necessary), while 'Every Muslim is charitable' may be absolute (some are charitable, but not always).

Key Terms
كيفية
ضرورة
دوام
إطلاق
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text and commentary, which modality is the most general?
A) Necessity (الضرورة)
B) Permanence (الدوام)
C) Absoluteness (الإطلاق)
D) Possibility (الإمكان)

Key Takeaway
The four modalities form a hierarchy from possibility (most general) to necessity (most specific), each adding a condition to the propositional relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Four Qualities of the Proposition and Modal Terminology
Bajuri defines the four qualities and modal terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four qualities (kayfiyyat) of a proposition and distinguish the terms 'jiha' and 'muwajjaha' as used by Muslim logicians.

Core Idea
Every proposition has an intrinsic quality that determines its modality.

In classical Islamic logic, a proposition is not just a statement of subject and predicate; it also carries an inherent quality (kayfiyya) that characterizes the relation between them. This lesson unpacks the four fundamental qualities — necessity, permanence, possibility, and absoluteness — and the technical terms used to indicate them. Understanding these qualities is essential for working with modal propositions, which appear frequently in kalam and fiqh arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four qualities
From the Arabic passage, extract the four kayfiyyat: ضرورة (necessity), دوام (permanence), إمكان (possibility), إطلاق (absoluteness). Note the brief definition given for each.
The definitions are: necessity = obligatory relation; permanence = holds at all times; possibility = not necessary or permanent; absoluteness = actual occurrence without time specification.
2
Distinguish the qualities conceptually
Recognize the key differences: necessity implies logical inevitability; permanence implies temporal constancy but not necessity; possibility implies contingency; absoluteness implies actuality without modal force. This distinction is crucial for classifying propositions.
3
Understand the term 'jiha'
Bajuri states that the word indicating the quality is called 'jiha' (جهة). This is the technical term for the modal qualifier. A proposition containing such a word is 'muwajjaha' (موجهة).
This terminology is standard in later Islamic logic. Do not confuse 'jiha' with other uses of the word in Arabic.
4
Recognize the classification into fifteen
Bajuri mentions that later scholars classified modal propositions into fifteen types based on these qualities. Although not detailed here, this alerts you to the systematic expansion of modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا بُدَّ لِنِسْبَةِ القَضِيَّةِ مِنْ كَيْفِيَّةٍ تَتَكَيَّفُ بِهَا فِي نَفْسِ الأَمْرِ، وَهِيَ : ١ - إِمَّا الضَّرُورَةُ ; أي : الوُجُوبُ. ٢ - وَإِمَّا الدَّوَامُ . ٣ - وَإِمَّا الإِمْكَانُ . ٤ - وَإِمَّا الإِطْلَاقُ ; أَي : الحُصُولُ بِالفِعْلِ. وَسَمُّوا اللَّفْظَ الدَّالَّ عَلَى تِلْكَ الكَيْفِيَّةِ : جِهَةٌ»، وَتُسَمَّى القَضِيَّةُ إِذَا ذُكِرَ فِيهَا ذَلِكَ اللَّفْظُ : «مُوَجَّهَةٌ»، وَعَدَّدَ المُتَأَخِّرُونَ القَضَايَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الكَيْفِيَّةِ المَذْكُورَةِ إِلَى خَمْسَةَ عَشَرَ :
Know that the relation of the proposition must have a quality by which it is qualified in itself, and it is: 1 – either necessity, meaning obligation; 2 – or permanence; 3 – or possibility; 4 – or absoluteness, meaning actual existence. And they called the word indicating that quality: 'modality' (jiha), and the proposition is called 'modal' (muwajjaha) when that word is mentioned in it. And the later scholars counted the propositions according to the mentioned quality into fifteen:
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the four fundamental qualities that every proposition must possess in itself, independent of its expression. These are: necessity (al-ḍarūra), which is obligatory relation; permanence (al-dawām), meaning the relation holds at all times; possibility (al-imkān), meaning the relation may or may not hold; and absoluteness (al-iṭlāq), meaning the relation actually obtains, without specifying duration. Bajuri then introduces the terminology: the word that indicates this quality is called 'jiha' (modality), and a proposition in which such a word appears is called 'muwajjaha' (modal). He notes that later logicians, on the basis of these qualities, classified modal propositions into fifteen types. The passage is foundational for understanding modal logic, as it establishes the framework for analyzing propositions beyond simple categorical ones.

إمكان
إطلاق
جهة
موجهة

Key Takeaway
Every proposition has one of four intrinsic qualities (necessity, permanence, possibility, absoluteness), and the word that expresses this quality is called 'jiha', making the proposition 'muwajjaha'.

The Absolute Necessary Proposition
Bajuri defines the absolute necessary proposition as the first of the seven necessary modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an absolute necessary proposition and distinguish it from other modal propositions by checking for restrictions of time or description.

Core Idea
Absolute necessary propositions state necessity without temporal or attributive restrictions.

Modal logic in Islamic thought categorizes propositions by their modality. The first of the seven necessary types is the absolute necessary. In this lesson, we will define it, see its affirmative and negative examples, and understand why it is called 'absolute' and 'simple'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
In your own words, restate the definition: the proposition judges the necessity of the relation (between subject and predicate) without any restriction by a description or a time.
The key phrase is 'مع الإطلاق عن التقييد بوصف أو وقت'.
2
Recognize the examples
Look at the affirmative example: 'كل إنسان حيوان بالضرورة' (Every human is an animal by necessity). Note that this necessity holds for all humans, at all times, without any condition. Then look at the negative example: 'لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر بالضرورة' (No human is a stone by necessity).
Both examples show unrestricted necessity.
3
Analyze the naming
The commentator explains: it is called 'necessary' because the quality of the relation is necessity, and 'absolute' because it is free from any qualifier (like a specific time or attribute).
The term 'مطلقة' (absolute) indicates this freedom.
4
Note the simplicity
The passage states that this type is 'simple' (بسيطة), meaning its modality is not composite. This will be clearer when the other six types are studied.
Simplicity contrasts with composite modal propositions that combine conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضَّرُورِيَّاتُ السَّبْعُ، وَهِيَ : - (١) - الضَّرُورِيَّةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ مَعَ الإِطْلَاقِ عَنِ التَّقْبِيدِ بِوَصْف أَوْ وَقْتٍ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانُ بِالضَّرُورَةِ، وَسَالِبَةٌ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ بِالضَّرُورَةِ وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : ضَرُورِيَّةٌ، لأَنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ نِسْبَتِهَا الضَّرُورَةُ، وَ : مُطْلَقَةٌ؛ لِإِطْلَاقِهَا عَنِ التَّقْبِيدِ : بِوَصْفٍ أَوْ وَقْتٍ، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي.
The seven necessary propositions, and they are: (1) the absolute necessary, and it is that in which the necessity of the relation is judged together with absoluteness from restriction by a description or a time. Its example, affirmative: 'Every human is an animal by necessity', and negative: 'No human is a stone by necessity'. It is called 'necessary' because the quality of its relation is necessity, and 'absolute' due to its absoluteness from restriction by a description or a time, and it is simple, as will be known from what follows.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the first of the seven necessary modal propositions, called 'al-ḍarūriyya al-muṭlaqa' (the absolute necessary). It is a proposition that judges the relation between subject and predicate as necessary, but without any additional restriction by a specific attribute of the subject or a specific time. Two examples are given: the affirmative 'Every human is an animal by necessity' – meaning that being an animal is an essential necessity for any human, not conditioned on any particular time or description; and the negative 'No human is a stone by necessity' – meaning that the impossibility of being stone is also unrestricted. The commentator explains the naming: it is called 'necessary' because its quality is necessity, and 'absolute' because it is free from any qualifier (like 'as long as it exists' or 'at a certain time'). He also notes that this type is 'simple' (basīṭa), indicating that its modality is not composite, which will be clarified later when the other six are introduced.

Key Terms
ضرورية مطلقة
الضروريات السبع
إطلاق
بسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is unconditional in existence
B) Because it is free from restriction by description or time

D) Because it is necessary

Key Takeaway
The absolute necessary proposition affirms or denies necessity without any temporal or descriptive restriction.

The General Conditional Proposition: Definition and Examples
Author defines the general conditional proposition with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general conditional proposition and construct valid positive and negative examples of it.

Core Idea
The general conditional judges necessity only while the subject's description lasts.

In this passage, the author defines a key type of modal proposition: al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma. Unlike absolute necessity, this proposition only claims necessity as long as the subject retains a certain attribute. We will unpack the definition, examine the provided examples, and learn to apply this concept to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the sentence that explicitly defines the general conditional. It begins with 'wa al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma hiya ...'
The definition has three parts: judgment, necessity of relation, and condition of perpetual description.
2
Identify the three key components
Break down the definition: (1) judgment (ḥukm) with necessity (ḍarūrat al-nisba), (2) on the condition (bi-sharṭ) of the perpetuity (dawām) of the description (waṣf) of the subject (al-mawḍūʿ).
The necessity is conditional, not absolute.
3
Analyse the positive example
The example is 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing.' Notice that the subject 'writer' has the description 'writing', and the predicate 'moving fingers' is necessary only while that description holds.
This illustrates that the truth of the proposition depends on the duration of the description.
4
Analyse the negative example
The negative example: 'No writer is still in his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing.' It negates the predicate 'still fingers' for the same duration.
Both examples show that the proposition's scope is limited to the time the description exists.
5
Summarise the conditions
The general conditional does not require the description to be permanent; it only requires that whenever the description holds, the judgment is necessary.
This distinguishes it from absolute necessity and from the specific conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمَشْرُوطَةُ العَامَّةُ وَهِيَ : الَّتي حُكمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ بِشَرْطِ دَوَامِ وَصْفِ المَوْضُوعِ»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : مَشْرُوطَةٌ»؛ لِمَا فِيهَا مِن اشْتِرَاطِ دَوَامِ وَصْفِ المَوْضُوعِ، وَ: «عَامَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ المَشْرُوطَةِ الخَاصَّةِ ، فَإِنَّهَا لَمْ تُقَيَّدْ بِمَا يَنْفِي احْتِمَالَ دَوَامِ الوَصْفِ،
And the general conditional: it is the one in which judgment is made with necessity of the relation on condition of the perpetuity of the description of the subject. Its positive example: Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing. And negative: No writer is still in his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing. It is called 'conditional' because of the condition of the perpetuity of the description of the subject, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional, for it is not restricted by what negates the possibility of the perpetuity of the description.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the general conditional proposition (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma) as a type of modal proposition. The author begins by stating the definition: it is a proposition in which the judgment (affirmation or negation) is made with necessity (bi-al-ḍarūra) on the condition that the subject's description (waṣf al-mawḍūʿ) persists. For example, 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing.' Here, the subject 'writer' is described by 'writing', and the predicate 'moving his fingers' is necessary only while that description lasts. The negative example similarly negates 'still in his fingers' for the same duration. The author then explains the naming: it is called 'conditional' (mashrūṭa) because it conditions the perpetuity of the description, and 'general' (ʿāmma) because it is more general than the specific conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) since it does not include a restriction that negates the possibility of the description's perpetuity (i.e., it leaves open whether the description persists permanently). This contrasts with the specific conditional, which adds the phrase 'not always' (lā dāʾiman), thereby explicitly denying permanence.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة
ضرورة النسبة
دوام وصف الموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Every man is an animal necessarily as long as he is a man.

Key Takeaway
The general conditional proposition asserts necessity only during the existence of the subject's description, not permanently.

General vs Specific Conditional: The Scope of Generality
Author explains why the proposition is called 'general' and contrasts with specific conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the general conditional from the specific conditional and explain why the former is more general.

Core Idea
The general conditional is broader because it does not negate the possibility of permanent description.

The author explains that the general conditional is called 'general' (ʿāmma) because it is more general than the specific conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa). The specific conditional includes an additional restriction that explicitly denies the permanence of the description. In this lesson, we will compare the two and see how this distinction impacts logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the naming explanation
The author states it is called 'conditional' due to the condition of perpetuity of the description, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional.
The word 'general' here refers to logical generality (aʿamm), meaning it has a wider application.
2
Identify the difference
The specific conditional includes a phrase like 'not always' (lā dāʾiman) that negates the possibility of the description being permanent. The general conditional does not have this restriction.
Thus, the general conditional leaves open whether the description lasts forever; the specific conditional denies it.
3
Classify example propositions
Given a proposition, determine if it is general or specific conditional. For instance, 'Every physician is a healer necessarily as long as he is a physician' is general because it does not say 'not always'. If we add 'but not always', it becomes specific.
Practice with other Islamic examples to internalise the distinction.
4
Understand the logical implications
The generality of the general conditional makes it more useful in syllogistic contexts where permanence is irrelevant. The specific conditional is used when we need to explicitly deny permanence.
This distinction is crucial for correctly applying conversion and syllogistic rules in logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمَشْرُوطَةُ العَامَّةُ وَهِيَ : الَّتي حُكمَ فِيهَا بِضَرُورَةِ النِّسْبَةِ بِشَرْطِ دَوَامِ وَصْفِ المَوْضُوعِ»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءٌ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ بِالضَّرُورَةِ مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : مَشْرُوطَةٌ»؛ لِمَا فِيهَا مِن اشْتِرَاطِ دَوَامِ وَصْفِ المَوْضُوعِ، وَ: «عَامَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ المَشْرُوطَةِ الخَاصَّةِ ، فَإِنَّهَا لَمْ تُقَيَّدْ بِمَا يَنْفِي احْتِمَالَ دَوَامِ الوَصْفِ،
And the general conditional: it is the one in which judgment is made with necessity of the relation on condition of the perpetuity of the description of the subject. Its positive example: Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing. And negative: No writer is still in his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing. It is called 'conditional' because of the condition of the perpetuity of the description of the subject, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific conditional, for it is not restricted by what negates the possibility of the perpetuity of the description.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the general conditional proposition (al-mashrūṭa al-ʿāmma) as a type of modal proposition. The author begins by stating the definition: it is a proposition in which the judgment (affirmation or negation) is made with necessity (bi-al-ḍarūra) on the condition that the subject's description (waṣf al-mawḍūʿ) persists. For example, 'Every writer is moving his fingers necessarily as long as he is writing.' Here, the subject 'writer' is described by 'writing', and the predicate 'moving his fingers' is necessary only while that description lasts. The negative example similarly negates 'still in his fingers' for the same duration. The author then explains the naming: it is called 'conditional' (mashrūṭa) because it conditions the perpetuity of the description, and 'general' (ʿāmma) because it is more general than the specific conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) since it does not include a restriction that negates the possibility of the description's perpetuity (i.e., it leaves open whether the description persists permanently). This contrasts with the specific conditional, which adds the phrase 'not always' (lā dāʾiman), thereby explicitly denying permanence.

مشروطة خاصة
